Speaking of “The Man from Snowy River,”
The Melbourne Argos says:—“There is hope for the
Australian Poet when a volume of verse goes into its
third edition within six weeks of its first publication, and
great encouragement is to be extracted from the fact
that the success of the book is entirely justified by the
quality of its contents.”
Mr. J. Brunton Stephens says:—“The more frequently
they are re-read the better they seem to be. I cannot
agree with the critics who say that they arc the best
bush ballads since Gordon. I think they are quite on a
level with Gordon’s, though, of course, each of the two
writers has his own special merits. . . . The best
book published in Australia for very many years.”

The N.S.W. Govt. Printer issues the third
volume of that province’s “historical
records.” Alex. Britton’s preface to \ol 1.
> reprinted, and F. M. Bladen, who edits
the volume, supplies notes, introduction, and
index. His work cannot he judged without
independent survey of his materials : it
appears to be well done. The introduction,
which summarises the contents, is clear and
succinct. The book is well bound and
printed; though figures might be substituted
for the defacing signs employed as guides to
footnotes.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Govt, is now issuing concur-
rently its historical records and a history based
on the records. The history, as a State task,
- smacks of the superfluous. If the State lays
the foundation it may well be left to learned
enterprise to erect the superstructure.
* * * *
The new volume deals with the period of
Gov. Hunter (1796-’99). Not till 1800 do
the local records begin to be of value : the
papers dealing with the province s infancy,
which might be expected to be preserved in
Sydney, have apparently been 'carefully
destroyed by interested parties. those
“first families”! Probably, when Wm.
Sikes, iun., became a man of importance in
the new colonv, the possessor of many hogs
and goats and convicts, he saw no necessity
for the publication of scandalous details
regarding his lamented progenitor. And so,
one conjectures, the Sikes Union for the
Suppression of State Papers was formed.
Held each son a lighted brand
In a bold, determined hand—
and—puff'.—the awkward little lot of docu-
ments collected by Govs. Philip and Hunter,
and Lieut.-Govs. Grose aud Paterson, made
a momentary black cloud on the Austral zenith.
* * *
Unluckily there still remained the docu-
ments and copies of documents at the anti-
podes—the letters to and from Governors,
the accounts of exploration and comments on
the colony and its people—collected m offices
at London. These are the materials from
which Mr. Bladen has made his volume-
doubtless with discretion. No editor c<wi
afford to tell all he knows.
* * * *
The N.S.W. records have interest, They
are alive, and talk. You see in them liow
strong Etruria grew by degrees up to the
level of Premier Reid and payment of mem-
bers. When Gov. Hunter took charge the
population of the settlements at and near
Sydney was less than 5000. How they lived
you may read in Robinson. Crusoe. The
Govt, fed most of them ; and even the Govt,
had frequently to take heed what it ate, and
drank, and wherewithal it should be clothed.
With such a land and such a climate the
colony could not easily reach, the stiaits
through which the young American colonies
passed. But it got as close to them as it
could.
* * * *

prices and wages and hours of labor by
Govt, ordinance, after the paternal fashion
of those days. Here are some of the prices
which he “ conceived to be fair and equitable
between the farmer and the laborer 100
years ago :

original figure.
* * * *
Gov. Hunter is described by a comem-
porary as * i a pleasant, sensible old man.
It grieved him that his people should be so
remiss in their religious duties. For “ here,
wrote a missionary whose style might have
liodel for the Rev. Meekin’s, “ we have
itend -with the depravity and corrup-
of the human heart, heightened and
ned in all its vicious habits by long and
ed indulgencies of inbred corruption,
ne following the bent of his own cor-
nind, and countenancing his neighbor
e pursuit of sensual gratifications,
iniquity abounds, and those outward
sins which, in Europe, could render a
i contemptible in the public eye and
ious to the civil law, are become
nable and familiar. Adultery, fornica-
fcheft, drunkenness, extortion, violence,
incleanness of every kind, the natural
mitants of Deism and infidelity, which
boldly thrown off the mask, and stalk
gh the colony in the open face of the
so that it is no uncommon thing to hear
•son say, ‘ When I was a Christian, 1
jht so-and-so.’ ”

* * * *
rhis “letter from a female convict to her
her,” printed in the True Briton (Lon-
n) of Nov. 10, 1798, gives a good idea of
3 condition of the colony :
[ take the first opportunity of informing you of
r safe arrival in this remote quarter of the
irld after a pretty good passage of six months,
ice my arrival I have purchased a house, for
lich I gave £2O, and the following articles,
lich are three turkies, at 15s. each ; three suck-
y pigs, at 10s. ; a pair of pigeons, at Bs. ; a yard-
g, £2; two muscovy ducks, at 10s. each ; three
iglish ducks, at ss. each; and a goat, five
lineas; six geese, at 15s. each. I have got a
rge garden to the house, and a licence. The
m is the “ Three Jolly Settlers.” .
I have met with tolerable good success m the
iblic line. I did a little trade in the passage
ire in a number of small articles, such
sugar, tea, tobacco, thread, snuff, needles,
id everything that I could get anything
r. The needles are a shilling a paper
jre, and fine thread is sixpence a skain. I
ive sold my petticoats at two guineas each, and
y long black cloak at 10 guineas, which shows
lat black silk sells well here; the edging that I
ive Is. Bd. per yard for in England 1 got ss. for
here. I have sold all the worst of my cloaths,
3 wearing-apparel brings a good price. I bought
roll of tobacco at Rio Janeiro of 541 b. weight,
•hicli cost me 205., which I was cheated out of; 1
mid have got 12s. a lb. for it here. I like-
wise bought a cwt. of sugar there, and
Iso many other articles. Rum sells for
s. 6d. per gallon there, and here, at times,
12. Any person coming from England with a
ew hundred pounds laid out at any of the ports
hat shipping touch at coming here are liable to
cake a fortune. Shoes that cost 4s. or ss. a pair
n England will bring from 10s. to 15s. here.
On our passage here we buried .only two women
tnd two children. The climate is very healthful
md likewise very fertile, as there are two crops a
mar of almost everything ; and I really believe,
vith the assistance of God, by the time 1 have
laid the forfeit, according to the laws of my
jountry. I shall acquire a little money to return
ionie with, which I have not the smallest doubt of,
ind to be a comfort to you at the latter end of your
lays. Any person that should have a mind to
come out here as a settler, by applying at the
Secretary of State’s Office, may have a free pas-
sage, and likewise two men and a farm here, which
is great encouragement. I should be very glad to
year from you the first opportunity. I live by
myself, and did not do as the rest of the women
did on the passage, which was, everyone that
could had a husband.
I shall conclude with giving my, love to my
brother and sisters, nieces and nephews, so am,
dear father, your ever dutiful, loving, and affec-
tionate daughter till dead.—S.B.

Jennings Carmichael’s Poems.
I Jennings Carmichael’s poems are issued in
legant form by Melville, Mullen and Slade,
r. F. Hogan, M.P. (Eng.), contributes a
>ompous preliminary puli' which asserts that
he author possesses the ‘ ‘ spirit, the sym-
pathetic insight, and the imaginative power
>f the true poet.” Nothing could be further
:rom the fact. Miss Carmichael s verses are
nostly pithless and bloodless, with neither
neaning nor message. They divide themselves
into two main classes —poems about children
md poems about scenery. There are two or
three in each class with a little charm : none
with any power. Of “insight” there is
hardly a gleam. The descriptive poems read
as if the author had sat down in front of her
scene, and deliberately tried to reproduce it
in rhymes. But her epithets rarely show
the scene to the reader. She has. no
“vision ” ; she is always outside her subject.
Devout enough, no doubt; but devotion
isn’t poetry. , * .
Perhaps it is Miss Carmichael’s talent for
hunting the tame moral from its lair which
made Brunton Stephens dub her ‘ ‘ the Aus-
tralian Jean Ingelow.” But the comparison
isn’t over happy. Jean Ingelow had a little
“ imaginative power ” and some capacity for
subjective thought : Jennings Carmichael
has neither. She wants even Ingelow s
knack of melody ; a line like
Grand is the leisure of the earth !
is quite beyond her. Miss Carmichael s
talent, in fact, lies in her power to put into
—>re or less monotonous rhymes the emotions
dch passing moods and objects stir in her.
snerally, the emotions are shallow, femi-
ae, and commonplace enough. When Miss
,rmichael strikes the personal note keenly,
e is effective —if your own mood is in
mpathy ; but her effect is neither powerful
ir permanent.
* * * *
Only Mr. Hogan’s ridiculous over-praise
rpels this protest. As some amends, here
Miss Carmichael’s prettiest poem :
WHEN LIGHTWOOD FLOWERS,
memory of landscapes comes to me,
The flush of springtide fills my heart again ;
he lissom budded twigs once more I see, .
A yellow largesse over hill and plain,
wealth of blossom blows upon the earth,
In the young passion of awakening hours,
ut brightest bloom in all the land has birth,
When lightwood flowers,
far-off river current curls and sweeps
Its gurgling waters in my ears once more;
watch again how each slow ripple creeps
In foamless wavelets on that river shore ;
see the grey boat moored upon the bank,
Beneath the shadow thrown from leafy bowers ;
hear the singing in the rushes dank,
When lightwood flowers.
till hang the gnarled roots o’er the placid tide,
Where I would lie in girlish, gladsome days,
hatching the sparkles o’er the river wide,
Till eyes were dazzled by the golden rays.
,till float the yellow blossoms down the stream,
Shaken from slender limbs in perfumed showers,
Jeneath whose sweetness I could dream and
dream,
When lightwood flowers !
; hear the waters swirl below the roots,
And seem to see again, with restful eyes,
The bronzy buds, and leaves, and spiral shoots,
Wedded to break the blue of cloudless slues.
Tow was I wont to listen to the sound
Of throbbing pulses and returning powers 1
The heart of Nature leaps within the ground,
When lightwood flowers !
The reedy marshes girdled by the hills,
The low-set orchard on the willowed road ;
The dreamful distances where blue mist fills
The bushy spaces of the birds’ abode.
The stretch of valley, rich with growing gram,
Dyed with the glory of the sunset hours;
The ranges haunted by a wraith of ram,
When lightwood flowers.
O ! springs of girlhood, ye were wondrous sweet \
O ! suns of girlhood, ye did shine so strong;
No springtides bring your flowers beneath my feet,
No noontides shine so brightly, nor so long.
The loveliest and best seems dead and cold,
Buried as surely as your lisping showers,
And memory only brings the days of old,
When lightwood flowers.
* * * *
This is not the work of “ a sterling acquisi-
tion to the poetesses of the Victorian era,
but of a writer of rather graceful emotional
verse ; and in that guise Miss Carmichael is
entitled to Australian welcome and esteem.
Some Recent Publications.
I George Robertson and Co. issue “ A Visit
to Blestland,” by W. H. Galier, which
carries dullness to the pitch of genius. Style
is admirably suited to matter —“Just as we
were about to set sail, Mac became possessed
of a doubt as to whether the locker
contained a certain condiment which he
very highly prized,” means ‘*When we
were ready to start, Mac. missed the mus-
tard ” —and the author never loses a chance
of using ten big words instead of two little
ones.
Says Lond. Speaker
No Australian poet has yet been quite'Satisfac-
tory, oven in a little way, and Mr. J. 13. O Mara
is not an exception. We get no new airs, no new
emotions from the newest world. They arrive at
writing very fair verse, .which in England would
crive thorn at best a tenthuate reputation, but the
passion is one caught faintly from English models;
the eyes that look on that brilliant new world are
jaded. They do not even take the best or second-
lest or third-best models. There is a pretence of
ree living, a convention of the wild and imcon-
entional in their verses which deceives no one.
'he sprinkling of the page with words un-
uniliar to English ears no more give [ we
fiould write “gives” in AustraliaJ the atmo-
phere than if the verse were written beside a
jondon lodging-house fire—which, indeed, the
reater part of it might have been. Mr. U Mara
ioes not affect the bushranger. He is .quite
imple and direct, and apparently has no ambition
o bring the South to use, or to do anything but
yrh pleasant and facile verse. His verse is
nanly and dignified, but it lacks the magical
uggestion that makes verse poetry.
50 far as Mr. O’Hara is concerned, this is
rery just criticism. For the rest, the writer
s apparently not familiar with the three men
it the top of our poets’ class, and he is cer-
lainly unfamiliar with “ The Banjo.”
The author of “Australian Gossip and
Story,” a series of articles reprinted from the
Sydney Stock and Station Journal, mora-
lises agreeably on themes sufficiently. re-
moved from the obvious to be interesting,
though he doesn’t stir far from the obvious
point of view.
The North Queensland Register Xmas
number does Charters Towers credit. Pass-
ing the printing, it is the best of Australasian
issues—bar only The Bulletin’s. Becke
and Montgomery contribute characteristic
tales; Jennings Carmichael and P. Luftig
give verses ; and the rest of the literature is
also original and Australian—not the.work of
foreign syndicate-hacks. Some of Payne and
Radford’s illustrations are very fair, if a little
stiff.

mrw B fn Oa™ED 0 a ™ED AT REDUCED
PRICES, SO AS TO BRING THEM tt\j
THINGS WITH THE ORDER OF
The Amount of the Reductions
Is fixed by the amount of duty paid. All goods
on which TEN PER CENT, has been paid will
be reduced TEN PER CENT., while goods
b T m § PER CENT, duty w?ll be
reduced FIFTEEN PER CENT. This is an
edict, from which no manner of departure will be
allowed.
The Thoroughness of the
Reductions
Cannot be questioned. When the whole of the
re-markmg is done, every article will show on its
ticket, or in some other way, the OLD PRICE
and the NEW PRICE at which the line is offered.
The Effect of the Reductions
Must be a great loss to ourselves. We desire to
be candid, and we repeat it will be a great loss.
But we are not distressed at that. We owe the
people a DEBT OF GRATITUDE WE CAN
NEVER DISCHARGE, but the change in the
national policy gives us an opportunity, and we
hasten to pay SOMETHING ON ACCOUNT.

THE COMRADE TRUE.
U T, n not quite alone,” he said,
r or though of comrades three
limre s one hath sped and one hath fled—
x et one remains with me.
ihe first—my dearest friend was he—
-1 knew his heart was true ;
He sailed away to Baffin's Bay—
I hold his I O U.
“ The next—he stole the girl I loved :
Her loss I did not rue.
A jade was she—but (woe is me !)
He took my dollars too.
The third God knows, he’s with me still,
He lent me sovereigns four •
N ° r u uannon boom or crack of Doom
ohcill drive him from my door.
Why count the friends I lost ? There’s one
1 always keep alone,
For only he of all the three
Will always be my own ;
He will not slight at morn or eve
I he old friend for the new;
Our debtors leave our hearts to grieve
Our duns are always true.” '

P. LUFTIG.
A SERMON.
Good (oiks, who weary foes and friends
With seekings after “ better things,”
VV „y. 9 arve your ways and shape your ends
With evangelic mutterings?
Give all your dreary fancies wings •
.Let loose your clinging, bat-like fears ;
u ,rii ‘U "diat the old-time poet sings :
1 will matter nought in fifty years ! ”
Say, can you tell what Death portends ?
_ Say, can you mark the crystallings
Ofsoul in matter ?—make amends
For Time’s unholy ravagings ?
a ' • s f en as the warning rings
Add wipe away the old. arrears ;
<< ,T, as fe the joys that freedom brings—
I will matter nought in fifty years ! ”
Drink of the cup that Bacchus lends;
Eist to the tender whisperings
Of Love that gracious Venus vends
To those who seek her rosy springs.
Take little heed of happenings :
Joy must be tempered oft with tears ;
Of worldly pleasure be the kings—
’Twill matter naught in fifty years ! ”
Envoy.
Come, speak your parts and dance your flings
Howe’er the vane of Fortune veers ;
Be reckless in your roysterings :
“ ’Twill matter naught in fifty years ! ”
N.S.W.
MACHETE.
CAPE RAOUL, TASMAN’S PENINSULA.
Scar ever frowning to the Southern pole
Over a sullen ocean, thou hast seen
Splendor of God and devilry of men,
Earthquake and tempest, and the stubborn soul
Of the oppressor; now thou art a scroll
Where Time has writ the fury that has been,
And thou for solace on the clouds dost lean,
From their full utterance gathering a soft toll.
The surges at thy base for ever thunder;
The piping winds like haggard spirits wail;
And from afar the melancholy main,
Tinged as if Sorrow’s palace was thereunder,
Yearns to thee for its solitary pain,
Unsoothed by the magic of a sail.
Maoriland.
HUBERT CHURCH.
AT THE CENTURY-END.
I.
What matters it to us, that in the seething,
Unheeding stream of Life our faith gets torn
Beyond redress ; that Truth is ever breathing
Death to the fondest hopes that man has born ?
What matters it to us—who dare not tarry
To cool in happy dreams the reeling brain,
Whither the mighty eventide will carry
Us —whence we came—to Nothing, back again ?
Into one eager lifetime we have pressed
Whole ages full of hate and love and thought
And work and giant deeds —carelessly casting
All peace aside, all quietness, all rest.
What wonder, that we sadly yearn for nought
But sleep eternal, silence everlasting ?
N.Q.
S. V. KOTZE.
II.
Where then shall Peace be found? Upon the
height
Of glory ? Or within the gloomy vale
Where Death sits waiting for us stark and pale
With bloodless lips and eyes that have no sight ?
Or shall we seek it mid the quiet light
Of shadowy cloisters where sad hermits wail
With stripes and fastings ? How shall these
prevail
Though they should kneel and pray the livelong
night ?
Not in this wise may ye attain the goal;
But rather on the level plains of life
Amid the daily round of toil and strife,
The stings that chasten and make strong the soul.
Quit you like men, be patient, brave and kind,
And ye shall gain thereby true peace of mind.
N.S.W.
E.W.N.A.

_ Highest Grade. Mild Plug Tobacco-.
YANKEE; DOODLE ,
“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobaoco that has evei
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement, for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
• years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
| exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
j forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
i been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business lias been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford to
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more American leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands: —“CON-
QUEROR,” “ SIR ROGER,’’ “ CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE’
ranks supreme.

scription outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 3s. 3d.
added for postage. England £l, with 6s. 6d. added for

prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
SATURDAY, JANUARY 11, 18911.
A Lesson in Land-Grant Railways.
The close of the Midland Railway arbitra-
tion brings Maoriland a step nearer to the
end of a most disastrous experiment. The
Midland is the first extensive land-grant
railway scheme which has reached anything
resembling a definite conclusion in Austra-
lasia. One or two very small ones got
through in times past with a moderate
amount of success, so far as the owners were
concerned, and a very small amount of suc-
cess as regards the country ; one very large
one in Westraliais in a feeble, semi-hopeless
state, and its financial methods have created
a great moral stench in the land ; a number
of others in Queensland have, so far, luckily
failed to get afloat ; and various proposals by
irresponsible local persons to build the S.A.
Transcontinental line on the land-grant and
British syndicate principle have so far led
to nothing. But the Maoriland Midland has
at last arrived at some more or less definite
result, and any province which dreams of
building railways on the land-grant system
should think hard over that result and all
that it implies. So far as things have gone
they show that the people who go into this
kind of business are mostly wild-cat specu-
lators who don’t risk their own money on it,
and who, apparently, have no intention
beyond grabbing the land, and exploiting
the State for further concessions, and then
abandoning a shoddy line to the debenture-
holders, or leaving it to be closed up and
deserted. Or, if that was not the purpose of
the Midland Co., then its doings can only be
set down to the awful stupidity of private
enterprise in general and British private
enterprise in particular.
* * *
The Midland syndicate started under ex-
cellent auspices. It was to build and work
235 miles of railway, and the State agreed to
pay in land-grants half the cost of the work.
The grant was to be handed over in instal-
ments as the line progressed, so that the co.
could raise money on it as it went along. All
the Government lands in Canterbury, West-
land, and Nelson —over 6,000,000 acres,
valued at nearly £4,ooo,ooo—were closed
against settlement until the line was com-
pleted and the co. had selected its grants,
the only reservation being that the State
could set aside mining reserves to the extent
of not more than 750,000 acres. Otherwise,
the co. could range all over this vast area
and select the best and most saleable pro-
perties, and the land was put in at such a
low valuation that the Midland Co. sold its
grants for fully one-third more than their
nominal value. Consequently, instead of
the State paying one-half the cost of the
venture, it really paid two-thirds, and if the
syndicate had been an honest and business-
like concern, it would have ultimately found
itself in possession of a line valued at about
£2,400,000 which would have cost it only a
little over £BOO,OOO. The work was esti-
mated to yield a return —with good manage-
ment—of 2 per cent, on its cost, which
would have meant dividends at the splendid
rate of 6 per cent, on the co.’s capital. It
was hardly possible to have a better prospect
than this, and the manner in which it was
fooled away was absolutely unique.
* * * »
The Midland Co., in the first place, flatly
refused to put up the necessary capital. It
paid up £250,000 only, and wasted every
penny of it away in office expenses, directors’
fees in London, commissions, interest on
borrowed money, travelling expenses, and
miscellaneous frill and dignity. It raised
£311,000 by the sale of land received on
account of its grant, and fooled that away in
the same fashion. Its own cash thus equalled
£561,000, not one coin of which was really
spent on railway building. Then it borrowed
£825,000 in London, and of this amount a
part seems to have gone the same way as the
capital and the land-grant—at all events the
whole amount spent in actual railway works
was not quite £760,000. And apparently it
accumulated other liabilities in addition, for,
according to the statement of its own legal
representative, its total expenditure was
£1,864,000 —£760,000 for railway building
and £1,104,000 gone in ways not very clearly
explained. Even if the State had given
twice the cost of the work in land, instead
of one-half, it could not have kept afloat so
utterly fatuous a syndicate as this.
* * * *
The line, as far as it went, was therefore
constructed wholly with borrowed money.
Said money was raised by selling 5 per cent,
debentures in London at £92 10s., so that,
after making a moderate allowance for
expenses, the money cost fully 5| per cent.
As the co. had no revenue from railway
traffic until the first section was opened, and
very little thereafter, the interest on the
borrowed money had to come, in part at
least, out of more borrowings, and the
interest on these further borrowings had to
come out of still more borrowings, so that,
if the Midland railway ever had been
completed, and it had finished on the same
lines as it commenced, it would have had to
earn about 6 per cent, on a capital cost that
was nearly twice as large as it should have
been, in order to meet its liabilities. In
other words, putting its value per mile on
the same basis as the State railways’, it would
have required to earn something approaching
12 per cent., whereas the State lines earn
barely 2f, if it was to keep out of bank-
ruptcy. This position was so utterly hope-
less that it is difficult to suppose that the
Midland Co. ever seriously intended finish-
ing and working the line. Whether it existed
principally to find billets for the directors,
or to grab the land-grant, or whether its
hope lay in bailing up the State for further
assistance, or whether it proposed to abandon
the line at last to the miserable debenture-
holders, or whether it had any definite pur-
pose or any definite idea where it was going,
is all a mystery.
* * * »
Then the Midland Railway Co. became an
insatiable mendicant sitting on the doorstep
of the Government offices and asking for
“ more.” It demanded an extension of time,
and got it. It demanded permission to alter
the route agreed upon at the Lake Brunner
section, and got that. It demanded permis-
sion to substitute an incline for a tunnel at
Arthur’s Pass, whereby it would save
£500,000 at a woeful cost in efficiency—and
it got that also. And, having got all these
things, it further demanded that it should be
allowed to abandon altogether the least-pro-
mising section of the undertaking ; that its
land should be exempted from the land-tax ;
and that the State should guarantee 3| per
cent, interest on the cost of the line. This
final demand was the last straw. The State
could have borrowed money at 3| per cent,
and done the work for itself at one-half
the outlay. In that case it would have
owned the railway ; its liability would have
been much smaller than that which the
co. asked it to undertake ; and when the
Midland line became a profitable spec,
some time in the future the profit
would have gone to the public Treasury.
The astounding British syndicate demanded
that the State should stand the loss, while
there was a loss, and that the co. should get
all the profit, when there was a profit.
Naturally enough, the Maoriland Govern-
ment refused to entertain this unspeakably
impudent suggestion, and soon after the
Midland Co. ceased work. A little later it
stopped payment of interest on its deben-
tures and became a hopeless ruin. Then its
contract time expired, and, as it had only
completed a little more than a quarter—the
easiest quarter —of the undertaking, and
there was no earthly prospect of further pro-
gress, the Seddon Government formally can-
celled the agreement and took over the un-
finished line as provided for in the contract.
It was driven to this course by the fact that,
until the work was completed or the contract
ended in some other way, all the State lands
in Canterbury, Westland, and Nelson had
to remain shut against settlement at an
enormous loss to the Treasury and the
community.
♦ * * *
The next stage of the long, dismal agony
of Maoriland’s land-grant syndicate swindle
was the arbitration, in which the Midland
Co. kept up its reputation to the end as the
most shameless joint-stock job that ever
came out of Bull-land. It demanded
£1,817,000 damages and various other items,
which, with the land-grant already received,
would have made something near £2,500,000
as the price of 75 miles of not-very-well-con-
structed railroad and seven miles of partially-
finished ditto. It claimed these amounts for
reasons which were as remarkable as the rest
of its proceedings : (1) The concessions
granted to it had been “unreasonably de-
layed.” The said concessions were granted
solely as a favor and with no shadow of right
whatever, and the Midland Co, demanded
heavy damages because the favor hadn’t been
granted more quickly than it was. (2) The
State has reserved certain mining lands. It
retained the right to reserve 750,000 acres,
and of this area it actually reserved only
about 150,000 acres, therefore it treated the
co. five times as well as the letter of the
agreement demanded, and the co. wanted
damages because it wasn’t treated still better.
(3) The State refused to allow the co. to
annex the timber on these reserved lands, to
which timber the Midland syndicate had no
claim whatever according to its contract.
(4) The State had imposed a land-tax which,
when the co. selected its lands and sold them,
would be payable by the buyers in common
with all other land-holders in Maoriland, and
thus it had, or might have, “depreciated”
the value of these lands. As the land which
was handed over to the co. at £1 an acre
realised on an average £1 75., it got much
more than it bargained for, despite the de-
preciation, yet it wanted damages in addition.
And (5) it wanted more damages—great
quantities of them—for the seizure of its
line, despite a cast-iron agreement in writing
that the line was liable to seizure if the work
was not carried out according to the terms of
the specification. There was a long, weary,
and expensive arbitration on all these points,
and finally the umpire, Mr. Blake, an Irish-
Canadian lawyer and politician, gave his ver-
dict against the co. on point.
* * * *
The situation of the Maoriland Govern-
ment in the matter is'now an interesting one.
It has got 82 miles of railroad in three dis-
connected scraps, and to make them of any
serious value it must complete the great line
itself. As the object of the whole business
was to avoid making the line itself, this is a
serious matter. The co.’s agreement has
blocked settlement in Canterbury, Westland
and Nelson for 10 years, and has lost the
Treasury 10 years’ land-revenue over
6,000,000 acres of country ; and, in conse-
quence of this reservation, there is still
practically no population along the route,
and hardly any traffic. The B„ate has paid
nearly £400,000 in land-grants for its 82
miles of line —only 75 of which are really in
working-order—or £5OOO per mile, which is
very little, if any, less than the amount for
which it could have been built and owned the
road at present rates. Even in the old, extra-
vagant days of high wages and bad manage-
ment, and lavish expenditure for land-resump-
tions, Maoriland railways only cost £7700 per
mile, and with low wages, cheap material,
economy, and practically no land-resump-
tions to be paid for, the Midland line, so far
as it is built, should have cost no more than
the State’s present outlay. In addition to
this, these three scraps of road purport to
be mortgaged for £825,000 to the British
debenture-holders, and the position of the
Government with regard to their claims is a
difficult problem. If the claims hold good,
then the line will represent a national outlay
of £15,000 per mile. And, as a further
trouble, the seizure by the State is only a
partial and ineffective measure. All the
Government is authorised to do is to run the
bit of line as the company’s trustee ; if it
more than pays expenses the co. gets the
surplus;, if it doesn’t pay expenses the loss
can’t be recovered from' the co., which is
utterly lost and insolvent. To acquire actual
possession it will probably have to pay for
the work in cash after already paying in land,
and before any ( definite result is arrived at
there will almost certainly be more costly
litigation. Probably the debenture-holders
will foreclose on the co.’s interest in its little
scrap of iron rail, and then the State, and
the co., and the debenture-holders, and the
miscellaneous creditors, will tight a quad-
rangular duel and ultimately arrive at some
kind of a settlement. Meanwhile, the co.,
being in England, has the capacious ear of
the British public, and its mendacious howl
goes out through the length and breadth of
the land about the unscrupulous brigandage
of the Maoriland Government. Altogether,
the Midland railway scheme is about the
most awful example of the land-grant swindle,
and the natural end thereof, that can well be
imagined
The Transvaal —and Some Reflections.
The attack on the Transvaal by I)r. Jameson,
the butcher of the Matabele, has come to an
ignominious ending. Doubtless the glorified
Rhodes arranged the plot, and Jameson was
merely his catspaw. If Jameson had br.mgiit
the chestnuts safely out of the tire, the monkey
would have taken the credit ; as it is, only
Jameson’s fingers are burnt. Probably, when
the invader marched over the frontier, he
reckoned upon reaching Johannesburg before
he could be intercepted ; and lie made sure of
Johannesburg assistance. Johannesburg—to its
credit —kept quiet; and the Boers were too
alert for Jameson, and entrenched themselves
right in his path. It does not appear that they
at any time took the offensive ; and Jameson
had no choice but to retire or to make a bold
stroke for success. The bold stroke, in this
instance, was mere madness. Probably not
twice their number of the best troops in the
world could shift, without artillery, 1500
Boers from a strong position in their own
territory. Jameson apparently tried to
do it with 700 or 1000 men—man for man
probably unequal to the Boers in guerrilla
warfare. There co.uld only be one result.
Jameson was forced to surrender, with a
loss of 240 killed and wounded ; Rhodes has
cast him off'; and lie is to stand his trial by
court-martial.
# * * *
The game was obviously to embroil England
so far that the Boers would be “settled” finally.
It was reckoned that once British blood was
spilt, once the British flag had been fired on,
Britain would have to suppoit the invasion
whether she liked it or not. Reliance was
placed on the real grievances of the “foreigners
at Johannesburg, on the warlike temper of the
Tories, on the still smarting sore of Majuba
Hill, on the influence of the Chartered Com-
pany and the Duke of Fife, and on the
glamor of the Rhodesian policy of “ all
South Africa a British province.” Had the
Johannesburgers risen, and had they and
Jameson won one battle, or even held their
ground, Britain would hardly have missed the
opportunity of at least converting her nominal
suzerainty of the Transvaal into an effective pro-
tectorate. The inaction of Johannesburg spoilt
the scheme. Jameson became merely an un-
authorised freebooter bearing arms into friendly
territory. The cutting of the wires—equivalent
to the burning of his ships—in order to prevent
his recall, made things worse. And, to crown
all, he failed to sustain himself. Not having
half-armed savages to deal with, he was
ingloriously beaten ; the climax of Laing’s Nek
was capped ; and once more the British flag
was trampled on by filthy Boer feet. The
episode is one to make any well-constituted
Tory gnash his teeth.
* * * *
But Jameson’s crime for whatever the
Boers have done they have done nothing to
justify attack by the mercenary troops of a
greedy British syndicate—has had consequences
infinitely more important than its local results.
It has stimulated a hardly-paralleled outburst
of indignation on the Continent against Britain
and her methods. For it cannot be denied
that, after all, Jameson was but carrying out
on a small scale what Britain has consistently
performed on a large. The bombardment of
Alexandria had not a whit more justifica-
tion than the invasion of the Transvaal :
and the policy now' sought to be applied
to the Boers is but the policy by which
Britain has won half her empire from
weaker nations or savage tribes. This time,
however, its brutality was too obvious. Ger
many has been stimulated to a most significant
protest; Holland is enraged; France is de-
lighted at the British reverse ; and there is not
a doubt that the sympathy of other European
nations is with the Boers—or, rather, against
the British. Stupid John Bull suddenly
awakes to find himself the most unpopular
person in Europe, and is indignant that his
motives and actions should be so misconstrued.
More, he begins to be a little alarmed. France
and Russia he knows are hostile to him ; but
if there is any nation to which he has a
right to look for support it is Germany,
with which he is connected by blood, by royal
alliances, and by g,n apparent concord o
European interests. That Germany should
turn against him gives even stupid John Bull
pause.
* * * *
It is high time that Britain seriously con
sidered her position, and began to set her house
in order for war. Her navy is her sole hope ;
she has not, like the Continental nations, her
manhood enrolled in her defence ; and were war
to break out suddenly, all her millions could
not buy her trained soldiers. It takes time to
make a soldier. You can do it in five years,
perhaps in three; but the foe at Britain's
gates will not wait even three years.
And now that it is certain that every
European power w'ould gladly see her humbled,
even if they would not gladly take a hand to
humble her, it behoves British statesmen to
face seriously the situation which has been
fronting them for the last score of years.
Quite apart from Australians’ direct interest in
the encounter, we must have some indirect
anxiety for Britain’s future. We hate British
cant and greed and hypocrisy, but we admire
British blood and grit and force. We have
nothing to gain, and a good deal to lose, by the
relegation of Britain to the. ranks of second-
rate powers —a contingency which looms ever
nearer. With all Britain’s faults, we cannot
wish her failure in the struggle before her—if
she has anything like a just claim to struggle for.
* * * *
In the Transvaal matter, Britain is clearly in
the wrong ; and, as this fact seems to be
thoroughly recognised, the squabble will
probably go no farther. But it is interesting
to ask how exactly would Australia stand were
war declared to-morrow and this country
embroiled in it by reason of the existing
British connection. How many men, cap-
able of bearing arms, have we to face a pos-
sible foe, and how many trained men ? How
many stand of rifles are available, and how
much ammunition? What artillery, uniforms,
accoutrements have we ? The Bulletin sus-
pects that truthful answers to these questions
would be by no means reassuring. Some day—
and perchance soou—the ponderous articles of
our newspapers and the ceaseless gabble of our
politicians will be cramped into one word—
Fight! We eat, and drink, and make merry,
and chatter, and scribble conceitedly enough :
suppose the grim utterance came—“ Thou fool,
this night shall thy soul be required of thee ! ”
—what then ?

Federation Critics.
Arthur Rae, an ex-member of the N.S.W.
Parliamentary Labor-party, writes at' great
ength to Sydney Daily Telegraph pointing
out obstacles in the way of Australian Federa-
tion Owing to the obvious and beautiful fact
that construction is difficult and destruction
vety simple, and that it is many hundred times
easier to show what shouldn’t than what should
be done, critics of this description are always
plentiful. | Also, the person who shows what
shouldu t be done always looks more powerful
and more eloquent than the other man, for the
same reason that a conflagration looks more
picturesque than the spectacle of a peaceful,
ordinary person laying bricks. One of Mr.
Rae s complaints is that the Federal second
chamber is likely to be called a “senate,”
whereas it would be more appropriate to call it
a “ States-council. ” This is a matter of com-
paratively small importance; so long as the
second chamber is well-constructed and does its
work to good purpose, The Bulletin, for one,
doesn t care much even if it is called a port-
manteau or a horse-box. Mr. Rae also com-
plains that there is nothing said so far about
any arrangement for secession, and that the
federation seems likely to make Freetrade
impossible—in other words, that most Aus-
tralians are Protectionists and there is no pro-
vision for putting a foreign-trade minority on
top But most of all he objects as follows :
While urging this contention, I submit, at the same
time, that it is extremely unfair for a constitution to be
drawn up by delegates, of whom some will be elected on
the plural-voting principle in force in Victoria, Queensland,
Tasmania, and Western Australia; those from this colony
on the basis of “ one man one vote,” and the South Aus-
tralian delegates on the single votes of both men and
women. Surely we must expect the return of more Con-
servative delegates from those provinces with a restricted
property franchise, than those elected by the more demo-
cratic colonies I have mentioned.
There is no remedy suggested for this obvious
difficulty—unless it be the remedy of waiting
till the more backward provinces catch up.
ihe same difficulty has been pointed out many
hundred times already, and no remedy ever
appeared—except waiting. And if New South
Wales holds back until Queensland and Victoria
and A estralia have got manhood suffrage, and
then B.A. and Maoriland insist on further
waiting till New South Wales and the rest
have womanhood suffrage as well, when the
end of the long interval comes things will be
no further advanced. By that time one or
more of the more advanced provinces will have
made further improvements in its system of

Du i Punishment.
“Here, as in England,” snorts Melb. Argus,
a feeling seems to be growing against the due
punishment of vile creatures who, from time to
time, perpetrate horrible crimes.” Which out-
break of scorn, by the way, was suggested by
the agitation for the release of George Dean
at a time when his horrible crime and personal
vileness were quite unproved. But no matter.
The point of the foregoing Tory remark seems
to lie in the application of that little word
due. Xo wrong-thinking Radical objects to
the punishment of horrible criminals, as far as
iiiE Bulletin knows. The gallows is objected
to on the ground that a compulsory corpse,
swinging from a Government noose, is
not a first-class moral reformer, nor a
valuable influence upon murderous-minded
people. Wrong-thinking citizens hold this
opinion. ihe Argus and other organs of
correct thought are in a position to state that
hanging is a due punishment whenever the law
calls for it. Xot so very long ago sheep-
stealers and forgers and “ disloyal” stump
orators who spoke ill of the monarohial insti-
tution were duly killed, but a “feeling grew ”
against these legal proceedings until Kiev dn!\
ceased. The word “ due,” it will be found on
reflection, is an ancient Tory excuse for brutal
opptession and general bad conduct on
the part of the powers that be. Wrong
thinkers have been slowly changing the due
into the undue for centuries past, nevertheless
existing evils have their worshippers by virtue
or the lamentable law which keeps them alive.
Respectability still points a fat finger at the
figure falling through the prison drop with its
head in a white hair, and says, “ Due punish-
ment ! Respectability said the same thing, in
the same tone of voice, when James Barrett
aged 16 years, the first “man” to be put to
death in Australia, was hanged by the Botany
ay Government for stealing provisions from
the state store-house.
Tiie viciously-inspired Criminal Law of New
South Wales in such cases as that of T. M.
Sheridan sins with equal malignity against the
accused the jury, and the Executive. It
classes the accused, who knows that he did not
mean to take life, with wilful murderers: it
constrains the jury, who believe that he did
not mean to kill, to return a verdict to the
effect that he wilfully killed, and it constrains
ie Executive, except under rare compulsion
from irregular sources, to inflict the highest
punishment for an offence which members of
the Council individually admit is not murder
in the first degree. This triple-headed iniquity
is the direct consequence of employing the
woul malice in Criminal Law in a sense
which it does not possess in morals. Every
wrongful act done intentionally without just or
lawful excuse is supposed, in Criminal Law, to
ie done maliciously, so that men like Sheridan,
who take life by misadventure, are, because
they intended a wrongful act at the time,
legaHy held to have taken that life with malice,
in morals the essence of murder lies in the deli-
berate taking of life to gain private ends or with
malice against the person. This definition
stands true to every mind, legal and otherwise,
it presented in the abstract; yet all concerned
in the trial of any such case under the existing
Criminal Code adopt the legal definition of
malice, thereby obfuscating their own intelli-
gence, perplexing and bewildering the general
public and often punishing with death persons
not morally guilty of the offences ascribed to
them. N. S. W. Parliament is promised a debate
on Capital Punishment next session, and
whether or not the death penalty be abolished
it is to be hoped that the word “malice” as
defined in Criminal Law will receive the atten-
tion of the House.
Judge Windeyer having obtained six months’
leave, the public concludes that he is making
ready to retire. The public’s wish is father to
its conclusion. The Bulletin, being in no
way dominated or influenced by the subtle and
powerful forces which for so long have einbas-
tioned a man whose continuance on the Bench
of Justice was, after the hideous Mount Rennie
trial, a public calamity and disgrace, gladly
lays a wreath of carrots on the official coffin of
his Honor. lor full vindication of the course
which it lias pursued in relation to Mr. Justice
Windeyer, whom it has only known and criti-
cised in Ins public capacity, it confidently
appeals to the verdict of history—to the undis-
puted and indisputable facts of the most shame-
,l o° •• , 1 tr * t * lat * las ta ken place under
the British flag during the last hundred years,
lor the rest, it wishes Mr. Windeyer health
and prosperity, and devoutly trusts that Aus-
tralia will never again see on the bench a man
so utterly unfitted by Nature to preside over
criminal trials.
“ Serious conflict/eared ” was the legend on
the newspaper-contents hills last Thursday
when news came that the African Chartered
Co. s guerrillas were pouring into the Transvaal,
lhe national hypocrisy looms up even there.
Everybody who knew anything about the
malter hoped for that row all he was worth.
I lie Boers hoped for it, for they are suffering
from swelled head of the largest and most
greasy description. The British in the Trans-
vaal hoped for it, for the large, fat, pious Boer
is an unbearable person. The Chartered Co.
hoped for it for the sake of territory and plun-
det* and influence. Rhodes wanted it for the
sake of his South African federation. The
papers wanted it for the sake of the sensation,
lhe world m general howled for it because
a great upstanding fight is a thing that
vvarms the public soul. And, in any case,
the invasion, if it had succeeded, was a
thing with many good points about it.
Lhe Boer is about the most unprogressive
and most ignorant white man on earth.
He is brutal, dirty, intensely pious, given
to long, dull sermons and hymns, given

A curious point of law. At YVhittlesea (Vic.)
a nre broke out on some land belonging to the
Board of Works and spread to. an adjacent
farm where it did much injury to property
and badly combusted a woman who tried to
put it out. There was a person in charge of
the Board of Works land, but he only looked
on and didn t attempt to stop the spread of the
S 8;, jury, after two trials, awarded
10s. damages for the property destroyed
and the burnt woman. Then the Full Court
considered the matter, and decided that though
the person in charge knew of the fire, and
didn t attempt to put it out, no damages were
recoverable unless it was shown that he dis-
tinctly approved of the conflagration, and so
another new trial was ordered. According to
this new and brilliant reading of the
farmers wife can’t recover damages for the
injuries done through the other man’s neglect
unless it is shown that the other man reallv
enjoyed the hre and was pleased to know that
she was injured, and as it is practically ini
possible to show whether he was pleased
or not, unless he waved his hat and
o leeiet when he saw her running away with
her skirt on fire, the ease hopeless.
Ihe most astonishing feature of the law is its
capacity for breaking out in new and unex-
pected places. If the same rule had been
applied to the case of Madame De Alba, who
was awarded damages for having her’head
shattered by a lift accident in the Freehold
Investment Co s building, she obviously couldn’t
have recovered anything unless the Freehold
Co. was shown to have distinctly approved of
the lift being in such a state as to lead to the
accident, and it had been obviously pleased
and happy to know that it was in such a state.
Ohiet Justice Madden is admittedly an ex-
perienced lawyer, but experience in law seems
° 3e . a )ou k as destructive of common-sense as
experience in rum or opium. Meanwhile, if
the plaintiff wins his case, the damages won’t
cover Ins expenses, and if the Board of Works
wms it will have spent a lot more in trying to
resist payment of the £272 10s. than the money
amounts to. The civil law is probably the most
expensive swindle ever invented— and the
stupidest.

Ed. Bulletin,—l can endorse, as far as Thurs-
day Island is concerned, the comments on the
Japs, by your Roebourne (W. A.) correspondent.
-the Jap. women are now at Croydon, Nor-
manton Townsville, and Mackay, supplying
Australia with any quantity of disease. Twelve
months ago there were a good number of white
shipwrights employed here, earning 15s. per
day; and there were three ship - building
slips owned by white men. Now there if
only one white man’s slip, employing the owner
and one man. Two Japs, own the other two
slips, and do nearly all the work, employing
between -0 and 30 Japs, at from ss. to 7s.
pci i ay. ibis place was opened by white
men and now the colored men rule the roost—
thanks largely to the local black-labor firms.
All kinds of Jap. mechanics and merchants are
coming here and leaving for the South by
nearly every “China steamer.” Within the
last two months they have introduced women
in men s clothing for the purpose of prostitu-
tion. Ihese women are attended by a keeper
to every gang, and are practically slaves, as
the police here know.—Pearl-Shell.
To the making of cycle heroes we shall have no
end. Given a bike (on time-payment) any sort
of young man may hope to achieve a transient
tame, and the trouble, very soon, will be to put
a name to the real champion of the hour. The
pacing ” question is peculiar to bike-racing.
We don t have steam engines to encourage the
competitors in long-distance running-matches
nor a lb-oar crew to show the way in a champion
sculling-race, yet cyclists require “ triplets ” to
bring them along. Many of them seem incap-
able of travelling their hardest “ on their owin’’
lo abolish pace-making from test-races will be
to abolish champions who wouldn’t be chain
pious if somebody else had had a chance to
hang on to the triplet.” And before
any sort- of champion has reigned for
six months, he will be overtaken by dis-
turbances in his stomach and dethroned If
his vanquisher gets biliary trouble next time
they meet he will go down because he wasn’t in
his proper form,” and some unexpected high-
geared outsider will capture the blue ribbon
tlirough “ stealing a march ” on the cracks and
being singularly free from pains inside at the
time. What with the triplet or no triplet,
nigh-gear or low-gear, one sort of track or
another sort of track, “true form” or colly-
wobbles, it will be difficult to take an intelli-
gent interest in cycling, such as one takes in
other sports whose champions don’t come and
go like shadows.

Imperious Fashion
Ordains that the summer season of 1895 is ended.
Social life, no less than climatological phenomena,
marks the changes of the seasons. The winter
season is Fashion’s Carnival; the summer its
period of comparative respite. The autumn and
winter seasons are the periods for indoor merry-
makings, festivities and functions, all of which
necessitate much more “ dressing ” (speaking
technically) than the out-of-door recreations of the
hot months. Howbeit, each change of season
brings its monster “clearing sales.” Surplus
stocks must be quitted to make room for the new
importations. This is the occasion of the startling
bargains now obtainable at Manning and Co.’s,
Collins-street, Melbourne. The cream of this
fashionable firm’s stock of Millinery, Costumes,
Fancy Dresses, Silks, &c., &c., is now being posi-
tively sacrificed to make room for new goods.
Ladies who cannot call in person are invited to
forward orders by post with a full description of
the goods they require. All favors by post, from
any part of Australasia, meet with the promptest
attention of Manning & Co., on the Block,
Collins-street, Melbourne.*

Sydney,
January 7, 1896.
My Dear Moorabtnda—
“ John-a-Dreams ” drew a good house to Sydney
Lyceum last Saturday, and the most gaped-at
woman in front was Mrs. Brough in a black gown
cut square with jet round what dressmakers call
the “decolletage,” and gauze sleeves; a diamond
star at her neck. Lively Emma Temple accom-
panied her and wore black, too, with a cluster of
crimson flowers on the port side. It’s an odd
thing that an actress always spends her night off
at the theatre —j ust as a waiter al ways goes to haunt
a friend still flying round with the chops, when he
has a holiday. In “John-a-Dreams ” sartorial
honors are even between Mrs. Wanklyn (Hilda
Spong, who returns in all her pristine slimness
and doesn’t steer herself along with her elbows so
jerkily as of yore) and Kate Cloud, Miss Beryl
Faber. By the way, Kate’s regeneration from
her dowdy past is one of the coinpletest phoenix-
ings ever seen on any stage, and makes it seem
almost impossible that so much deep-seated purity
and old-established virtue could ever have done
those things they didn’t oughter have done. Miss
Faber can’t help her air of superhuman goodness,
but it makes her confession in a virginal white
gown sound like baseless hysteria. Why doesn’t
she sew a black hem round her spotless skirts to
help the public imagination a little?
In the first act Kate (under a) Cloud wears
yellow brocade with big sleeves and a soft drapery
of lace about the bodice, which is brightened at the
neck with spring green and has glittering sequin
trimmings ; in the second, she wears white, while
Miss Spong sports an ideal cycling costume (it is a
shooting-suit in her case) of brown heather tweed
with short skirts and tan boots, a well-fitting coat
and vest, and a Tam-o’-Shanter with an edge of
peacock-blue. In the third act, Hilda wears a
very pretty silver-grey. skirt, with a violet blouse
covered in grey chiffon, small grey bonnet trimmed
•with pansies. Miss Faber also wears a grey skirt
with a black satin brocade blouse. It is in this
act, when the two men are fighting over her, that
Kate suggestively remarks, “ I II wander down to
the church.” As both men wait to marry her,
this sounds like a pretty broad hint for one of
them.
Cecil "Ward, as the bold, bad baronet, is the
first villain I have ever seen smoke a pipe.
Hitherto on the stage a pipe meant high moral
rectitude, and the villain always fused a cigarette.
Also, Cecil’s villain does most of his diabolical
plotting in a sac suit and a hard-boiled round hat
—he is indeed a novelty. Titheradge doesn’t
exactly look like an opium-eater, nor does he very
much resemble his ancestor on the wall ; John-
a-Dreams is not his best part by long chalks. The
new scheme of lighting at Sydney Lyceum seems
certainly kinder to the complexions of the stage
ladies, for the stage-light is more refined and
dimmer than usual, and if the new semi-illumina-
tion catches on actresses can go back to printed
calico for brocade, and glazed lining for French
silks, as in the good old days, and nobody except
the first row in the stalls will be any the wiser. I
thought the dim after-dinner light on the yacht in
the first act was excellent—indeed, the only draw-
back to the intense realism that prevailed aboard
the Moonbeam was the energetic sailor in the
fourth act, who hung on to the wheel like grim
death although the anchor wasn’t shipped.
On dit that Williamson and Musgrove will not
renew their lease of Sydney Lyceum, which ex-
pires with the year, but will do as other companies
do —simply pop in a” 1 out of whatever playhouse
suits ’em—rumor h... ug it that their first love,
the good old Royal, which in the past carried
more fleas to the square inch than most theatres,
will be their principal scene of action. Of late
the gorgeous Lyceum has been lite,rally eating its
head off. The B. and B. Co. gave up the long-
lease system years ago.
The annual dance held last Friday by the Moss
Vale Lawn Tennis Club in the local “ new hall ”
new to the extent of smelling new—was a cheerful
affair. The decorations were done very neatly by
the committee, which also took the supper in hand
with tlie very best results. The floor was even,
and as Mons. Kowalski, who was present, highly
approved of the dance-music played by the P.A.
/ pnd, there was no flaw in the mounting. Gov.
Hampden was too tired, or bored, or something,
to turn up, and the silver lining of his absence was
that it saved the committee all the wear and tear
of erecting a dais ; and so many glittering mili-
taires —with short red mess jackets and long,
arrowy legs with a tinsel stripe all the way down—
were spotted about the room that an unadorned
Fersonage like H.E. was not missed spectacularly.
t is very amusing to see the way some horse
soldiers walk a ballroom —under protest, as it
were, and apparently in splints, and it appears
to be correct f or them to hold their hands as if the
last four buttons on their weskits were missing
and they wanted airily to conceal the discrepancy.
But there is one bad habit not common to Captain
Gingah, and that is sliding his hands into his
pockets. Mars holds himself up by his sword-hilt
if he feels he musi be supported, though a side-
long glance at his epaulettes is a wonderful
reviver.
The dresses were on all fours with the rest of
the show. Mrs. Scrcggie (wife of one of the hon.
secs.) wore creamy gauze over silk, and moonshine-
colored fixings on tie bodice; Miss Richardson
(feminine hon. sec.) wi sin sunset pink with butter-
fly sleeves and a charming necklace of pink roses ;
Miss Bessie Tarrant (who shortly goeth ’Ome to
be married), wore pale eau-de-nil satin with pink
sleeves; a medico’s wife presented a blushing
appearance in pink brocade ; a wealthy widow
fittingly wore yellow, the exact tint of Australian
gold ; and a plump milit;. ry matron in black with
big yellow sleeves evidernly didn’t put much faith
in the pocket Ezekiel’s dismal foreboding, “Woe
to the women who sew pillows to all armholes ! ”
The ever-fashionable Bong-Bong, or Bong-Tong,
races are billed to eventuate on the 16th and 17th
instants at the picnic race-course. The race-ball
happens on 17th, and with the Governor to be
there as large as life, will, as usual, be the most
important society fixture of the recuperating
season.
From W. H. Paling and Co. I have received
Aug. W. Juncker’s latest song, “Star of the
East,” words by Russell Rogers, and dedicated to
“my friend, Mr. Phillip Newbury.” “Star of
the East ” is not labelled sacred, but it is so full
of “glory to God ” that the person who loves to
make Sunday hideous with song cannot do better
than buy “Star of the East” at once. It is a
good, chest-expanding melody of the “ Jerusalem ”
stamp, and makes up into a most effective warble.
From the French Musical Instrument Depot
comes Mons. Kowalski’s charming melody, “Twi-
light of Love,” words by Gilbert Parker. “ Twi-
light of Love ” is the mezzo-soprano song that
made such a good effect at the production of
Kowalski’s oratorio, “Future Life,” on Xmas
eve. It is published with both ’cello and violin
obligati. From Gordon and Gotch comes a very
lively-looking “Australian music portfolio” (by
the way, one hardly sees where the “Australian ”
comes in) with a mob of gaudy masqueraders on
the cover and a good lot of things inside : “The
Diver,” “ To Anthea,” “Quand tu chantes,” “ O,
Rest in the Lord,” with the plaintive “Valerie
Waltz ” and the “ Palotta March” to hold up the
instrumental end of the log. It is really a mar-
vellous shillingsworth.
Medical men are really the least impressionable
of mortals, therefore I am not a bit surprised to
hear that a well-known Sydney medico has taken
the rooms lately the scene of the most sensational
double suicide ever known in Sydney. Neither he
nor his wife shrinks from occupying an apartment
fraught with such gloomy memories. After all,
the experience they once had in China was worse
—when they rented an alleged haunted house, in
which the gore of a murdered Parsee still lent a
rich tone to the floor of the front parlor. But
they merely threw rugs over this engaging sou-
venir of the former tenant, and forgot all about it.
“Pat,” at Sydney Royal, is a rorty sort of
utterly irresponsible whirligig, which principally
deserves praise because it keeps cutting off short
at the meter the dreadful Irish hero with no
braces on, and substituting a gay and gladsome
ballet for his shamrock-scented maundering*.
How nice it would be if everybody followed suit,
and, when the heroine came on with her hair
down, shoo’d her off in favor of a pas de quatre !
Minna Phillips sings sweetly, and looks very en-
gaging as a phenomenal colleen with golden hair.
Most stage-colleens look as if they stood on end in
a pot of blacking, but Ould Oireland evidently
knows how to turn out a blonde now and then.
Florrie Forde, as a Gaiety Girl, wasn’t quite so
screamingly dressed as Maud Hobson, but her
mauve chiffony frock only wanted a few more
yards in the skirt; and her blue silk with a black
lace flounce and things embroidered in gold was
festive. In the Gaiety, girls are dressed with that
wonderful playing into one another’s hands so
common behind the footlights, where you never
see Tottie in green trying to “kill ’ Flossie in
blue—not among the chorus, at least. Miss
Beaney Gellatly’s high-kicking is a beautiful
thing of its kind, and if the “ Statute Nymph ”
rather suggests that “Beaney’’has two or three
artificial legs concealed among her overwhelming
black and red skirts, it is a graceful show for all
that. Harrie Ireland looms large and rather heavy
as a Society widow, and Mr. Westmacott, one of
the best-dressed men on the local stage, also turns
out to be a comic vocalist of the deepest dye.
“ Pat ” is brightly and effectively staged.
An English duchess has lately been giving out
her views on those amateur attempts at nuns,
Anglican deaconesses, u horn I always look ujion
as so many possible bigamists, since they wear the
ring which marries ’em to the Deity (so the cant
goes), and yet take no vows of earthly celibacy,
but are liable to go off at any moment with their
most moneyed admirer. The Duchess got home
with her left on the subject of foreign missions
in the course of her remarks: “ Women could
always be found to act as missionaries to the
heathen . . . yet for more prosaic . . .
duties of work among our own poor sufficient
volunteers were not forthcoming, especially from
the Church of England.” And when the Anglican
sisters do consent to stop at home and do a little
slumming, they put in most of the time in decid-
ing what length their veils ought to be, or whether
it wouldn’t be a good ad. to wear a crown of
thorns round their bonnets in the Autumn ! The
only way out of the difficulty would be to make it
one of the rules of the game that no nation shall
christianise itself. Thus if we send missionaries
to jolly Japan, Yum Yum will have to come here
with a portable Joss-house and return the compli-
ment.
W hich reminds me that a pretty choristeress in
a leading Australian church has been getting a
good ad. in religious circles by expressing her firm
detenniuation to go to China and drag Ah Sin
into the narrow path of Christianity by his little
pigtail. She was one of the prettiest girls in the
choir, too; so first the parson tackled her and
pointed out in a solemn voice that her first duty
was to her aged mother, or some poetic relative of
the sort, and that it was plainly her thorny path
to remain like a rose at the head of the band.
After that, the wardens got on her trail and
pointed out how the yellow heathen is a lot more
religious than you’d think, much more so than
her own back alley ; and after the war-
dens, the organist and the curate took a hand
in dissuading her. Consequently, the whole
church hung breathlessly on her decision (she
really was a pretty girl), and when at length she
said that she wouldn t go to China —well, then
the parson’s wife got red in the face and openly
remarked that she was a designing little cat.
A Sydney surveyor had an amusing experience
the other day. He and his assistants were
measuring a rather dubious street with the pretty,
bright silver tape common to the process, when a
lady stepped up to the best-looking assistant and
said, “Fardonme, but what do you pay a yard
for that?” The surveyor named the high price
necessary, and the lady appeared discomfited.
“ Uh, so dear as that? I only wanted some to
make a bustle! ” and with a radiant smile she
passed on. Now isn’t that a woman all over ?
Everything subservient to dress. She only looks
upon the stars as so many effective buttons run-
ning to waste. Like a girl I saw, the other day,
looking at a mountain waterfall blown by the
wind. After a breathless period of gazing out
came the truly feminine remark : “ What a lovely
bridal-veil that would make ! ”
Lord Hampden’s speech at the Hielan’ Gather-
ing on New Year’s Day, ye ken, left much to be
deprecated from a fashionable standpoint—l refer
to that part where he alluded to not being proud
of his ancestors who got hanged for poaching.
Remarks of that kind are quite uncalled for in
Australia, where everybody’s grandfather didn’t
die in his bed either. Gov. H. could have won
local renown by saying that the ecstacy he derived
from contemplating that hanged ancestor must
ever remain among the proudest emotions of his
life (wild cheers from Potts Point).
My Melb. correspondent writes :
“Roland Cunningham is for the moment a
‘ ladies’ idol ’in the .London world. One gushful
society paper speaks of him as the handsomest
tenor on tlie English stage. Melb. folk who have
seen ‘ Roley ’ lately say he certainly has improved
vastly. Always good-looking, he has now lost tlie
namby-pamby giriy expression which characterised
him when in Melb., and his voice has gained much
in strength and power.
“ A recently-imported Age man sets the fashions
in the Melb. press world. He wears ties har-
monising with the rest of his attire, pink limp
shirts and sensitive pants which turn up sympa-
thetically at the hems when it rains in London.
Apart from this beauteous youth, Age men are
proverbially shabby—as were Akgus men until
lately, when, fired by the noble example of the
aforementioned youth, almost the whole staff
blossomed out into top-hats, ‘ street-sweepers’and
patent-leather boots.
“ A dozen years ago a Melb. merchant died
leaving his widow penniless. Having some skill
(or rather experience) as a palmist and futurist,
she set up in a suburban main street. Last year
she had amassed enough by half-crown fortune-
telling to trip to England and the Continent. She
returned after a year’s absence, and continues to
thrive at her ‘ profession. ’
“ A crematorium is to be erected in St. Kilda
cemetery. As the cemetery is situated in the
midst of a thickly-populated suburb, the fumes on
“ cooking ” days will be obnoxious to the residents,
who are already petitioning the authorities.
Opposite the cemetery is a mansion prophetically
called ‘ Closeburn.’
“A brilliant and fascinating poster of Meunier’s
decorates Melb. hoardings.' The subject is the
New Woman —an audacious little minx with long,
narrow, Trilby feet, and a ravishing mouth, who
glances wickedly at passers-by out of half-closed,
heavily-lidded eyes.
“The Brasseys are the most desperately ener-
getic governors Victoria has yet had. They rise
with the sun and speed round St. Kilda beach on
their bicycles—they rush back to breakfast, then
off again to committee meetings, opening of things,
morning calls —anything, just to put in a good
day’s work. They are essentially of the fnend-
and-acquaintance-making order. You hear of
them at breakfast in one big Toorakian house,
at lunch in another, or dropping in promiscuously
to afternoon tea. They can tbe accused, either, of
putting on airs. If their own carriage isn’t ready
—well, the other day, H.E. was seen wildly rush-
ing out of his own gates, racket in hand, to catch
a passing tram. This delights the ordinary tram-
riding 1 populace. It may be a big bid for popu-
larity, but it’s a very homely and harmless one.
The Brasseys take local society as they find it.
The Mars-Buckley lot have captured them just as
completely as they captured Lord Hopetoun ; but
so have some other people as well, at whom
even Lady Loch, the first to begin this sort of
promiscuousness, drew the line. The danger the
Brasseys run is that of having no standard at all.
“Since Bradfield and his songs have left it is
the fashion in Melb. society for conversation to be
as extremely risky as possible. A story is about
concerning a dashing widow whose accomplish-
ments in the way of Equivoque and double-entente
have taken full advantage of this license. A
newly-imported johnny had met her at a garden-
party, and after an afternoon with her and others
of her set came away marvelling at ‘ Colonial
Society.’ ‘I thought you’d draw —haw—the line
somewheah,’ he remarked in a general way to a
‘ fwend ’ who had no clue to the johnny’s mistake.
The next afternoon johnny drove to the fair
widow’s villa and was further astounded at the
style and surroundings. Then, primed with the
fizz he had taken, he awaited her. Presently she
entered—and no one knows exactly what occurred,
except that shortly after a crushed and bewildered
and very sobered Englishman was tottering down
the path to his cab. Since'then the widow’s con-
versation has been pruned and prismed except
where she is safe among intimates.
“ In ‘ Djin Djin ’—the record show up to date at
Melb. Princess’s—Mr. Barnes’s dresses are among
the chief attractions for the women. It is safe to
prophesy that Flo. Young’s magpie dress, or her
brown and crimson get-up as Prince Eucalyptus,
will be seen on more than one young person with
shapely limbs, at the next winter fancy-dress ball.
Flo. Young looks her best as a boy. Some,
though, are simply wild over Flora Graupner’s
hand-painted wedding-dress—ivory satin, with
blue and purple irises; being tiny sne looks appro-
priately like a Japanese doll.

“ John-a-Dreams,” put up at Sydney
Lyceum on Saturday, is a play which is hard to
locate. It looks a rather good drama when splen-
didly staged and played by an excellent co. ; if it
were staged a little worse and played by an inferior
co. it would probably fall so suddenly that its skull
would crack loudly on the pavement and a crowd
of seedy men would gather round to see the corpse.
There is distinctly less in it than in “Captain
Swift” or the “Idler.” Two young men—a
dreamer who takes opium and gazes into the sea,
and a moderately bad young baronet —have made
a vague, unstamped agreement to be always loyal
to each other. They call this “ the Oxford com-
pact,” and drink to the health of it considerably.
Then they both fall in love with a girl who has a
“ Past ” of the usual monotonous kind ; if that
girl had been gaoled for stealing, or had committed
murder or burglary in her childhood’s hours, it
would at least be more original. The girl rejects
the baronet, and the dreamer sits up all night and
indites a poem to her on two sheets of paper—
which is a very long grind for a very small poem.
Also, he tells his old clerical father about the
opium, and, when he is still staggering under the
revelation, the girl tells him about her Past.
After that there is more maundering over the
Oxford compact, and the baronet drugs the poetic
dreamer in an impossible fashion and tries to carry
off the girl in a yacht, but, being retarded by bad
weather, he is overtaken and nothing very definite
happens, except that it is generally understood
the dreamer and the girl will get mar wed shortly
with the consent and blessing of the old, shocked
clerical parent.
* * * «
The dialogue of “ John-a-Dreams ” is nof
especially brilliant, and the humor is somewhat
scarce, and though the drama is neatly con
structed there is none too much meat upon it. A
lot of the things that might be expected don’t
really happen. The girl’s Past doesn’t rise up
against her. The opium-dreamer doesn’t see hor
rible visions of crocodiles and worms. His poen:
leads to nothing. A certain estranged couple whe
are supposed to be funny have apparently quar
relied about nothing in particular, and they
get partially reconciled for no very definite
reason. Titheradge and Boucicault show to
moderate advantage in two characters which
are not large enough to hold them. Miss Fabei
plays the girl with the Past prettily and success-
fully, and the two strongest characters are Cecil
Ward as the bad baronet and Granville Blake
who makes a big, muscular success as Hilda
Spong’s “Johnny” husband and the man who
dismisses Hilda’s lover in a shattered condition.
A very unusual and beautiful scene on board a
yacht, with the big mainsail shaking in the breeze,
and the cool green moonlit ocean in the back-
ground, does as much as anything else to help
“ John-a-Dreams ” along to its unspecified desti-
nation.
** * *

very hottest spell of the year. What the public
wanted just then was something cool with a lump

Bicycling Hi Hot Weather.
All riders know what a delight it is to enjoy a
smoke as they go pedalling along, but during the
hot weather a pipe is too heavy and a cigarette
too enervating. Most cyclists are now smoking
ouly Frossard’s Cavour Cigars,“which, from their
delicious fragrance and freedom from nicotine,
make them the ideal wheelman’s weed. Although
the nicotine is extracted from Frossard’s Cavour
Cigars “in vacuo,” they retain all the qualities
desired in a first-class cigar. The finest riders of
the day indulge in nothing else, amongst them
Pouvet, the French champion, who swears by
them and recommends them to all riders. We dc
likewise, and in wishing you many a pleasant spin
during this new year, we hope that you will take
our advice and smoke only Frossard’s Cavour
Cigars. Mild and fragrant. Obtainable every-
where in packets of 8 for ls, #
of ice in it, and the spectacle of a hot black nigger
being walloped by his infuriated owner was not
appropriate. In any case, “ Uncle Torn ”is a
play which has seen its best days, and Little Eva’s
entry into paradise isn’t taken seriously now, and
when the heavens open after Tom’s decease and
show the long flight of steps at Lavender Bay
jetty all polished with Pears’ soap and lined with
angels, the public naturally expects to see Geo.
Lean steering the ferry-boat into the picture. The
Pollard version of the drama, however, is pro-
bably intended to be taken in a light and airy
spirit. There is much small nigger, and dog, and
comic song, and breakdown and Virginia reel in
it; and Marks, the comic lawyer with the aged
umbrella, pervades it all through ; and, as the
small nigger and the dog and Marks take up so
much space, Little Eva has to sicken and gradually
fade away and die of slow consumption all in 10
minutes, and almost all the beautiful remarks
she makes on her death-bed are crowded out.
Ihe conversion of Topsy is also cut out, and
Uncle Tom is hurriedly walloped at 10.30 and
dies almost instantaneously. This airy way of
treating the business makes the lugubrious drama
almost cheerful, and doesn’t harrow up the audi-
ence in the old, dreadful fashion.
* • * •
One illogical feature in almost all versions
of “ Uncle Tom ” is the extreme old age of the
hero. As a rule, he starts in the first act as a bald
and rheumatic nigger with a white patch of wool
above his ear—a nigger so obviously only fit to be
boiled-do n tli it the large prices paid for him are
ridiculous. By the time he comes into Legree’s
possession he is a doddering cripple, without a leg
to stand upon. Despite all discrepancies, however,
the co. played the piece throughout remarkably
well, the youthful Quealy, as Marks, being especi-
ally noticeable as a clever comedian. And by the
time this appears in print the juveniles will have
wisely gone back into comic opera, which is their
strong point. To-night (Thursday) “ Cloches de
Corneville ” will be produced (n.v.), and if D.
continues to V., “ Girofle-Girofla ” will follow on
Friday, and “Pirates of Penzance” on Saturday,
with “ Madame Fa vert ” and “ Ermime ” in
reserve.
** * *
Her Majesty’s pantomime continues a
highly profitable spec., and it has brightened up
considerably of late. It goes faster than it did,
and Friday and Saturday have got a strong hold
on the audience, and the public is gradually being
educated up to Marie Luella’s legs, which can
only be fully appreciated by a cultivated taste.
The suicidal person who cast himself out of the
gallery is nearly recovered now, though, with the
usual inverted cussedness of things, the two
people he fell upon are both far from well. He
was a good advt. for the pantomime, however,
and he might easily have thrown himself over in a
worse cause.
“Pat’’goes on his misguided Irish way
at Sydney Royal, and his painful resemblance to
every other Irish drama still continues. He is as
purposeless and as incoherent as he was at the
beginning, and the reason of his existence remains
a mystery which will not be solved until the ocean
gives up its dead and their ghosts drip solemnly in
long rows on the sad sea-shorp.
