Mr. George Bull, of the Australian Star,
a well-known and much-esteemed Sydney press-
man, who is departing shortly for the foggy land
of his brother John, is to have a m itinee benefit
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) on Friday of this
week. The Criterion, Royal, Tivoli, and Her
Majesty s companies all take part in the pro-
gramme, and Premier Reid, General Hutton, and
other mighty persons lend their patronage and
moral support to the proceedings.
* * #
The two music halls have done nothing
very special this week. Lawler s establishment in
George-street is still cheered by the presence of
the mirthful Benham. The Tivoli announces
the last nights of Alice Leamar, whose absence
will create a serious void, unless she can be re-
engaged with great difficulty at enormous expense,
and induced to shed the light of her countenance
on the scene a little longer.
The joyous magnificence of W. and M.’s
panto, at Melb. Princess’s is just now employed in
making itself talked about. The first audiences,
expecting nothing remarkable, were so dazed by
“ Djin-Djin ” that they went home with only half
an idea of the total effect. Then their uncles,
cousins and aunts came along, prepared to see
something unusual, and therefore better able to
appreciate the spectacle at all points. The Firm’s
latest effort invites criticism, and will fatten on it.
Artists Gordon and Goatcher painted the scenery
bet ween them, John Wallace arranged the order
of things, and manager Williamson drew up the
‘ < k* 1 campaign. Also, Bert Royle devised the
book, ’and Leon Caron made the musical stew.
As for Coleman’s valuable legs, they grew upon
him several years ago.

Santa Claus,” in conjunction with “ The
House that Jack Built" and a ramshackle giant
ot loose principles, is flourishing at the Royal,
banta Claus ” has not yet earned The Bulletin’s
earty blessing, but it shows a disposition to
reform, and Miss Florence Wilton protects it
from strong language in the meantime. The new
ady is vivacious, and her tight-fitting rationals

ally himself with Barren—might do much for this
antique pantomime show. Miss Maud Williamson,
who took somebody else’s place as a prince after

her airy summer garments, and adds a touch of im-
portance to the co. Also, the Fire Ballet (new to
Melb.fc is a toothsome suggestion of roast fairy,
not too underdone.

Dan Barry’s dramatic company holds'forth
at Melb. Alexandra. Jlist now the bill
or iare is “ Foiled,” and the hero is sentenced to be
sawn asunder by Augustus Glover, an actor whose
hne broad style of wickedness and large rolling
eyes and voice qualify him for the job. Augustus
has several intended victims in the melodrama
under notice, but luck is against him and lie dies
without one deliberate murder to his credit. At
the end of the first act he surprises an Australian
into dropping dead of heart-disease, otherwise his
career ranks as a failure. Miss Lora Mostyn foils
him all the while. Dora is a black tracker with a
prejudice against Augustus because he cut her
tongue out in days of yore, arid every time he
goes to commit a capital offence the dumb
heathen follows his footmarks across country and
blights his scheme. “Foiled ” would be a better
melodrama if Augustus were allowed to attain his
end occasionally. The story has a well-defined
commencement and some strength of character
until it degenerates into a frantic grab at new
horrors. The circular-saw sensation and the in-
cident of hurling a female off a three-foot cliff do
more harm than good to Foiled.” The manage-
ment should furnish Mr. A. Glover with a meat-
axe, and allow him to do a little plain and fancy
mutilation in place of the absurdly complicated
atrocities which come so utterly to grief. Some
stirring acting by Glover, J. P. O'Neill, and Miss
Duggan is slunk into the evening’s entertainment.

appear at the Opera House on Saturday (11th)
with a new lot of songs acquired during his recent
travels in far lands. On the same evening Miss
Alice Leamar, late of Sydney, will appear for the
first time with the Melb. co. The joint arrival of
these two favorites promises to be the cheerf idlest
thing that has happened to the Opera House for a
very long time.

Lucy Escott’s demise is wired from Paris. Escott and
Squires did not marry until near the end of their Aus-
tralian professional life, wherefore they lived happy ever
after. Squires was fraudy, a pale little fat man, with an
inevitable falsetto ; and Escott only had the rags of her
voice, even when she came, but was a splendid actress and
remarkably fine woman.

a bland stranger began to chat with him. Says our
friend : “ Excuse me, sir, I am listening to the play.”
Afterwards he found he had snubbed Longfellow.

“ Prof.” Kennedy had another turn at the hypnotic
sleep business in Adelaide, and this time Frederick Keat-
ing, the subject, was sent off for eight days and nights.
Kennedy says this is a record. Keating, by the way, is
the man who was put to sleep for six days by Kennedy in
Melb. Kennedy “is the only man who can awake his
subjects thus hypnotised,” and people are asking what
will happen if, in the middle of one of these experiments,
the Professor gets run over by a tramcar, or something.
Apropos Thomas Kennedy and mesmerism. The writer
knew a splendid “subject” some years ago—an amateur
actor and very impressionable. He was the best subject
ofaS.A. lecturer on mesmerism, and went everywhere
with him. Suddenly the young fellow went mad, and for
several months was on the verge of death, but eventually
he grew better, although he can never fully regain his
health. The lecturer—then and still a well-known busi-
ness man—gave up lecturing and now never refers to the
subject.
“The solicitors of the families of both Arthur Dacre
and Amy Roselle,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “persist in their denial that either of the un-
fortunate mummers could have been in want of money.
What a mockery to say they • could have had what they
wanted,’ when Dacre was full of indignation, even before
leaving for Australia, at the want of sympathy, and the
refusal to help them when they had even gone so far as
to sell off all they possessed to provide for their wants ! ”
All the English papers seem agreed that Arthur Dacre
was the sole cause of the unhappy couple’s misfortunes.
Before they left, Dacre refused £3O a week engage-
ments for Amy Roselle. “Both or none,” was his
invariable answer. Only once was the wife permitted to
act alone, and she gave up after the first week, as while
she was at the theatre her husband sat moping at home in
a dreadful state of suspense and melancholy.
Dear Bulletin, —In last week’s Poverty Point there is a
suggestion that Ada Colley should have her voice sent to
Europe to be trained. Sarcasm, surely. It isn’t so much
training that Miss Colley needs, so far, but voice. Little
material to work upon, little sweetness or power, or any-
thing in Miss Colley’s singing except a clever trill and
skilful production of falsetto trick notes. Hers is one of
the lightest and least musical light soprano voices that
ever knocked an uneducated audience, but it is exceed-
ingly well-trained for its music-hall purpose.— Mark Hazy.
A London man recalls a Dacre story told by Arthur
Dacre himself. A provincial manager was notorious for
providing ill-furnished and insanitary dressing-rooms.
Dacre complained in vain. One evening the manager
}vas in a terrible state. “ Dacre,” he said, “ that
iron bar across your room fell down this evening and gave
your dresser such a blow that I had to send him to° the
hospital.” “By Jove,” said Dacre, “ I’m terribly sorry to
hear that.” “ Yes,” said the manager, “and it’s only by
God’s mercy that the bedroom jug and basin were not
dashed to atoms.”
The young man who gushed in Melb. Age over “ Djin-
Djin,” foamed all over Williamson’s shirt-front on account
of the alleged newness of the idea. It was time all the
well-worn panto, themes, such as “ Crusoe,” “ Sindbad,”
etc., were dead and buried, and so on. But a more
cautious youth, who was sent to scribble about “ Santa
Claus” at the Royal, gushed in praise of Coppin for avoid-
ing all new-fangled notions and keeping to the good old
tried lines.

in “ I don't want to play in your yard ” (Melb. Princess’s),

circs. Little Ivy is practically a stage veteran.
The earthquake in Melb. Princess’s panto, is derived
from an Am -ricaii drama of long ago. Manager William-
son has been saving up this spectacular effect for the last
20 years, awaiting a favorable opportunity to slip it into a
show. “ Djin-Djin ” was the opportunity, and the earth-
quake is realising J.C.W.’s fondest hopes.

“ Trilby,” produced by the bright, deserving little woman,
has no humor, satire or fun in it.

Miss Fortescue (the lady who got £lO,OOO as a solatium
for her wounded affections from the late Lord Garmoyle)
was, on the whole, a bad failure in South Africa. The
South African Review on her Juliet: “Miss Fortescue
manifests tender emotions by a sing-song whine, rage
with the rasping gutturals of a virago, and sought to
arouse recognition of the critical situation in a climax by
bellowing gibberish which was not recognisable as the
text. If costumes could have done it, Miss Fortescue
would have illustrated a reason why Juliet’s father wished
her to wed a wealthy suitor at short notice. But thsre
is more required for the part than an ability to carry
fantastic millinery with the art it demands. More than
this Miss Fortescue has not got ” The Standard
and Diggers’ News contends that most people in the For-
tescue Co. play tolerably, with the glaring exception of
the leading lady. “ Melodramatic ‘ mouthing ’ and rant-
ing and grimacing and turning up the whites of her eyes
and elocuting in an unpleasantly stilted and -wooden
fashion isn’t acting.” Interviewed by the Press, Miss
Fortescue said that Johannesburg was an odious town.
“ What / call living, is passing your time profitably with
dignity to body and mind. Can you do that in Johannes-
burg ? To lead a healthy life you require to be surrounded
by ,a certain sort of cleanliness of body and soul. In
Johannesburg you can not.” Too true. But how beauti-
ful (remarks the paper) Johannesburg would have been
had not Miss Fortescue’s performances been the most
wretched failure on record 1
The Bourke (N.S.W.) Anglican church and its ad
joining hall were burnt to the ground on Boxing Day.
The hall had turned in many an honest penny to the
preachers from quadrille parties and travelling theatricals,
and nasty people whisper that the place was struck by
the Almighty’s hand for having been used for worldly
pleasures. Some 16 swaggies helped the scanty fire
brigade in its at tempt the extinguish the flames, writes an
unregenerate Bulletin correspondent. The day was
terribly hot, but the swaggies worked with a wiil, and
saved much furniture. The men were all hard up, and
some of them hadn't even had a feed on the day when
“ peace and happiness shall reign upon earth,” But they
didn’t get a meal or a drink, or even “ thanks” for their
labor.
Only Melbourners can understand why Williamson
forbade Elton’s gag, in “ Djin Djin,” on Leonora Orelli’s
appearance. “Here is the Orelli’s sweet strain.” The
underlying reference is to the Reilly-street drain, and, of
course, Orelli was the first to scent it.

J. F. Robin, one of Rickards’ new people now appearing
at Melb. Opera House, seems to travel chiefly on his hearty
laugh, or cackle. Robin reminds one, in a small way, of
Jolly Nash, the fat man who shook his sides in Australia
for a while during the “ eighties.” Nash-he was among
the early fathers of London music ’all entertainment
lived, and lived well, by his infectious laughing songs for
thirty years, and may still be flourishing at the old
“ wholesome funny ” business. The jolly one’s cachinna-
tions were works of art in their way.

medium, Madame Eusapia Palladino, is a graduate of Melb.

the Psychical Research Society. Madame had previously

Coleman, the eccentric dancer now agitating his legs at
Melb. Princess’s, is wont to faint on the smallest provoca-
tion. “Tiny” has won a reputation for wisdom by
posing as a preternaturally-solemn man.
Leoncavallo’s new opera—a musical story of Thomas
Chatterton, the boy poet and suicide—will be produced in
Italy at Easter. Great things are expected of “ Chatter-
ton,” which will probably set the musical world talking
about the hungry young genius who wipe ! himself out
with a dose of poison 125 years ago.
Miss Gerrard, the latest and least of Melbourne singing
girls to be sent ’Ome, writes the usual letter re her voice.
Santley, she says, has expressed himself as very much
pleased with her organ of sound, and “ the improvement
already noticeable in her singing.” The damsel left for
London six months, or less, ago, and after getting there
she was obliged to run away to Scotland to recruit her
health, she says. She made a “lengthy sojourn” in
Scotland. So the journey ’Ome, the loss of health, the
lengthy sojourn aforesaid, and a reasonable time for
making a noticeable improvement in her singing, have all
happened between the dates of her leaving Melbourne
and her letter to hand last week. Miss Gerrard has
faith in herself, but that’s not much use to the pos-
sessor of a thin voice and weak chest. One of Florence
Young’s best notes would blow her off her feet.
The average mummer in these cold hard days seems to
divide his life into two parts ; three fourths of the time he
is out of a job, and the other fourth he is being secured
with great difficulty at enormous expense.

Mr. It. G. Vallack, head of the big Sydney
softgoods firm of Gardiner and Co., suddenly died
last week of heart-disease, aged 65. Mr. Vallack
was a level-headed man of business who eschewed
parliamentary and civic glory ; consequently,
there are “no flies ” on the firm of Gardiner and
Co.
Private business worries, as well as shocks
to his democratic system, have told rather severely
upon Dr. Maloney, M.L.A., so the little member
for W. Melbourne is just now seeking a nerve
tonic in a trip to India. Being an object of Tory
scorn and Argus derision, this dapper Republican
possesses charms for The Bulletin, hence his
portrait on page 13. It has been alleged by the
enemy that Dr. Maloney went to India with a
view to joining one Geismardo, alias Professor
Richards, a somewhat shady “electric-healer,”
in whose system of treatment he sincerely
believes without regard to the healer’s ante-
cedents. As a matter of fact, he didn’t know
of Richards’ whereabouts at the time the journey
was decided upon, since when the wily Prof, has,
without authority, announced the coming of his
former champion to back him up. Meanwhile,
Dr. Maloney desires to heal himself with briny
breezes and change of scene. The Vic. Assembly
could have better spared many a cooler-headed
politician, and The Bulletin hopes to see Maloney
back again in his old fighting form as soon as
possible.

(Now in fashion.)
’Twas a hatful of tricks
That the milliner knew
And I missed the high-kicks
Through her hatful of tricks-
Tliere were sunflowers six,
And a stuffed cockatoo,
In the hatful of tricks
That the milliner knew.
T.D.

The Giver of all (food things
To grant new life to Nellie’s leys,
Which now are same as wood things.
Both paralysed in middle age !
Alas ! ’twas not surprising :
They danced together on the stage,
And each was paralysing.

SOCIETY
At a recent Melb. Caledonian dinner one
M‘Gregor remarked that no country was governed
well unless Scotchmen were at the head of affairs.
“ Scotchmen,” said he, “ruled in Victoria when
things were going well, and Victoria would never
be again in a prosperous condition unless its ad-
ministration were largely entrusted to Scotchmen.
Shades of Munro and Gillies !
The Highland Gathering (20,000), at Syd-
ney Cricket-ground, was a gorgeous display of
kilts, hose, plaids, bonnets, naked knees, tartans,
heather, thistles, and Macs. One Scotchman
wore a .£lOO get-up, and another had wavy black
hair glistening with fusel oil. Gov. Hampden
came to luncheon, and was not properly received
—he was greeted by a mere Maclaurin instead of
by Quong, chief of the clan Mac Tart. H.E.’s
remarks at luncheon were chiefly to the effect that
he wasn’t a Scotchman himself, but one of his
relatives some time back was hung for poaching.

Back block pub. New barmaid, to whom
the boss has given strict orders not to serve any
blacks. Enter blackfellow, who has just seen
the boss ride away. “ Gimme rum, missey.”
“ No, can’t. Boss says not to serve blackfellow.”
“ Oh, me no want ’em for myself—my lubra very
sick, going to have ’em picanniny. She very bad.”
New barmaid, sympathisingly : “ Dear me, I hope
she is getting seen to.” “Oh, no, she very bad—
picanniny bimeby.” New barmaid, deeply
touched, gives King Billy some rum in a bottle,
which he takes with thanks, and is leaving when
New Barmaid (loq.) : “ When do you think the
baby will come ? ” King Billy : “ Oh, bimeby—
long, long time yet. Me only got married yester-
day.”

The newest device practised by ladies in
I otfls, it is alleged, is to leave a pair of No. 1
) oots outside the bedroom-door to be cleaned,
while the owner surreptitiously polishes her own
No. 5 hoof-cases inside with a bottle of blacking
which she carries in a dressing-case. She also
A DEFINITION.
TEACHER: “What is a mother? ”
CHOURUS: “ The thing what cooks our dinner .”

No. 1 boots and^make them look as if they had

Since Malthus has become the patron
saint of Maoriland women, the aged man who
harangues at the back door has ceased to have 12
young children at home. He has found that even
innocent girls of 18 grow indignant and slam the
door in his face on learning that he has been
unable to work for 10 years, and has nevertheless
added to the population in that period.
Applications were lately invited for the
position of Resident Med. Officer of Adelaide
Hospital at £5OO p.a., and, although many applied,
the Board reported that none of the applicants
were eligible, and asked the Govt, to secure a
medical man from ’Ome. Premier Kingston re-
fused point-blank, and said he would appoint
none but a local man, and as the Board declines to
recommend anyone, there is now another “ Hos-
pital ” difficulty. The Bulletin hopes that by
“ local ” man the Premier doesn’t necessarily mean
an Adelaide man.
A decent old Wesleyan, Parson Wells, died
recently in Melbourne, and the obituarist lifts up
his voice in wailing, and states that he has gone to
join these great and honored associates in the
ministry—Butters, Draper, Binks, Dare, Symons
and Egglestone. Then he assumes a more cheerful
key, and remarks that there still remain to the
Church these indefatigable toilers —Waugh, Iron-
side, Blamires and Watsford. Which is very
consoling, no doubt, but the whole eleven of them
put together are not one-quarter so well-known as
Carbine, or Zimmerman, or even Alf. Josephs, of
Bendigo, and never will be, unless one of them
cements his murdered wife under the hearth-stone.
And in the rush and hurry of this sinful genera-
tion, the obituary of the great Butters and Binks,
deceased, sounds just as unimportant as the news
that a leading cockroach has pegged out in 1 -
kitchen. The irreverent Australian doesn’t even
know that there ever were any Butters and Binks
—and never will know it.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—The representatives of
Her Gracious on the Australian mainland seem to
come more prominently before the public than
those in the Little Island. But at no time in the
history of Tas. have the Governors distinguished
themselves by the performance of praiseworthy
acts. Davy, the first I can remember for the
moment, was a most disreputable swab. Sored
was weak morally, too fond of elevating his
little finger, and is chiefly remembered for his
part in the formation of that hell on earth, Mac-
quarie convict-settlement. Arthur was a grant-
giving, insufferably arrogant autocrat; and poor
old Franklin doddered about the rough scrubs
looking for the North Pole, or something, and did
no harm and very little good. Denison was, per-
haps, a fairly good man in the wrong place, and
was intensely hated by his subordinates. Col.
Gore Brown was a gentleman, with no pomp or
vanity in his composition, but, as a ruler—enough
said. Du Cane was noted for his agricultural-
dinner speeches, and for his lady with her bull-
dogs, but for nothing worse ; whilst Strahan was
a jolly dog, who spent more money during his
term than all the rest put together. Acting-Gov.
Lefroy was painstaking and popular, but, p G

was too good to be made a common hack, he was
withdrawn. Hamilton was nothing but a figure-

figureheads rolled into one, whose aim is fishing,
and whose name is- unknown to thousands of the
population. We import our Governors as we im-
port our stallions, and bulls, and rams, and, if
they turn out failures, we should be able to get rid
of them by shipping them back to the ’Ome they
adore. —C. H. Read.

♦ * * *
A Cairns drunk, while under the influence,
recently, roamed over the Esplanade and, under
the hallucination that lie was a prize bull, made
the night hideous with his roars. Cairns is a
great place for hallucinations. The average
citizen of that sultry port actually believes that a
pigtail and a chop-stick are the arms of Banana-
land.
* * * *
A leading dame, of striking aspect, has
suffered a cruel disappointment. Her ardent
desire was to career round on wheels with the
vice-regal set—to which end she repaired to a
noted cycling teacher in a country town. After a
week’s hard work he told her it was no good—she
fell off on an average five times in as many
minutes. The dame has at last reluctantly come
round to the trainer’s point of view : “To be able
to ride a bicycle you should see straight in front
of you—if for any reason you can’t you had bettei
let it alone.”
* * * *
At this date it would seem that Melb.
tramway tracks were laid down solely for the con-
venience of cyclists, while a few cars are run every
now and then to shelter some of them from the
wind.
* * * *
In a somewhat wild and woolly Gippsland
district, where a Bulletin correspondent spent a
few days recently, most of the competitors entered
for the local New Year’s sports failed to put in an
appearance, and to eke out the bill a handicap
spitting-match was arranged on the ground. The
prize, a wooden pipe and five pounds of plug
tobacco, was offered by a resident storekeeper.
The winner, an expert axeman, spat from scratch,
and expectorated close upon 1 Aft. Each com-
petitor found himself in beer. Feeling ran very
high, and the betting was all in favor of a smal 1
Irishman, who crippled himself by blowing out a
necessary front tooth at the second try.
It is perhaps needful to mention that
this “par.” is not printed altogether
with the idea of popularising a new ,
indigenous form of athletics in Aus-
tralia. The other most interesting
item at the sports mentioned was a
battle between a retriever and a cross-
bred ram for £2 a-side. The dog was
knocked out in 7min., and was not
even accorded decent burial. Then tin-
owner of the dog fought the owner of
the ram for natural love and affection,
and the day’s entertainment concluded
with an attempt by a Comishman to
beat the local beer-drinking record for
ten pints. He finished 2sec. behind
time, and then refused to pay for the
beer as per agreement. Altogether it
was a chaste and elevating exhibition,
and demonstrated satisfactorily that tin-
healthy love of sport characteristic of
the true Australian is invariably equal
to the occasion, and capable of flourish-
ing under the most adverse circum-
stances.

A hungry, exhausted wretch
aauled out of a ship’s hold at Freeman-
tie, W.A., the other day, said he had
stowed away in a boiler, subsequently
covered by 500 tons of cargo,
with three bottles of water and
some bread and meat. He
thought four days would see
Freemantle, but he was cooped
up for a fortnight in total dark-
ness, run over by rats, and
didn’t know when night et in
or day began. The same vesse,
carried 14 other stowaways, ten of whom worked
their way, the rest chancing gaol on arrival.
Dear Bulletin, —You say, “ The most
sacred traditions of Melb. Govt. House are being
ruthlessly upset by the Brasseys, and Wallington
looks on in horror at all sorts of vulgar people,
with whom the Hupetouns would never have
associated, being invited to dinner and luncheon.’'
The fact of the matter is this--Wallington, of
yore, ran the vice-regal show for the particular
benefit of a set —in whom, of course, he sincerely
believed. He invited the guests (for Lady H. had
no friends to speak of), and the boss entertained
them. The Brasseys are disposed to select their
own friends, or acquaintances, instead of leaving
the private secretary to prescribe for them. The
new guests are probably less vulgar and snobbish,
on an average, than the mob that Wallington had
branded Al. The day of sassiety selection by a
long, thin shadow (Agnes Murphy refers to the
Wallington influence in her silly little book) is
ended, and the handy-man has found his official
level. When the Brasseys go to the Princess’s
they spread themselves in the manager’s box, with
the Freeman-Thomases and a lord or two, whilst
Wallington and any other small suite-potatoes
strive to present an imposing appearance in the
stage-box beneath. It is amusing, this new
arrangement, and it works one humorous result.
After Brassey has put in a “command night,"
ex-Acting-Gov. Madden comes along and engages
the manager’s box, that he may keep himself in
Duncan Gillies was a Scotchman,
Duncan “ whustled up ” the Boom :

A PHILOSOPHICAL HUSBAND.
Though my wife has got a lover,
I’m not torn by jealous fears ;
No ! by all the stars above her
It has never caused me tears.
Why should we, Time’s chances wasting,
To old age unloved decline ?
Both of us Life’s sweets are tasting—
She goes her way, I go mine.
(I might mention, ere I’ve done
Why I nurse no jealous elf,
My wife’s ways and mine are one,
And her lover is—myself.)
Melbourne.
R. K. DEE.
THE WANING MOON.
Two in a train, and I;
Two fools, as tho’ no one were by,
Squeezing and snuggling and kissing galore ;
Yearnfully gazing—new-wedded, no doubt
But I couldn’t stand it. I opened the door
And quickly sprang out.
Passed a month, and that couple again
I saw getting into my train,
And I swore “Just my luck!” and felt deuced
annoyed.
But they]—out of opposite windows they craned.
They had tasted Love’s cup, and, of course, it had
cloyed ;
I smiled —and remained.
Lithgow.
YARRAN.
There are statesmen who laugh at our aims,
And others who will not allow
We have any political claims ;
But what do we care for them now —
Seeing how
As we have the support of the Chow,
The manly and liberal Chow ?
The Lords of Creation may sneer
And think us a little bit “ gone
In the head” —they are welcome to jeer—
Our triumph is coming anon,
Marching on,
Encouraged by Cabbagee John,
’Neath the banner of Cabbagee John,
He is not over-healthy, maybe.
His habits are not over-clean,
But what’s that to us —we can see
The way th *■ his tendencies lean.
He’s obscene.
If you like —but how well does he mean !
How exceedingly well does he mean !
Then it’s “ Forward, Quick March ” of the S< x !
We’ve rebelled, after ages of wrong ;
They have trodden too long on our necks,
We shall teach them a different song,
Hot and strong.
John Chinaman leads with his gong,
John Chinaman leads us— Allons !
TWO INTERPRETATIONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Woman’s place is by the fireside as a mothei
and a wife;
Reverentially protected from the evilness of life ;
Mixing not in unclean problems which the loud
new women seek,
Leaving us to lift the fallen and with laws protect
the weak.”
“ Woman’s place is by the fireside, when the fire
is black and dead ;
Woman’s place is in the attic with the snow
wreaths overheard;
By the factory-foreman’s counter, pleading hard
for wages won—
And upon the midnight pavement when the
sweater’s work is done.”
M.L.
C. CLYDE.

* * *
About the largest Xmas-box going was
the one which arbitrator Blake left behind him in
Wellington. When the hearing of the Midland
Railway dispute was finished, Blake wrote out a
voluminous decision and left it sealed up, with a
/ >n w’ l6 outs . lde setting forth that he wanted
A2lOO for his services. The Government and the
co. were both sanguine, but badly scared, and for
five days nobody had courage enough to apply for
YMi'h'imir' 6 dreadful document. There were
Ki,ooo involved; one way the co. would be
burst and the other the Seddon Government
would be ruined beyond repair. Finally, the co’s
solicitor applied for the paper and read it; and
then the Midland Railway syndicate fainted in a
corner, while the Government called wildly for
champagne and threw up its hat and never
saw it again. But all parties had a pleasant
Christmas holiday with the sealed verdict
preying on their minds, and the Midland
Co. lost the one chance of its existence during that
hve days waiting. The general manager and the
solicitor should have burgled the fateful packet in
the dead of night, and finding the contents were
unfavorable, should have replaced it with another
looking exactly the same so far as concerned the
outside, but containing a -and-costs
verdict m favor of the co. Then the largest
debenture-holder should have gone out and secretly
smothered Blake, by shoving him head-first into a
geyser, so that there could be no awkward ex-
planations afterwards, and after that they might
have formally opened the verdict with a light
heart. But they missed the opportunity, and it
will never, never return.

nard bitch, Osborne (imported), by Nero 111., ex Katrine.
It is only last week that we eulogised so favorably this
grand hitch, and we feel certain that the fancy in general
sympathise with Mr. in his heavy loss, which is all the
more severe in consequence of Osborne having recently
visited Mr. —-’s dog Captain 11., from the result of which
union great things were anticipated. No reason can be
assigned for the cause of death, and the hitch looked all
right hut a short time prior to her being found dead in
her kennel.
Dear Bulletin,— You mention the fact of
a Yarrawonga man having produced a beautiful
flower from a marshmallow inoculated with the
sap of the pelargonium. Really, you amuse me !
I have now for some years been growing potatoes
(murphm (jardenienses or vuli/ares) from an
original stock inoculated by myself with the juice
of the salt-bush and of a pepper-tree. It is a
superior spud, and we use it mostly for Irish stew,
which thus requires no seasoning. Last year I
inoculated my strawberries with turnip-juice and
the raspberries with tomato-sap, producing in each
case a ten-fold larger crop, and one which gave
splendid financial results in connection with the
local jam-factory. But this year I have exceeded
the wildest hopes that ever floated through the
brain of a company-promoter. I inoculated a
healthy gooseberry-tree with sap from a sample of
wheat, spraying the plant regularly with soda-
water. And, I assure you, on the word of a
farmer, that all the pastry-cooks in Melbourne
cannot beat my crop of gooseberry tarts.—Rich-
mond Farmer.
* * * *
The Commercial Travellers’ dinner at
Sydney, attended by Gov. Hampden, was des-
cribed by the papers as a “brilliant” gathering.
In fact it was so brilliant that to prevent people
from being killed by the glare of the thing, the
company and speakers included Mr. David Storey,
M.P., the biggest bore elected to N.S. Wales
Assembly.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Your “Plain English”
correspondent, “Heretic,” is a sturdy indivi-
dualist. He would have a privileged class in
sexual love. ‘ ‘ One man, many women—result,
normal reproduction ; one woman, many men—
result, sterility and specifically deterrent disease,”
says “Heretic,” without troubling to remark that
many women for every man were an impossible
state of society unless each woman scattered her
favors around. What “ Heretic” really means is
“ Some men, many women.” Those are my senti-
ments, always assuming that I ought to be
included among the polygamous minority. Let
the other fellows content themselves with one
virtuous lady each at the most, or only a portion
of a Delilah. But for me, Mormonism ad lib —
Another Warm Individual.
* * * *
The split among the N.S.W. artists has
led to a movement on the part of the new (and
more strongly professional) society for the placing
of the annual State vote for art instruction (now
controlled by the old Art Society) at the disposal
of the trustees of the National Gallery, and for
the institution, as at Melbourne, of art-instruc-
tion classes at the Gallery itself.
* * * *
Vic. Govt. Printing Office lias just taken
on 150 compositors for the electoral-rolls —a three
months’ job. Which recalls thaft an Australian
artisan now-a-days must be a Jack-of-all-trades
if he wants to live. Workmen seek employment
on the lines of shearers —no shearing, then some-
thing else. A man this month is napping stones ;
in February he will be a carpenter, in March a
printer, and so on.
* * * *
The most painful aspect of the Sheridan
case is the sufferings of the condemned man’s
devoted wife, whose desperate earnestness in
laboring for a commutation earned for her the
sympathy of all. For Sheridan personally it was
impossible to churn up much pity: a man
who had been directly concerned in the death of
at least half-a-dozen women, and who, after all,
couldn’t take the significant hint afforded by a
14-years’ sentence, almost deserved all the bad
luck in the criminal code.
Recent Bulletin testimony as to the
length and normal circumference of Gippsland
worms is corroborated by the nicest of Melb. bar-
damsels, who was bom in the big-worm district
and knows them well. In repose these reptiles
have a circumference equal to the end of the
plated prodders used for prodding bits of lemon in
a squash, and when fully extended they
measure about four feet from start to finish, or a
little less. Dreadful worms, the vision says, but
not more evilly-disposed than the ordinary human
worm who applies for drinks.

* * * *
One Australian city possesses a charitable
lady whose speciality cous sts in supplying tracts to
sick foreigners in the various hospitals. She im-
ports vast quantities of these tracts printed in
German, Russian, Italian, Spanish, and a multitude
of other languages, and whenever she hears that
Antonio or Dmitri or Hans or Pedro or Sven
Svensen or Said Abdallah ben Daoud has been
laid up through something heavy falling on his
head in the fish-saloon, she looks out a bundle of
suitable literature and rushes to his assistance.
Her Greek tracts, however, are not imported.
They are supplied in manuscript by a deranged
clergyman in Callan Park, and she assumes that
they are full of beautiful sentiments, though as
likely as not they consist of wild essays’ on the
legs of the ballet. Recently a Greek was brought
in suffering from concussion through a 40lb.
schnapper dropping on his spine, and he was
visited and presented with ten tracts, all the work
of the deranged preacher. He read three of them,
and then gave a loud shriek and died. Then the
sympathetic lady packed up the tracts and sent
A MIDSUMMER’S-DAY DREAM.
them by parcel post to his bereaved mother in
Corinth, to show that her son had died in a beau-
tiful frame of mind, and what her feelings may be
when she rer4» s them no one knows.
* * * *
In Melbourne, the other day, a laborer
who had recently been given employment on the
sewage-works fell dead whilst drawing his first
week’s pay. This sort of thing might be much
commoner in Melbourne but for the fact that
employers usually break it to their men so gently.
Even the weakest heart can bear up against the
sudden accession of “ four bob a day.”
* * * *
The Salvation Army reckons on ulti-
mately acquiring as a settlement the whole of the
valuable Jenkins estate between Manly and Narra-
been, the proprietress of which, a very old lady,
daughter of the original Crown grantee, has
already made the Army some very handsome pre
seats in land and money.

Miss Horsfall, a prominent member of the
Melb. W.C.T.U., stated in a paper read by her
recently that the body in question was appealing
to 32 Governments to disarm and to love each other.
That ought to close the mouths of male scoffers
who say that women know nothing of practical
politics.

It is written that when Gov. Brassey was
driven into Sturt-street, Ballarat, and surveyed
the statues of great men scattered about there, his
first remark was: “Fine—very fine! but hardly
healthy, I should think. Why not have the ceme-
tery further out ?”
* * * *
The W.C.T.U. organised a meeting of
adult deaf mutes in its Melb. hall, the other
day. This is the sort of audience that would
enjoy the average W.C.T.U. orator. The Argus,
reporting the proceedings, says : “ In the evening
recitations, &c., were given.” A teetotal griffin
reciting “ The Curse of Drink ” on her long, bony
fingers must be an inspiriting spectacle.
At an agricultural
show held recently in
the vicinity of Colac
(Vic.) one of the prizes
was for the best
‘ * dressed hen. ” Some
of the primitive inha-
bitants didn’t fully
understand that high-
toned way of putting
it, and as a consequence
one of the entries was
a, hot and distressed
bird with knicker-
bockers on, and a skull-
cap, and its tail stick-
ing out from under-
neath a silk jacket.
Also there was a large,
fierce exhibitor about,
who wanted to “ wipe
the flure ” with the
committee for refusing
to consider his agitated
animal in awarding the
prizes.
* * *
The rev. head of a
Melb. college is about
to give a Sunday morn-
ing address to commer-
cial travellers. Drum-
mers do not always
spend their Sunday
thus. In one Austra-
lian town, the head-
quarters of many travel-
lers, Sunday morning
is always passed in the
local brewery, to which
a shilling admission is
charged; proceeds go
to the hospital. The
drummers, it is sup-
posed, find it easier to
pray in the cool atmosphere of a brewery.
* ♦ * *
Prof. Morris, in a recent Argus article on
Australian slang, showed that he had got much of
his matter from recent Bulletin correspondence
on the subject. His screed was very incom-
plete, and did not touch on several interest-
ing problems. For instance, how are “fossick”
and “ tucker ” derived ? “ Billycan,” which
puzzles the Prof., is obviously a corruption of
“ boiling-can.”

The cycle has already begun to push the
horse aside. In Melb. a milkman pedals around
the suburbs with a gigantic can of cow-juice in
tow.

favorite amusement when a boy was to see a pig
killed.

Alfred Austin, the new laureate, was well
dubbed by Browning in his Pacchiorotto “The
Banjo Byron ” —no reference, however, to our
“ Banjo.”
Eight gunners lately went duck-shooting
at Campbell Island in the Murray River. They
got exactly one duck apiece, but potted among
them “143 tiger snakes, from 3ft. 6in. to 6ft.
long.” The Bulletin doubts a 6ft. tiger snake.
Dearß.—“ Bump ”is right about the corn-
bativeness of the average ram. If “ Banjo ” had
paid a visit to Mt. Morgan he might have seen
there a sheep that was a match for any dog in the
town. The local tykes thought it huge sport to
bait him on his first arrival in the township, but
a very brief combat (especially if in a corner)
satisfied their warlike aspirations.—P.L.

A native boy was arrested the other day at
Kimberley (S. Af.) for illegal diamond-buying, on the
following affidavit by Dr. Otto “lam a medical offici r
of the De Beers Convict Station. On the 23rd July, 1895,
convict No. 5200, David, alias Solomon, a convict under-
going sentence at the station, was admitted to the station
hospital. I examined him, and found symptoms of
tetanus, resulting apparently from an incised wound
about 2in. long on the inside of the calf of the leg, and
about sin. from the knee-joint. On the f 1 owing day I
considered it necessary to open the wound and see if
there was any extraneous matter inside, and on doing so
I found buried in the flesh, about 3in. deep, a piece of rag
tied into a small bundle. I removed the rag, and opened
it in the presence of the Chief Constable, and found it to
contain 31 rough and uncut diamonds. Th«p e diamonds
I handed to the superintendent of the station. ’

It would appear that Mrs. Maybrick has c
chance of being released shortly on the ground
that there is some doubt as to whether death
directly resulted from the arsenic administered by
her or from the constitutional disease from which
it was proved Mr. Maybrick suffered.
The “ Manhattan ” Luncheon & Tea-rooms,
“Basement,” Equitable Buildings, George-street,
Sydney, are the largest, coolest, and most popular
in the city. If you are in search of a first-class
mid-day luncheon or afternoon tea, look in at the
above rooms, where you will find the cuisine the
very best obtainable in town. In order to enhance
the attractiveness of afternoon tea, Miss Shaw,
the proprietress, has made arrangements for the
attendance of an excellent string band every
Thursday afternoon, between the hours of 3.30
p.m. and 5.30 p.m.*

File Wild-Cat Column.
There was presented, the other day, to N.S.
Wales Assembly a return, moved for by E. M. Clark,
M.L.A., of the working of the great land-resumption
swindle on the railway extension from St. Leonards to
Milson’s Point, North Sydney. Said extension is 24 miles
long and the amount of land (unimproved) which had to
be resumed was a fraction over two acres, which was
obtained at a cost of just about £6OOO an acre. Also, for
some reason not fully explained, the Government took
nearly eight acres more, which was not actually
wanted, at nearly £7OOO an acre. This is, roughly
speaking, somewhere between twice and three times the
average selling value of land in that locality. There are
sundry curious features about the list of purchases. One
man, who probably was a harder customer to deal with
than the rest, is reported as having got £2OOO for three-
sixteenths of an acre. Another got £6OO for a little over
one-eighth of an acre, which wasn’t wanted—in fact, it
was resumed entirely by mistake. Another person’s
1 vnd was resumed by mistake at a cost of £3OO.
Itather less than I acre, which wasn’t needed, was pur-
chased for £2720-at the rate of £ll,OOO an acre.
Taking it all round, the land which was resumed because
it was wanted is set down at £12,000, and the land
resumed “in excess of railway requirements”—in other
words, the land which wasn’t wanted—seems to have cost
£5',800. With all these figures in view, The Bulletin
st ill wants to know when a little Bill is going to be intro-
duced providing that the land-tax valuation, with, say, 10
per cent, added for disturbance, is to be the standard of
sill future resumptions ? Under the present system every-
body seems to get about twice as much as the fair value
of his property, and the man who makes the most row
gets four times as much.
* * * *
Dalgety and Co., Limited, present a better
b-■dance-sheet than is usual in the stock and station and
\v v -I business at this disastrous season. During the year
/■II led 30th June, 1895, the co. successfully met the
interest on £2,866,915 owing upon debentures, wrote
down the book-value of its assets by £102,000, and still
has a net profit for the year of £83,896, which enables it
to pay 8 per cent, dividend, and have £3826 to add
to the balance carried forward. Also, the reserve fund
'if £130,000 is intact, Considering that wool has dropped
16 per cent, since ’93, and 33 per cent, since ’B4, this is a
remarkably good showing. At the same time, the
£2,866,915 of debentures Took rather top-heavy. This
consists of irredeemable 4 and 44 per cent, stock, and four
different kinds of terminable debentures varying from 3
to 44 per cent. The average rate of interest is a shade
over 4 per cent., and when Dalgety and Co. borrow money
in England at 4 per cent., and then invest their reserve
fund in British consols at 2f per cent., there seems to be
a considerable loss on the deal somewhere. At the same
time, the fact that the co. has commenced to issue deben-
tures in a small way, at 3 per cent., shows that its credit
is very high. It also takes a good deal of the glitter off
the two Government loans which were recently floated at
the same figure.
* * * *
Some of the recent doings of the City of Mel-
bourne Bank liquidator are remarkable—to put the matter
mildly. Just before the collapse, the bank received £3OOO
from'the Prahran Council to pay interest on its deben-
tures in London, and £7600 from the Board of Works for
a similar purpose. One Judge decided that in the first
case the bank was only an agent and the money was held
in trust, and he directed that it should be repaid in full
instead of going into the general smash. In the second
case, another Judge decided the exact contrary. Then
the Full Court considered both cases, and resolved defi-
nitely that both sums should be repaid as a prior claim
on the assets. Now, the liquidator has got permission
to “blew” a lot more of the assets in fighting
these two decisions (with the creditors’ money) before
the British Privy Council! The liquidator himself
has no personal interest in the matter. All he really
needs is a legal decision which will give him authority to
take one of two. courses, either of which will—or ought
to—suit him equally well, and the Full Court gave him
the authority. He can have nothing to gain in
cozening the Prahran Council for the benefit of the other
creditors, or in dozening the other creditors for the sake
of the Prahran Council. But the habit of fighting every
small legal question to the bitter end, regardless of ex-
pense, is characteristic of liquidators. The hungry legal
sharks gather round the carcase and presumably whoop
on the liquidator in his wild career, and when he spends
a very b g sum in law-costs trying to get the very best in-
formation as to who a very small sum should be paid to it
is called “ protecting the interests of the creditors.”
• * * *
Robert Byrne, a South Melbourne auctioneer,
lately went bankrupt for a few hundreds of thousands,
and his estate paid 20s. in the £ and left £BOOO over to be
handed back to the insolvent. Whereupon the court, in a
prosaic, cold-blooded, unemotional fashion, gave him an
ordinary, plain discharge just as if he had been a common
one-Fink-in-the-£ insolvent. That court should have
been moved to tears, and should have made an entry on
its records in a-broken voice that there was one honest
man left in Melbourne, and given Byrne his certificate
printed in gold letters on vellum. And then the dis-
charged insolvent should have been escorted home in a
carriage by the Governor, with a brass band in front and
all the Judges riding behind, and after their carriages
there should have followed an interminable procession of
bankrupt boomers on foot, with the men who paid 19s.
in the £ walking next to the last carriage, and the men
who paid 4d. running on all fours in the Just at the remote
ehd of the caravan. Melbourne doesn’t deserve to have
an insolvent like Byrne.
* * * *
If the Kingston Ministry goes down at next
S.A. elections it will probably be by reason of its financial
policy—which is the weak spot in almost every Austral-
asian Government. Kingston has done much better than
some of his predecessors, yet there is another probable
deficit looming, and S.A. is weary of deficits. Still, it is
noteworthy that, taking the period from ’9l to June, ’95,
S.A. and Maoriland are the only two provinces where the
public debt has decreased in proportion to population ;
they each owe about £2 7s. less per head than they did in
’9l. Also they are both in a pretty tight place, as all
these provinces are when the foreign capital stops rolling
in. Tasmania and Westralia meanwhile have a boom on,
or in immediate prospect—and in proportion to popula-
tion, they have borrowed three times as much in the last
four years as all the other five provinces combined. There
is a dreadful sameness about booms in these parts-they
all come from borrowed money. Westralia has naturally
the biggest boom - it has borrowed £lB per head within
four years: Tasmania, having borrowed £7 Bs. per head,
is getting up the next best boom. Victoria, which is
actually paying off £250,000 of its past debts out of the
year’s revenue, isn’t booming.
J * * * *
The Seotch depositors, it is cabled, have resolved
to reject the E.S. and A. Bank’s reconstruction scheme,
on the ground that the institution is asking altogether
too much for human endurance. Scotch depositors are a
hard lot to deal with, and even liquidation is sometimes
pleasanter than facing several hundred infuriated Macs.
in a small room and trying to explain, amid a strong
gmell of whisky, that their siller is na’ forthcoming.
* * * *
The latest big Melbourne insolvency, that of
Michael Ferguson, represents an almost total loss of
£170,000 to the City of Melb. Bank, and £77,000 to the
Bank of S.A., while six other banks are let in for con-
siderable sums. The mortgages and amounts owing to
“ secured ” creditors are stated at £404,000, and the
whole value of the securities is set down at £60,000.
Under these conditions what is a secured creditor ? The
A.M.P. Society is also in the schedule for £23,637 Bs.
Altogether, it is a fine big boom insolvency, with half-a-
million liabilities, and very little in the way of assets.
* * * *
The N.S. Wales revenue for last half-year was
£4,936,620, and the expenditure £5,086,239, so there is
another apparent deficiency of £149,619. Treasurer Rem,
however, represents the balance as a surplus of £535,9ij6.
This result is arrived at by charging £573,783 to the
previous half-year, and £173,405 to “ 1894 previous
years,” so that even yet it seems G. Reid hasn t finished
making additions to the great “ Dibbs deficit.” This sort
of thing is eminently dispiriting. When the public
accounts were put on the alleged “ cash basis,’ each year s
expenditure and each year’s revenue were to stand against
one another, and if the money that went out of the
revenue during the year for whatever purpose was more
than the amount which came in, then the difference was
to represent the deficiency. There was to be no saddling
of this year’s shortage on to previous years, which does
no good and only makes confusion. And, apparently,
Mr. Reid is still not quite finished with the old stupid
system, whereby the year, say 1894, closes with a surplus,
and the said surplus appears in Coghlan’s statistics, and
there is a great whoop in consequence ; and then in 1895
half-a-million is charged back to ’94 and makes a big
deficit, and more sums are charged back to it in ’96 and
’97 and ’9B; and ’95 ends with a surplus, which is also pub-
lished with much joy, and is afterwards obliterated
through amounts being charged back in ’96 and ’97 and
’9B and ’99, and so on ad infinitum.
* * * *
Q. revenue and expenditure are both rising.
For the half-year just ended taxation returns have in-
creased by £22,000 and railway revenue by £70,000 as
compared with the half-year ended 31st Dec., ’94. On
the other hand, ordinary expenditure has increased by
£67,000 and loan expenditure by £138,000. About the only
good point of Boodlewraith Government was the economy
which Treas. Nelson enforced in ’93 and ’94. It looks as
if the province felt it was safely over the “burst,” and
was getting ready to borrow and boom again.
But things are on the mend generally. Even
little Tasmania has increased her 1894 revenue by £60,064
to make the total of £761,942 for 1895. Custom receipts
improved by £23,000. The change in the incidence of
land and income taxation decreased the land-tax by
£19,000, and increased the income-tax from £lsB9—the
first instalment under the new system to £46,528. And
there was a small increase under nearly every other head
of revenue. They are slow in Tasmania, and, at time of
writing, the Treasurer didn’t know what his expenditure
was.
* * * *
From the Judge’s farewell speech to Jabez
Balfour, previous to giving that podgy sinner 14 years
penal servitude ; “ You stand convicted of frauds extend-
ing over a series of years, and which I am satisfied you
carried out in connection with the group of companies
with which you were connected. . . . And you have
been the means of dishonestly obtaining from the public
large sums of money. ... It has been suggested that
during the period in which your fraudulent operations
were carried out, had times been more auspicious, if there
had been greater commercial prosperity in the country,
your operations would not have been disastrous —might
even have been successful. Success might have covered
the fraud and prevented its discovery, but success cannot
turn fraud into honesty. ... In the course of this
trial, certain crimes were spoken of as vulgar crimes, as
though a distinction should be drawn between the com-
mon methods of the ordinary criminal and crimes accom-*-
plished by the ingenious devices of men of education and
position. All I can say is ” Well, the learned gent
gave Jabez 14 years. The words in italics are specially
worth the notice of —certain people.
* * * *
There are innumerable buyers in Sydney for
N.S.W. Government stock, and the supply is so scanty
that during December, ’95, only £9O worth changed
hands. It sold at £ll2, and at that price it only yields
the investor, allowing for the loss on redemption, a shade
•over per cent. Therefore N.S.W. securities are now
going in Sydney at a better price than G. Reid’s famous
3 per cent, loan realised in London, and the blank foolish-
ness of that famous deal sticks out more conspicuously
than ever.
* * * *
The finding of the Hands Across the Sea mine
(W.A.) may be set down as another example of the
horrors of Der-rink. A party of prospectors were tramp-
ing patiently along the dusky track, when they came to a
bush shanty. They had one drink apiece and moved on—
all except the boozer of the party, who stayed behind.
An hour later he started after his mates with a thousand
bees buzzing in his head, and when he came to a cross-
road he lost all recollection of the instructions he had
received, and started with a demoniac howl on the wrong
track. A mile or two further a bullock-puncher told him
in a flow of kindly profanity that he had got off the gory
road,‘but he pointed out certain land-marks bv which he
might strike across country and find the blanky track
agai rf. He struck across accordingly at a blasphemous
jog-trot, arid half-way he stubbed his toe against a
magnificent outcrop and came down with a thud. When
he had finished hopping round dizzily with his foot in
both hands and cursing, he found that, he was standing
•on gold, and it was the Accursed Drink that had brought,
him 'there. An Adelaide paper started this lie, which
seems a verv probable lie as lies go.

“ Bayley’s Reward claim,” says Murchison
Times, “ has a record to date of 53,5000 z., this being the
product of 8440 tons. The average yield right through is
6oz. 7dwt., even after allowing for the poor returns of
late. The mine has been the most prolific producer in
Western Australia, the Great Boulder, New Chum, Star
of the East and Consolidated Murchison (Day Dawn)
•coming next in the order named. The combined
production of the last-named mines has reached
56,0u00z. ; so that Bayley’s Reward claim has yielded
nearly as much as all of the others ” But Bayley’s Re-
ward is now almost as ancient (and auriferous) as the
Pyramids. Let’s see—who was Bayley, and what was
his Reward ?

A correspondent suggests that in view of the
difficulty experienced by prospectors in many cases owirg
to lack of water to try prospects, Govt, would do
wisely by supplying iron tanks, say 400 gals. each, made
flat, to fit the beds of iron drays constructed durably for
the purpose—said articles to be lodged at each police-
station in mining districts and lent out to any
bona-fide miners on their finding security, and for
prospecting purposes only. Miners would find business
people to back them, and it would be the cause of new
goldfields being up. *

writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, succeeded

* * * *
Tired of prospecting the hundreds of outcrops
around Coolgardie, of coarse-grained, milky-white, and
rock-salt colored, and, above all, barren-looking quartz,
work is directed to find somewhere or other some
formations similar to the Boulder, which has proved itself
a mine, and, better still, has done more towards taking
the wool off John Bull than all the others put together.
A leader, 9in. thick, with a formation 2ft., found two and
a-half miles north of Coolgardie, is the latest thing
“ exactly like the Boulder ore.”
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin, —A word about the West. The
other day an ad. appeared in a Perth paper for 20 car-
penters to proceed to Coolgardie. Forty men rolled up
at the appointed time, and, as the agents found difficulty
in selecting, a ballot was decided on. Forty slips of paper
were prepared—2o blanks and 20 having the name of the
employing firm. By the time this work was finished 20
more carpenters had appeared, and the agents deemed it
advisable to go straight on with the ballot while they had
a chance. One applicant generously supplied a hat (he
never saw n again), the slips were thrown in, and a mad
rush ensued for the coveted prizes. A policeman tried to
keep order, and, finding this impossible, wisely retired.
After the battle the question of “ terms ” arose, and they
were : £4 a per week (for Coolgardie !), lucky winners to
pay their own fares, keep themselves, and buy their own
water. As 90 per cent, of them were stone-broke, the
result was a crowd of gloomy faces, about the only
fortunate one being the man who stole the hat. There is
more demand for carpenters here than for anything else,
and yet crowds of “swampers” are landing every week.—
T. H. Wilson, Perth, 18th Dec.

being “ staked ” on a fence, was busily engaged for two

brought by cyclists from Kalgoorlie, where the unfor-
tunate Matheson had large business dealings.

The story goes that two unemployed men from
Sydney Labor Bureau, who recently secured Govt, rail-
way passes and went to the country to try their luck as
gold-miners, induced a Crow Mountain selector to let
them prospect for gold on his ground, at the nominal
rental of 10s. per month. Soon they struck a patch, the
first three tons of which yielded 171 oz. of gold, which,
added to about £5OO worth of specimens, gave over £llOO.
The next 14 tons yielded £5500, and now, so says the
story, these miners are offered £15,000 for their claim.

At Wyalong during last year Channon’s battery
crushed 2153 tons of stone for 37960 z. free gold, without
reckoning tailings and concentrates—the average being
loz. 15dwt. 6gr. free gold. The largest cake was from the
Welcome Stranger, namely, 11330 z., from 185 tons.
At Nicolas and Reymond’s battery, from Oct. 30 to
Dec. 31, 780 tons were treated for 11930 z. of gold, an
average of a little over an ounce and a half per ton. The
biggest lot treated was 260 tons from the Perseverance
f0r6200z., and the best average was 14 tons from Hen-
nessy and party for 50oz.

cleaning up on 19th Deo. 38 tons stone for 418 oz. gold
just lloz. per ton. Total yield for year, 194 tons for
1040 oz. gold, to which must be added 250 z. from pyrites,

* * * *
Gympie’s year closes none too brightly, though
the field has seen worse days than ’95. The yield was
, 78,8870 z. from 80,392 tons —a good enough average ; and
£125,266 was paid in divs. The pity is that the profits
came from so few claims—the Phoenix is responsible for
more than half, £63,750, and the Phoenix P.C. Tribute
paid £17,775. 1 North Glanmire paid £10,800; 1 North
Phoenix, £6OOO ; Jones’s Caledonian single lucky crushing
brought £4950 ; and the Crown and Phoenix’s legacy from
’94 yielded £2166. Add Phoenix Golden Pile, £2400; 1
North Smithfield, £9OOO ; S. N. Zealand Junction, £1350 :
and you have almost the “ demnition tottle ” of Gympie
div.-paying claims for the year.
There is hardly a field in Australia (bar Ballarat)
so well supported as Gympie. As fast as the money
comes in in divs. it goes out in calls, and out of last year’s
£125,266 profits, £117,566, or thereabouts, were put back
in the ground again. Many claims are crushing con-
tinuously, and making ends meet, without being able
to get * on the div. list. Nothing under about
15dwts. to the ton has much chance of paying
a div. on Gympie, but a claim can exist on about lOdwt.
The trouble is that when the patch of good stone comes
it generally finds a lot of accumulated liabilities or neces-
sary works waiting to be provided for, and the div.
vanishes before it is fairly grasped.
Gympie’s total yield since the field started is
estimated at £2,035,916, which, at £3 10s. per oz., was
worth £7,125,706. The average annual yield since the
end of ’77 has been 78,3840 z. -with which last year’s
result (78,8870 z.) closely coincides.

Thanks to cyanide, Croydon’s total yield in-
creases while the yield from the mines is lessening—not
for want of gold, but for want of water. The 1894 total
was 81,2080 z. For 1895 the mines produced 54,7210 z.
from 39,094 tons which pans out much better per ton
crushed than either Towers or Gympie figures, Cyanide
got 33,5190 z. from 27,680 tons total, 66,774 tons, 88,2400 z.
And another big dam or two on the True Blue and Golden
Gate lines would have brought the figures very nearly to
the 100,00Coz. mark.
* * * *
The best-boomed outside-show on Charters
Towers the Golden Surprise—has at last received the
stamper-test. A crushing of 80 odd tons put through
yielded something over 2oz. The odd pennyweights in
this case do not matter, as the shareholders were looking
forward to from 6 to 8 oz. per ton, according to the elasti-
city of their respective imaginations. The result justifies
the name of the mine. As the reef, or leader, is onlv 4 or
5 in. thick, in hard country, and is composed mostly of
urown-stone, the disappointment at the result is further
accentuated.
Mills United, Charters Towers, is reducing
its crushing- engagements. Cause—falling away of out-
put. The mine is now keeping only 20-head stamps going
at the Burdekin mill.
Brilliant Extended Block, Charters Towers:
Prospecting "haft is now down about 1600 ft. It is esti-
mated the Brilliant reef will be cut at 2250 ft.—about the
middle of the year. It is also, of course, confidently ex-
pected that the reef will be cut, but that is another
matter. Reconstruction, for the second time, is round-
aboutly hinted at in the last report.
Peabody mine, one of the properties of the Mos-
man (English) Co., Charters Towers, has crushed 97£ tons
for 2710 z. The stone is from a recent discovery in the
shaft, in an under-a-cloud portion of the field, which
should now look up.

When your artesian bore is down and you are
drawing a million gallons per diem it isn’t all plain sailing.
At a Central Queensland bore the other day, the pressure
suddenly dropped from 40lb. to about half that. The
casing had begun to “ bridge”—that is, to silt up and fill
with mineral deposit.

One of Australia’s few living
millionaires is noted for the
meanness that passeth all under-
standing. He suffers from a de-
lusion that he is slowly losing
all his wealth, and his existence
is consequently wretched. The
last man who got any money out
of him was a ne’er-do-well relation,
who upon his first appeal was
repelled. “All right,” he said,
“I’m not a very pretty object—
my toes are showing out of my
boots, my pants are torn, I don’t
smell like Araby the Blest, and the
hair is sticking out of the top of
my hat. I shall go and sit out-
side your front gate, and tell
passers-by and visitors who I am. ”
“Go and be damned !” the o’ ’
man growled ; but after the wreck
had been there for a few days,
“a message came from the
house.”

never gives charity promiscuously on week-days,
provides himself every Sunday with a number of
3d. pieces, which he distributes to such ‘ de-
serving objects ” as he may meet while on his way
to church. The Domain deadbeats know tins
“ weakness,” and stick him up with surprising
regularity.
One satisfactory feature about S.A. Gov.
Buxton is his athletic figure and entire absence of
adiposity. The ex-brewer is a fine man for his
age, and it is bad luck for him that his Jeff eristic
face makes him an easy victim for the caricaturist.
Justice Windeyer was, when in Parlia-
ment, the author of the Act which enables
“ colonial ” barristers to occupy seats upon N.S.W.
Supreme Court Bench, and by a coincidence was
the first Australian barrister appointed under the
Act.
Ex-Premier Parkes wants a lump sum as
a grant from N.S.W. Parliament; he will not, it
is declared, ever take an annuity. “Old Jack”
Robertson declined an annuity and got a lump
sum, which he at once distributed amongst his
creditors.
Mark Twain intends writing his book on
Australia while in South Africa. He was offered
.£2OO each for ten articles for Harper’s Maga-
zine, but refused, as he reckoned they would spoil
the book, which he estimates will be worth
<£lo,ooo.
In her second book, “Snags,” Miss Sophie
Osmond, a Melb. pen-lady, refers to the shearers’
strike as “ the nearest approach to an outbreak of
socialism that has ever happened in Australia.”
Miss Osmond, by the way, is the Austral Salonite
who passed the cake to Kipling.
When the convict-ship John Berry arrived
at Sydney, in 1820, she had on board a prisoner
named David Lee, aged 28 years, who finally
gravitated to Parramatta Asylum, where he died
on last Boxing Day in his 103rd year. There can
be no doubt of his age, as his “record ” still exists.

larnev,” which is to appear in the Primrose
Lea ueG v ' ette. Surely, it is her Ladyship of
Jersey who i j meant!

London Daily Chronicle dismisses Jen-
nings Carmichael’s book of poems in one wither-
ing line. It just quotes a bit of J. F. Hogan’s
eulogistic introduction—about the young lady’s
gifts—and says : “If Mr. Hogan believes this, he
will believe anything.” Richard Le Gallienne
writes most of the reviews of poetry in the
Chronicle.
Ex-Governor Carington has placed his
family estate, Wycombe Towers, in the market
for sale, not, mind (as he is careful to let it be
known), because he is at all hard-up, but simply
for the reason that he has bought (at a much less
price than Wycombe will fetch) an old estate
which belonged to his mother’s family, Gwydir
Castle, in Wales.
Mrs. Threlfall, of Sydney, several of
whose lately-issued and successful “Starlight
Songs ” The Bulletin had the honor of first
publishing, has had a book of “Cradle Songs”
accepted by publisher John Lane, of London.
It will be out in the spring. Mrs. Threlfall, in
adopting the vein indicated by the title of her new
literary venture, has found her obvious metier.

who was in bed at the time, dealt him a blow in

Westralians now in Melbourne say that
ex-Governor Robinson spends his time in London
principally in writing letters of introduction on
behalf of a shoal of speculative company-promoters
to prominent persons in Australia. Calvert, the
muoh-advertising, came along with a pocket-book

E. L. Ettershank died at his residence,
West Melb., on Ist inst. The name evokes a
crowd of recollections of a most exciting period in
Victorian history. Glass, Quarterman, and
Ettershank were proceeded against by Govern-
ment far a breach of privilege, the proceedings
being taken by the Leg. Assembly itself without
the intervention of the law-courts. The matter
was in connection with the battles between the
squatting interest and a legislative majority over
the land. The .Judges released the three persons
mentioned from an imprisonment ordered by Par-
liament, whereupon G. P. Smith, as Attorney-
General, appealed to the Privy Council, which
upheld him, but nevertheless the offenders were
n>■bi In I L-u'k t< • prison. The Government was
satisfied with establishing the supremacy of Par-
liament over the Supreme Court.
Senior-sergeant Cassin, in charge of the
police at Adelong, N.S.W., died the other day.
The Sergeant was second in command at the fight
at Wantabadgery station when Moonlight’s party
stuck up the homestead. It is not generally
known that the Moonlight gang proved a great
disappointment to the police. At the time the
Kelly scare was in full swing on both sides of the
Murray, and some <£Booo was offered for the Kellys’
capture, dead or alive. When news of the stick-
ing-up reached Gundagai, Adelong and Wagga,
the impression that the Kellys held Wantabadgery
was rife, and every constable within 30 miles who
could be spared armed himself to the teeth and
hurried off to the scene. When the fight was done,
it was found that two bushrangers had been shot
dead, and Senior - constable Bowen mortally
wounded, and that the prisoners were an almost
unknown crowd who, with the exception of Scott
(“ Moonlight ”) were amateurs in crime.
Gov. Fowell Buxton is now known in
Adelaide as “ Chooky ! ”
On the eve of their discovery of the
famous Brothers’ Home tin-mine ? Tas., tha
Krushkas called at Sunnyside, Ringarooma,
and asked for a night’s lodging. Foreigners,
unknown and penniless, they failed to create a
favorable impression, and were shown into
the. stable as a favor. Fact is stranger than
fiction. The Krushkas now own Sunnyside,
and many thousands of acres around it, and
one of the brothers married a daughter of the
house at which they were told that “ they’d
find plenty of old bags in the stalls to make a
shakedown.” But, notwithstanding the hun-
dred thousands they have won from their
mine, Sunnyside, one of the most beautiful
little homesteads in Tasmania, is now in utter
ruins.
A yarn about cricketer Bonnor—who,
it will be remembered, attained English social
fame through Ruskin’s gush over his magnifi-
cent physical proportions. Augustine Pirrell
(“ Obiter Dicta ”) says he met the Australian
at the Johnson Club, Fleet-street. The talk
was about Dr. Johnson. Bonnor bluntly re-
marked that he had never heard of the man
until that evening. When somebody sniggered,
Bonnor, flushed, added : “ And, what’s more,
where I came from you can ride a horse 60
miles a day for three days and never meet a
soul who has heard of Johnson either. But I
say this much, if I hadn’t been Bonnor, the
cricketer, I would have liked to be Dr. John-
son.”
The new poet-laureate is admirably
chosen. Once, some honor attached to the
office : it was a badge of literary merit which
a people’s poet might be proud to wear. Now,
the laureate is royalty’s tame cat, its official
adulator and greaser. He pares the royal
corns, trims the royal nails, and is allowed to
hang about the royal kitchen and fry odes
and dirges in butter when there is a birth, or
marriage, or death to be officially celebrated
or deplored. No better poet could be chosen
to perform these duties than the highly-
respectable Alfred Austin. His verse is
sometimes melodious and clever, and always
utterly uninspired. It is absolutely loyal
and absolutely proper. Royalty may confi-
dently rely upon him to purr nothing which
might not appear in the Court Circular
itself. The new poet-laureate is admirably
chosen.
In connection with the proposal to settle
an annuity on patriarch Parkes, instead of giving
him a lump sum in cash, it might be well to
remember that the Old Man has, more than once,
made public boast of his conviction that he would
live till he is 120 years old. Wouldn’t it be
cheaper to start him (with <£lo,ooo added) as chief
prize in an art union, the winner to take over the
responsibility of looking after him for the remain-
der of his days ?
There was once in Victoria a Surveyor-
General named Ligar who mapped out a track
through Gippsland to the Kiandra diggings. The
route was known as “Ligar’s Track,” and so indi-
cated by calico bills nailed on the trees along it. It
was a gruesome and awful path, and those who
used it took their lives in their hands. One
digger who went by it took the trouble on his
return journey to cut all the G’s out of the name
of the surveyor. Ever since then it has been
known as “Liar’s Track.” A warning to care-
less surveyors.
Judge Windeyer— The Bulletin doesn’t
quite like to call him Justice Windeyer—says he
is not necessarily going to retire on the expiration
of his leave. But the fact that theS.M. Herald,
which has always been deeply in the Judge’s con-
fidence, farewells him may be taken as indicating
that it is his Honor’s present intention to retire.
His leave will probably be devoted to fishing in the
Privy Council pond. Failing a P.C.-ship it is
just possible that his Honor may be offered the
Chief Justiceship of Ashantee, which country
England is about to annex and civilise —by
degrees.

A tin of Australian canned meat, over 20
years old, was the other day opened in Melbourne.
It was perfectly fresh, though flavorless, after
travelling 400,000 miles or so on a coasting steamer
since its purchase.

And London Christian World (Nov. 7) gravely

The news lately wired that the Q. Govern-
ment Resident at Thursday Island has discovered
two rivers on the Cape York Peninsula, running
into the Gulf, and named them respectively the
Embley and the Ley, is rather perplexing. It is
rendered more so by the statement that they are
large and navigable for a considerable distance.
The Gulf side of the Peninsula has been traversed
over and over again of late, has been taken up for
cattle runs, and generally become as well-known
as the Sydney Block. Where these two navigable
rivers could have hidden themselves all this time
is a mystery. If Calvert had discovered them, or
even David Lindsay, no one would have wondered,
but Resident Douglas is a man of veracity and
experience.
Meteorologist Wragge is said to be stuck
for storm titles. Apropos of which one of the
most shriekingly funny stories on record comes
from the Tasmanian Telegraph Department. But,
sad to say, it is unprintable.
A very high medical authority says that
Queen Victoria finds increasing difficulty in
respiring, especially when asleep. In Nature’s
craving for oxygen is found the cause of H.M.’s
long and rapid open-carriage drives in all
weathers.

culture some portion of those energies now often
so profitlessly expended amid the storm and stress
of colonial politics.”— Westminster Review.
Dear Bulletin, —The Australian rabbit
can only mount a tree under favorable conditions.
The tree must be either hollow —with an aperture
at the top of the spout, for exit—or else leaning at
a very considerable angle. In the former case, the
animal usually works up with the aid of his back.
Wire-netting being set perpendicularly blocks the
prolific little pest, unless small enough to get
through. Witness the tens of thousands found
dead, at times, along the netting, which has effec-
tually blocked them from water. — Muzzle wood.
Among the convicts still lingering in Wes-
tralia, is an “old hand” whose repeated escapes
from the most closely-barred cages puzzled his
keepers. As a last resource, a specially-constructed
cell of bars was provided for him. “ Now, get out
of that,” said the gaoler grimly, “and you oan
have your liberty.” “Right, Guv’nor,” said the
convict, “ I will call before breakfast to-morrow.”
He kept his word, and got his ticket-of-leave. It
was not till then that he exhibited a tiny saw,
which for years he had carried secreted in his
mouth.
The Hot Wave.
About this season of the year look out for the
hot wave. Every now and then one sweeps over
the country, blistering the face of nature with its
scorching breath. It turns the fields of living
green into shabby patches of dirty brown, dries
up the rippling rills, and drains the life and vigor
from all animate objects.
How tedious and uncomfortable the long hot
days are. The cattle cease browsing and seek
the stream, where, half immersed in the cool
water, they meekly chew their cud ; the house-
dog lies stretched and panting in the shade, and
permits the dusty tramp to approach the door
unheralded; the birds hush their song and hide
in the deep recesses of the woods.
They are fortunate beings, for they may indulge
in the rest and quiet which nature craves. It is
the human beings who suffer most at these periods
of unnatural depression. The system revolts at
physical activity and demands a repose, but the
daily grind must go on. The harvest must be
housed, the wheels of commerce must be kept
turning. From field and shop the commands of
labor are imperative, and household duties cannot
be neglected. Men and women must work,
though the extreme heat makes physical effort a
seeming impossibility. They are periods of great
danger. The system, depleted of strength and
vigor, easily yields to the encroachments of
disease. The slightest irregularities are speedily
transposed into dangerous maladies, and the
necessity of keeping the system regulated and
toned lip during these periods is urgent. To those
who appreciate the situation it is not necessary t©
say now is the time when the use of Warner’s Safe
Cure is found most valuable in maintaining a
regularity in the natural organs, necessary to a
healthy condition, and in giving a .on' and
strength to the system which can be acquired by
no other means. Its combination of system-regu-
lating, blood purifying, and strength-giving pro-
perties is unequalled, as has been proved by its use
by thousands of people in all parts of the civilised
globe.
The following experience, like thousands of
others, testifies to the value of Warner’s Safe Cure.
Mr. Murray L. Allan, Government Officer, of
Goulburn, N.S.W., says: “ I have much pleasure
in lustring witness to the efficacy of Warner’s Safe
Cu e T had been troubled for many years with
congestion of the liver, and I must candidly state
that nothing has ever given me such relief and
built me up to such a degree as this remedy. 1
will always recommend it. I may also state that
those to whom I have commended Warner’s Safe
Cure have derived great benefit therefrom.”
