POLITICAL
POINTS
In place of “ Anglo-Australian, absentee-
parasite,” &c., what’s the complaint with “ Ex-
haustralian ” ?
The U.S. Monroe doctrine applies only
to Ameripa. J. Bull’s Monroe doctrine applies to
the whole earth.
Rhodes, in re Boerland, plays the
game of Cavour towards Garibaldi. This is to
disavow the thing, but take advantage of it, if it
gees.
Q. Premier Nelson may not seek re-elec-
tion owing to ill-health. Should he retire, recreant
Philp, the cocksure Tozer, and the smug Barlow
will each struggle desperately for the reins.
The Braddon Government helps along the
good cause of economy by selling - the departmental
waste-paper, old records, blue books, &c., to the
Maoriland paper-mills at £2 a ton. It made <£4o
last year out of this new source of revenue, and is
now known as the “ Waste-paper Ministry.”
Isaac Isaacs, the Vic. Attorney-General, is
strangely anxious to keep Johnny, his brother,
before the world. Lately they were ban-
queted together at Bright, and, as a matter
of course, the reporters of Ike’s speech gave the
brother a word or two of mention. Johnny, as an
M.L.A., is a screaming joke in real life, but his
brainier relative wants him to be taken seriously.
The Supi’eme Com-
Bill, reducing the power " nd
dignity of the Northern
Court, has been a bitter pill
to Townsville.
Just as Yictoria has
built up a big butter export
trade with England, news
comes that Argentina has
started to export on a large
s ::ale to the same -universal
market. And the Argentine,
with low wages, cheap land,
shorter distance from the
British market, and a de-
preciated paper - currency
which makes his sovereign
worth about 60s. for all pur-
poses within the country,
can cut out the Australian
hopelessly, provided he pro-
duces a good article, which
he probably will learn to’ do
some day. It is the old
story —there is no reliable
market except our own. If
the Victorian spreads him-
self to supply his own wants
i i the matter of sugar, and
thus saves the <£500,000 a
year which goes out of the
province to buy that article,
and if he can save the
£ 400,000 a year that has
hitherto been paid for coal
imports, he will do more
reliable good for his province
than if he struggled with the
butter-export trade till the
day of judgment.
The N.S. Wales Leg.
Council made only one
amendment on the Federal
Enabling Bill, its effect
being that the province’s
delegates to the Federal
Convention are not to be paid
as originally intended. Pre-
mier Reid wisely yielded the
point, else Federation, so far
as N.S.W. was concerned,
would probably have got
back to where it started
from. Also, he very un-
wisely announced that some
way would be found for pay-
ing- the delegates after all —
in other words, he announced
that the Government would
find some underhand method
of bursting up the statute.
There is a fine, ruffianly un-
scrupulousness about the
average party Cabinet with
a big party majority behind
it. TJnder the present system
the House can’t seriously in-
terfere with the Govern-
ment’s doings unless by de-
stroying it altogether, and so
long as there is no alter-
native save the not too popu-
lar Lyne, Reid may do almost
anything he pleases. He
must do something desper-
ately bad to make the
Assembly put up Lyne in
his stead, and G.H.R. trades
•n this fact in many ways.
Acting-Justice Cohen’s appointment to a
permanent N.S.W. Supreme Court judgeship may
now be regarded as almost a matter of oertainty.
But why on earth should Mr. Cohen be
appointed while a man of Edmund Barton s
calibre is available ? T s it beoause Mr. Cohen is a
political friend and Barton a political opponent or
the Reid Cabinet? Is the bench of justice to be a
political stamping-ground ?
Dear Bulletin, —Do you notice that Hing-
land has made a sudden rise in the favor of
theatre-audiences here since ’Murka began to
“come it a little too thick” in the Venezuela
matter? Australia’s alleged love for America is
not a very solid sentiment when tested. — Blanker
Grab.
Q. Attorney-General Byrnes, who pro-
mised the electors of Cairns never to desert that
northern constituency as long as it stood by him,
is now advertised as a candidate for North Bris-
bane at the coming elections. The seat-holders at
present are MTlwraith and Kingsbury. Neither
is likely to stand again.
Maoriland now requires foreign bagmen te
pay a license fee of <£so per annum before they are
allowed to pervade-the province with their samples.
As a result a good many Australian and other
drummers have abandoned Maoriland, and the
native drummer finds things looking up. In a
province run on protectionist principles the foreign
bag-person is about the most legitimate object of
taxation that could be discovered.
The S.A. Labor M’sP. who sat in the
expired Assembly have to run the gauntlet of a
plebiscite shortly, when their names will be
coupled with those of eight Labor men recently
chosen. It is regarded as certain that, of the
retiring members, Wood, Hourigan, and M'Gilliv-
ray will be dropped. In S.A. it is much better to
be an M.L.C. —a billet which lasts for nine years,
as against three years in the Assembly.
Item from the Freetrade Border Post
(Albury, N.S.W.): —
A district miller states his intention of importing Cali-
fornian wheat per Port Melbourne, taking it in bond to
Albury, when the duty is removed, as promised by the
Reid Government at the end of the year. He estimates
that he can thus land wheat at Albury at 3s. 7d. to 3s. Bd.
per bushel.
Another joyous prospect for the N.S.W. agricul-
turist.

THE HAWKERS' LICENSING DAY.
"'me Albury Bench seems to have taken up a position in connection with
Assyrian and other foreign hawkers from which it should promptly be shifted
by the authority which makes Benches. The local police objected to the grant-
ing of licenses to some of these aliens, on the ground that many complaints
had been received of the conduct of colored hawkers in terrorising women and
children. Thereupon the police magistrates intimated that henceforward no
licenses would be granted by the Albury Bench to them. It seems to me that
this line of action is as mean and unwarrantable as ever a civilised Government
permitted. It would be as justifiable to refuse ordinary citizens’ rights to Irish-
men or Scotchmen, because a few of their race misbehaved.”— Sydney D.T.

Federal Convention: Reid, Wise, M‘Millan,
Want, Lyne, Barton, Copeland, Ashton, Ewing,
and the inevitable Parkes. Ten probable Vic-
torian delegates: Turner, Isaacs, Peacock, Best,
Deakin, Shiels, Trenwith, Service, Wrixon, and
Tucker. Ten likely S.A. representatives : King-
ston, Downer, Cordon, Holder, Cockburn, Horn-
burg, Symons, Baker, Gillen, Jenkins. Ten Tas-
manians : Braddon, Clark, Fysh, Moore, Douglas,
Bird, Burgess, Pillinger, and two others who are
so doubtful that they may be anybody. If Wes-
tralia is represented, and her delegates are elected
on anything resembling a liberal suffrage, they
will probably consist of John Forrest and nine
men that have never been heard of before. And
if the Boodle Ministry can work the elections as
it usually does, Queensland’s 10 should be Nelson,
Tozer, Barlow, Byrnes, Drake, Glassey, Powers,
Philp, MTlwraitn’s ghost, and either Morehead
or Beelzebub for last place.

unemployed, is now dreaming about a scheme for
inducing shipping go’s to bring out poverty-
stricken immigrants at £l2 a head, the state sup-

The Age : “If ex-Premier Parkes had re-
ceived a testimonial from the public, no word
would probably have been uttered against him.
But, as it is, the representatives of the N.S.W.
Eabor-party have raised their voices in protest;
and, if the grant is carried over their heads by
mere force of numbers, it will be robbed of all its
value from a national point of view. If the ex-
Premier consulted his own reputation, he would
decline any grant of public money that any section
of the taxpayers objected to, and leave it to his
personal friends and sympathisers to fill up the
void. A donation to which half the donors are
compelled to contribute against their will is a
theft, and not a gift at all.”
Trenwith (Yic, M.L.A.) on man’s chival-
rous treatment of woman
I believe we have set up a certain standard of etiquette
that makes the decently-inclined of us lift our hats when
we meet a lady in the street whom we know, which
makes us get up in order that a lady may occupy a seat,
and which makes us rob women of half their wages when
we employ them. I would ask honorable members not
to forget, when we are remembering the good things
which we do for women, that when we employ them in
almost any capacity we rob them of half the wages that
they ought to get. Man has “ worshipped ” women, to
use the language of the honorable member for Mel-
bourne, by oppressing them during all ages. The pro-
posal to give them equal power with man is one means
that will be used as a lever to elevate them from the
position of social and economical degradation into which
they have been driven.
The Age also was very much hurt by
M.L.A. Prendergast’s declaration that a little
repudiation by Australia of the interest due to the
British bondholder would do no harm at the
present time. The Age is a bold, bad bi-metallist,
which says that the appreciation of gold has
doubled the burden of interest to the prejudice of
Australia, that one sovereign now is worth what
two sovereigns were 22 years ago, and that a
return to a fixed legalised ratio between gold and
silver is needed to adjust matters. Prendergast
accepted the Age premises that the official stan-
dard of value had swollen in a wicked, hurtful
way for the benefit of creditor nations —but Pren-
dergast would not wait for the long-deferred
remedy. He proposed a short cut to exactly the
same end, and the Age called him a wicked
repudiationist
In Maoriland, if a “ prohibited person ” is
found in an hotel he may be fined £5 ; the hotel-
keeper may be fined £5 for allowing him to be
there; and any person who accompanied him
when he went in, or accompanied him after he
was in, or incited him to go in, or incited him to
drink, or directly or indii-ectly helped, or incited,
or encouraged him to obtain drink, may be fined
£lO. The word “accompany” is so large and
general that, under this supremely silly law, it
isn t safe to go into a Maoriland hotel alongside
anybody, or directly in front of or directly behind
anybody ; and it is highly dangerous to tell any
casual stranger, inside or outside, that it is a hot
day, lest he be a prohibited person, and the
remark reminds him of his thirst, and thereby
‘ * indirectly incites ” him.
Smith was an Australian politicians’
“ghost,” a clever pressman who managed to
quarrel with every political party. But he had
gone away, left the country —was in hospital, some
thought. A candidate, addressing the electors,
said : “ I could never think of associating myself
politically with such men as Mr. Smith. ” Smith’s
voice (from body of hall) : “ Did I not write your
political address for Frog Flat last year?” The
candidate was short-sighted. Peering down
among the upturned faces, he stammered : “ Well
—ahem—l ” “Did lor did I not?” roared
Smith. “ Well—ahem—really, gentlemen—l ”
“Did I write your speech?” again demanded
HIS OTHER HALF.
(Talking pair in background.)
SHE: “See the newly-wedded couple. They say he married beneath hiw"
HE: “Rather!”
Smith. “ You did,” softly ejaculated the candi-
date as he collapsed amid the jeers of the citizens.
It wasn’t Smith after all, but a man with a voice
like Smith’s, put up by the opposition purely on
the off-chance.
The wobblesome Sydney D.T. now says
that the “accuracy of Judge Windeyer’s decision
in the Dean trial was most amply vindicated by
subsequent events ; ” also, that “ Judge Windeyer
has the satisfaction of not closing his career until
it has been demonstrated that, whether he com-
mitted an error or not in his methods in that
matter, he did no injustice.” Which would be
very good soft-sawder as applied to Judge Lynch,
but surely not as regards Judge Windeyer, who is
an official of an ostensibly civilised community.
As to the argument of “ subsequent events ” vindi-
cating illogical and tyrannous judicial actions,
just apply it to the Mount Rennie case, presided
over by the same Judge Windeyer, where four
boys were hanged, mainly on the evidence of two
men claimed by the Crown to be decent citizens,
but proved by “ subsequent events ” (within a
month and 18 months respectively) to be a forger
and an arsonist. It is the privilege of the Deity
alone to be vindicated by “subsequent events.”
Byrne, Q.C., whom “Tom” Slattery has
retained to lead the Privy Council appeal on the
N.S.W. Payment of Members difficulty, is author
of probably the most useful Act passed by British
Parliament last session. The Act, which came into
force on Ist inst. and is entitled The Summary
Jurisdiction (Married Woman) Act ordains that
where a husband wilfully refuses or neglects to
provide his wife with reasonable maintenance she
can leave him and sue for a weekly payment of
not more than £2. This sum she can obtain in
the lower court through any magistrate; if she
wants more than £2 she will have to apply to the
Divorce Court. The Act also provides that where
a woman “ goes wrong ” through the fault of her
husband that fact will not disentitle her to claim
the maintenance specified. Tire first intention of
the Act was to give women the pecuniary benefits
of the Divorce Court without the costly litigation
whiclr divorce proceedings entail, but the author
has included principles which carry the rights of
women much farther than the courts have hitherto
been willing to sanction. Mr. Byrne received the
support of the Radical members, and notwith-
standing the startling features of his measure he
carried it through with comparative ease. If he
succeeds as well with the N.S.W. business he has
taken in hand, and he is understood to express
confidence in his ability to do so, Premier Reid
and the Constitution will have another -Wrestle in
the open.

Report of the Trustees of the Sydney Cricket Ground .
In publishing the attached balance-sheet for the
year ending the 30th September last, being the
20th year of the existence of the Sydney Cricket
Ground, the Trustees desire to lay before the
public a brief history of the ground from the
beginning of the Trust.
The Trustees were appointed in January, 1876.
At that time the ground, with the exception of a
small area on the western side, close to the present
entrance to the grand-stand, was as nature had
formed it. A hill on the eastern side, where the
new stand has recently been erected, extended to
the centre of the playing ground where match -
wickets are now pitched. There was a water-course
at the foot of it, by which flood waters from the
southern slope of Paddington and the Rifle Range
found their way to a water-hole on the southern
end of the ground, and thence to the Botany
dams, from which the city received the water-
supply. At the Northern end, the site of the pre-
sent tennis-courts, there was a long, shallow
lagoon, much frequented by wild fowl during the
winter months.
Recognising that the want of a Cricket-ground
had been a long-felt one, the work of alteration
and improvement was undertaken with a will.
The funds of the Cricket Association, £750, were
generously handed over by that body to the
Trustees to assist them in carrying on the work,
and the Government of the day obtained a vote of
£5OO towards the cost of fencing and making
approaches from the Randwick-road. These sums,
however, formed But a small portion of the cost of
bringing the ground from its original condition to
the state in which it appeared when the first Aus-
tralian Eleven returned from England in Novem-
ber, 1878.
The Trustees had first on their own personal
account provided funds by making advances and
procuring credits to the extent of ,£2OOO. Sub-
sequently, assisted by Mr. F. H. Dangar, they
procured a credit of another £2OOO, the guarantors
being indemnified by Messrs. M. H. Stephen,
Stephen Campbell Brown, Arthur Docker, John
M. Gibson, W. C. Goddard, and the late William
Clarke.
From the beginning of the season 1877-8 the
Trustees have been assisted in financing and
managing the ground by a committee selected
from the representatives of the Cricket Clubs then
forming the N.S.W. Cricket Association —Mr.
Gibson, Mr. Goddard, Mr. Docker, Mr. Teece,
Mr. W. B. Walford, and Mr. Beal—these gentle-
men (excepting Mr. Walford deceased, and Mr.
Teece, who is now a trustee), with Mr. Faithfull,
Mr. Bray, and Mr. Portus, forming the Ground
Committee of to-day. The late Mr. W. B. Wal-
ford, up to the time of his death, was honorary
Treasurer to the Trustees as well as honorary Trea-
surer to the Cricket Association. That gentleman
was succeeded by Mr. Teece, and the connection
between the Trustees and the Cricket Association
continued until 1884, when the Association decided
to take control of its own financial arrangements.
Prior to this, the Trustees were ex officio members
of the Committee of the Association. Now, only
one of them holds a seat on that body by virtue of
his office, just as the Trustee of the Domain
Ground holds his seat at the present time.
The Trustees have, since the Association under-
took the management of its finances, received
from Intercolonial and other cricket matches,
managed by the Cricket Association, the sum of
£2198 10s. 2d., the Association receiving £10,557
2s. 7d. clear of all expenses in connection with the
matches, hut from this sum the expenses of teams
visiting other colonies had to be met.
Meetings of the Ground Committee are held on
alternate Thursdays, at which generally a Trustee
attends, when all matters in connection with the
ground are considered and arranged.
Besides the valuable services of the Ground
Committee, the Trustees have had very great help
from the Mayors of the City (beginning with the
late Mr. James, Merriman), Aldermen, Town
Clerks, Mr. R. Seymour, and the Principal
Officers of the Corporation. Without the help
received from this quarter, the Trustees would
have been obliged to lay out large sums of money
in procuring material for forming the embank-
ments which afford to thousands of people who
patronise the outer portion of the ground a good
view of what takes place within the arena. While
thanking their municipal friends, the Trustees are
mindful of the public, from whom the greatest
assistance has come. They, by their generous
support, have enabled the Trustees and the Ground
Commitee to form a perfect ground, equal, if not
superior in its appointments and surroundings, to
any in the world.
While thus acknowledging the public patronage
it is desirable to point out that the Trustees have
not received the whole of the gate-receipts. The
Trustees’ share of these receipts has but seldom
exceeded 20 per cent., the balance going into the
hands of those bodies controlling and managing
the various sports which take place on the ground.
During the 20 years of the existence of the grounds,
the receipts from all sources have been as
follows :
The amounts received by the various bodies who
have engaged th i ground are not given here, b.u
some idea of the sum th ■/ would total may be
obtained by multiplying the amounts under th '
headings of erica ‘t-mitches and sports by 4,
Thus, while the Trustees have received £33,581
6s. Bd. in percentage on gate receipts from Cric cet,
Football and other sports, the Association ■.
Unions, Clubs, and Leagues conducting them hav >
had approximately £L34,0(44. From this sum tli •
bodies in question have, of course, had to defray
the whole of the expenses in connection with th ■
various events conducted by them, includ,ap-
prizes, wages, and expenses of gatemen, &e.
The statement of the 20 years’ receipts and e
penditure has been compiled from the yearly-
balance-sheets, which, after having been duly
audited by well-known and qualified accountants,
have been periodically published in the Sydney
papers.
The past season has been by far the most profit-
able that the Trustees have had, the visit of th •
English team being mainly accountable for it
Never has there been greater interest taken in
Cricket matches in the colonies than in those
played by Mr. Stoddart’s team during the pist
season.
Besides the improvements already on the ground
and in course of erection, the Trustees are h v »g
plans prepared for a new Ladies’ Pavilion < n th ■
site of the old one between the Members’ Pavilion
and the Grand Stand. It will have a frontage o
125 ft. and stand back 44ft. from the p’aying
ground. There will be two galleries, a lower and
upper, each having separate refreshment and retir-
ing rooms. This structure with its terraced front
will give seating accommodation for at least 2004
persons. Accommodation for the Press will b ■
provided almost in a line with the wickets at th ‘
Northern end of the ground.
The Trustees have also in view the extension of
shelter accommodation for the patrons of the
outer portion of the ground by adding a cantilever
to the present eastern stand and erecting another
stand on the top of the south-eastern embankment.
For the season 1877-8 the members’ list numbered
418 ; for the past season it numbered 2401. Whilst
this increase is gratifying it is to be regretted that
the members are far below the members’ roll of
the Melbourne Ground. The annual subscription
to both grounds is the same, and whilst the mem-
bers’ entrance-fee to the Sydney Ground is One
Guinea, it is Three Guineas to the Melbourne
Ground. For the past season the income of the
Melbourne Cricket Club from members was up-
wards of ,£OOOO, whilst the income of the Sydney
ground from the same source was, as the balance
sheet will show, £3632 Bs, 6d.
During the year Mr. Philip Sheridan, who has
filled the position of Managing-Trustee for nearly
20 years, resigned from the trust, and Mr. Richa-d
Teece was appointed a T ustee in his place. Tu •
Trustees desire to place or record their deep sense
of the valuable services gratuitously rendered by
Mr. Sheridan ; it being principally owing to his
zeal that the ground is in its present satisfactory-
position.
In concluding this combination of history and
report, the Trustees desire to offer their thanks to
the press of the city for the generous notices of
the ground and the sports that have taken place
there. The desire of the Trustees has been to
make the ground popular, and in this respe it the
press has materially aided.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OUR CO-OPERATIVE GROUP
(Told by a Member.)
vVe was growing sick of loafing, we was tired of
Brisbane Soup,
So we up and started forming a Co-operative
(Iroup—
Barman Bill and Mick the Lumper, Plumber
Brown and Ikey Jones,
Whose occupation previously was chiefly breaking
stones.
There was Thomas Wall, confectioner, and Smith
who painted signs,
Young Jenkins from the grocer’s store, and Meggs
who mended blinds;
O’Shea the long compositor, M‘Quilk the mason
short,
With the sundry wives and kiddies which they
never could support.
There was Bloggs, the carpenter, who talked of
cows and country air,
And Brice, a softy gentleman who paid for every
share ;
And Mitchins with the fruitful wife who on him
had bestowed
A score of little Mitch ins, and another on the
road.
From a glance at the foregoing ’tivill be easy, sir,
to see
How splendid were the prospects of our new com-
munity,
For our members represented every calling out of
hand,
Except the class who cultivate and dwell upon the
land.
They gave us for a settlement a strip of trees and
stone
(Tho’ had it been Toowoomba loam we never
should have known) ;
But whilst we had a pound or two and Govern-
ment supply
It may be that for half-a-week we got on pretty
spry.
As the journey up was jolty and our nerves not of
the best,
’Twas agreed that work be started by a week of
perfect rest,
So we lay about a-smoking and a-lounging in the
sun,
And ate up all the women cooked as soon as it
was done.
Our Secret’ry, one Thomas Wall, accustomed to
the pen,
And Barman Bill and Lumped Mick were chief
committee-men ;
And Brice, the gent as first financed our daring
enterprise,
He drove the cows up mornings when too damp
for us to rise.
It appears the chief advantage of a Group for
mutual toil
Is that nineteen men can go to sleep whilst One
man tills the soil;
And when a member speaks about the fence that
should be run,
The scorn that greets him makes him add, “ I only
spoke in fun.”
Our Secretary did not toil. He had a playful
way—
He k opt a-granting to hisself a public holiday ;
And took our only cart and horse to reach the
nearest pub.,
Rem irking “ that he needed strength and had to
go for grub.”
Wh' st the Group was still discussing all the
features of his sin,
Bill and Mick, the chief committee, further
troubles did begin—
They “planted” the tobacco, docked our flour
with ribald jokes,
Whilst their wives (on double rations) called us,
“Them there vulgar blokes.”
And when the cheque for bullocks came—we
guessed it from the first —
They never bought no beasts to kill—but went
upon the burst.
And it turned out subsequent their bills for whisky
all were sent
As vouchers for potatoes to a guileless Guving-
ment.
’Twas only human nature that the work came to
a stop :
We was looking for the whisky—we had never had
a drop ;
When the Secret’ry returned to camp and turned
out Tim M'Phayre,
Remarking “he must have that hut as a stable
for his mare. ”
Whilst we was gathered round MThayre and
nearly come to blows,
Up yonder in the milking-yard another row arose,
It seemed that Mrs. Ann O’Shea and Mrs. Jane
M‘Quilk
Had driven milkman Brice away and taken all the
milk.
And milkman Brice, he found himself unable to
pursue—
Last night he borrowed of an axe and chopped his
toe in two —
But Mrs. Mitchins and the kids amid the general
fray
Got access to the ration-hut and took the “ junk ”
away.
We have written to the Guvingment to tell of our
success,
And asked for cheques to go away, and also for
“redress”;
And the only one who doesn’t talk of damages and
law
Is Brice, the softy gentleman, who’s dying of
lock-jaw.

Dear Bulletin. —Re the Wilson divorce-
case (Melb.), you say “the law arrived at one
its most remarkable decisions. ... It decided
that because both of them (husband and wife)
were entitled to a divorce neither of them ought
to have it. ” A commentator who applies the term
“remarkable” to that Wilson decision, and sup-
pose 3 that he has discovered anew legal absurdity’
must b 9 very young indeed. [Nothing was said
about a new absurdity.—Ko. B.] Tne revolting
doctrine that misconduct puts both p etitioner and
respondent out of court, and draws their matri-
monial tie tighter than other people’s, avas
formulated long before The Bulletin was born,
and has been thundered from the Bench by
learned moralists Lord forgive them !—ever
since. It was remarkable once upon a time. It s
a chestnut now.— Court Crier.

Dear Bulletin, — Re the “stoicism” of
Australians. I was in work ; my mate was not.
lie went to another township about 50 miles off to
look for work got drunk, and took a dose of
nitric acid. \\ hen I heard of it T boarded the
tram, arrived at 10 p.m. that night, swallowed
half a tumbler of whisky, and rushed to the hos-
pital. 1 approached the bed with tears in my
eyes, and, leaning over him, said, “Great
Heavens, old man, what did you do this for? ” He
looked at me calmly for three or four seconds,
then, wiping his swollen mouth, remarked—
You ve been drinking !”—H.Y.
Dear Bulletin, —A recent correspondent
asks what good plain rabbit-proof netted fences
u r< m: r 1S tru ® that ra hbits have acquired the
nabit of tree-climbing? First, though rabbits do
climb trees, if there are any forks about, they
seem to have a knack of getting their heads
caught—as the descent is being made, pro-
/r° Wlt}l wire-netting. Thousands of
labbits attempt to climb the fences round the far
western dams, but, unfortunately for themselves
too soon conclude that they are'at the top, push
them heads m, and so get caught. More carcases
are to be seen hanging near the top than else-
where. Apparently there is a difficulty about the
aescent when the top is reached, yet a few un-
doubtedly do scale 3ft. Gin. fences, though it is
only by rare accident. A much greater number
dash through the netting; the regulation ISin.
gauge is certainly ineffective. But, anyhow,
rabbit-proof fencing out west in N.S.W. is a
rarity, though hundreds of miles of what is thus
described have been built. The fence offers some
resistance to sand ; and a decent bush sandstorm
will cover the top wire in an hour or
two, so that the rabbits can go over with
ease, or in far less time will eddy out
| enough of the fence to give passage under-
neath. It is one thing to keep the fence round a
20-acre lucerne paddock rabbit-proof, and quite
another to keep rabbit-proof a 20-mile fence that
passes over sand-hills and across water-courses. It
is also one thing ; to risk putting up the lucerne
paddock fence, and another thing to risk putting
up the 20-mile fence round the l-sheep-to-10-acres-
paddoek where you are pretty certain the posts
will be eaten away inside five years. But the men
of the Eastern and Central divisions don’t seem
to think of these differences when they set them-
selves cheerfully to dismiss the rabbit problem.—
Fencer.
Dear Bulletin, — Re leucoderma, I have
seen amongst the Dyaks occasional individuals
with reddish hair, greyish eyes, and skin light
enough to show a liberal freckling. There was no
affinity to the albinic type, and, as the men were
unquestionably full-blooded Dyaks, I was obliged
to hypothesise some such reversionary peculiarity
as that by which, in breeding, for instance, from
white poodles, there may come to hand at any
time a perfectly black puppy or a brown or both.
—Macassar.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Aided by a wicket which “ did a bit,” N.S.W.
downed S. Aus. for 134 runs in the second innings, and
won the match by an innings and 34 runs. It was hard
for Giffen and co., especially as a stronger N.S.W. team
had beaten Bananaland by only nine wickets, and the same
team had beaten Vic. by only 123 runs.
Borne good fielding was done in the match.
Reedman caught four Sydneyites out in the first innings,
one of whom was the top-notcher, Donnan. In S. Aus.
second innings, MTxibbin caught three men out and
bagged four wickets to his own cheek.
Howell did most of the destruction in S. Aus.
second innings, getting five for 49, as against M’Kibbin’s
four for 73. Nevertheless, MTxibbin, for the trip, comes
out with 20 at 17 ’7 each, while Howell got 13 for 19T each.
Donnan’s batting average for the trip is 99’G6 ;
the N.S.W. team’s batting average is 34'3.
Howell got Lyons again in the second innings ;
and MTxibbin got G. Giffen each time. Now that llowell
knows Lyons’ weak spot, the swiper may become as easy
a gilt to him every time as he used to be for Peel in Eng-
land, or as G. Giffen used to be for Richardson.
The feature of the N.S.W. cricket trip was the
batting of Donnan. Successive scores of 163, 46, and 93
against the best Vic. and S.A. talent are champion’s form.
Thebigpoints of the S.A. match were the batting
of Donnan and the bowling of Howell. Howell bowled 44
overs, 17 maidens, for 88 runs and 8 wickets; MTxibbin
sent down 41 overs, 9 maidens, for 134 runs and 8 wickets.
Sydney Cricket-ground may shake hands with
itself over the recent victories. For, this being the first
time in eight years that N.S.W. has beaten Vic. in Melb.,
and the first for five that she has beaten S. Aus. in
Adelaide, public interest will get nearer enthusiasm-point
in interprovincial matches than formerly.
* * * *
After three hollow wins at Christchurch, Wel-
lington and Dunedin, the N.S.W. cricket team got an
unlooked-ior check when it met the All-Maoriland team
in the first trial match, losing by 142 runs. M.L. made
a much better stand in the second innings (254) than
anticipated, and on Fisher taking 5 wickets for 20 and
Robertson 3 for 44, the rout of the visitors was practically
complete. Callaway took 7 M.L. wickets in the first and
8 in the second innings.
* * * *
Will G. Giffen never learn to change his own
cowling? Three wickets for 140 runs almost equal to
the whole S.A. first-innings score of 152. “ Garrett twice
lifted the mighty Giffen over the leg-chains !” It should
be: “Giffen kept himself on so long, and bowled such
rubbish, that even the venerable Garrett resented it, and
hit two extra soft ones clean out of the ground. Giffen
thereupon changed the bowling at the other end.”
Donnan top score again with 93 in the first
innings. Donnan, going in first, has done big things this
season —against Btnianaland, 40 and 30 (not out); against
Vic., 160 and 46 ; now 93 against S.A.
• * * *
“ Billy ” Bruce, most popular and unassuming
of Australian ci icx -iers, will probably stand out of the
team visiting England, as his law-practice is more lucra-
tive now than cricket, and to go Ome would simply mean
losing it all. So Bruce will probably lay to his book.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —“ In ” couples Eady with
Coningham tor a place in next Australian Eleven. Now,
beyond once making a first-rate score in Hobart against a
second-rate team from Victoria, what has Eady ever done
to be in the same street with Coningham V Giffen, it is
true, once sent him a badly-worded congratulatory mes-
sage by cable ; but a cabled certificate of merit from the
Great Giffen, even if it were in good form, would not keep
a batsman’s wicket up against English bowlers. In Tas.
N. v. S. match, just played, Rady's bowling was com-
pletely collared by second-class batsmen, while neither
his batting nor his fielding was up to even Tas. high-
w iter mark. Eady, it is said, could be improved. Then
why not improve him first and pick him afterwards ?
Yours, &c., Theta.

The wail of discontent is already heard over the
proposal to have o ily 6d. gates at Manchester and Ixen-
nington. Casli results are the first consideration ; the

* * * *
Tommy Drew, a likely S.A. colt for interpro-
vincial honors, stands only sft. ljin.—much shorter than
Sid. Gregory.
* * * *
Samples of daily-paper cricket-report: “M‘lvib-
bin bowled with deadly effect”; “the wily MTxibbin ” ;
■ * the tricky bowler, M’lxibbin ”;“the invincible MTxibbin ”1
This after MTxibbin got four wickets for 61 as against
S.A. Jones’ seven for 103. And Jones bowled on a better
wicket and against a team which made 320 runs, while
S.A. scored 152. Later we’ll get it: “The graceful
Howell, having bared his magic arm, came with willowy
movements and smiling mien to the bowling-crease, and
delivered his fatally subtle ball; the astonished wicket
fell.”
» * * *
The second innings of N.S.W. against Victoria
and the first against S.A. were remarkable for the amount
of life displayed by the last joint of the tail. Garrett
made 70 against Vic. and 61 against S.AII and Kelly put
up 58 against Vic., 33 against S.A^
N.S.W., having the advantage right through,
did no more than a fair thing in beating Vic. by 123 runs.
* * * *
Sydney Cricket Ground trustees report a most
successful year to Sept. 30. Receipts were £10,139, and
after paying off £l5OO debentures the expenditure leaves
£lll9 to carry forward. The English Eleven’s visit
brought the trustees £3629 in ground rent and profit.
Only one thing is certain re the next Australian
Eleven. The selection committee will be more generally
and violently abused than any previous choosers of a
team.
* * * *
The cricket-writers who gushed over Albert
Trott’s achievements against Stoddart’s weather-beaten
crew, at Adelaide, are looking very foolish now.
* * • *
Had Tom Garrett’s batting in the second innings
of N.S.W. v. Vic. been performed by ’Enry Parkes it could
hardly have excited more comment on the batsman’s
age. Hearing the remarks of the crowd, and reading the
Melbourne newspapers next day, one could have supposed
“old Tom” to be about 94 next birthday. Garrett is
sixteen years younger than Grace in first-class cricket
experience, and would doubtless live through another
Australian Eleven tour with more credit to his country
than several of the rapidly-deteriorating boys who will go
’Ome next March. Re “veteran” cricketers, there is
some talk of restoring Barlow to the Lancashire County
eleven, and Barlow, The Bulletin fancies, is nearly old
enough to be Garrett’s father.
* * * *
“I am the biggest hitter on earth. I can drive
a ball farther than any living man. I may be caught, but
no living man can bowl me. I play for the public. I
bring the London crowd. When 1 go in to bat ’phones
and wires fly everywhere to let the world know that I am
in. When I am fluked put how they dwindle away!
Th.y are bothering me to go home next year, but 1 won’t
go there is nothing in it. I ought to think of my
country, 1 know—the team will be a failure without me.
I have spanned the universe several times. I have ”
And so on. Now, who is he ?
* * * *
There was a bicycle race-meeting at Melbourne
Cricket Club ground on New Year’s Day. The somewhat
monotonous announcement that “ all the champions ”
would appear drew an £875 crowd of holiday innocents,
who came fully prepared to see “all the champions”
struggle for supremacy and “smash records” it possible.
The weather was favorable for glorious bike d nigs. A
couple of liberal championship prizes might have given
the public something like the fun they had expected to
get tor their money'.
Nominally a meeting promoted by the Melb.
Bicycle Club in the interests of sport, this was, in truth,
a money-grabbing show fixed up by the M.8.C., the
M.C.C. and Zimmerman, the profits being “ wi.reked ” by
those three institutions. £2O/ was the amount, recklessly'
lavished upon sport, and £668 (less modest exes.)
represented the profit when all was over. A good
day - ’s work for the showmen a bad day’s sport
for the public. Zimmerman, without doubt, proved
fully satisfactory as far as he went. No bicycle rider
could be more sick than the Murkan looked on Jan. Ist,
with his drawn, pasty face, and a silk-handkerchief tied
round his long neck. His appearance was that of a
“goner,” as far as his old championship ability is con-
cerned. Zim. came out for his heat in the 2-mile Brassey
Stakes (a handicap which absorbed more than hall the
total amount of prize-money invested) and made a
decent pretence of being on ttie job. The heat was won
in 4min. 58sec., and yet it was too fast for the great Zim.
and the renowned Parsons, who gracefully retired before
the finish. They were understood to be reset\ ing them-
selves for classic events.
Three of the advertised champions didn’t show
up for the principal handicap, or anything else. Pither
couldn t ride on account of a mishap ; Martin was going for
bigger heansat Bein'i and Don Walker also stayed away',
because, perhaps, hem magement’s prizes didn’t make it
worth his while to .... .-here. Bellow, of Vic., captured
the Brassey Stakes final, with the hard-working Italian,
Porta, second, to the regret of everybody who appreciated
his plucky - try to win outright, and his bad luck in
getting blocked by some of the local candidates.
Zimmerman’s second appearance occurred in his
heat for a One Mile Invitation Scratch Race. Prizes of
£2O, £7, and £3 were the temptation offered to contest-
ants in this high-sounding affair, consequently' champions
didn't make a uig muster. Two starters for Zim. s heat.
They' were both bound to get places in the final, so their
time, 2min. 47sec., for the mile, w 7 as rather kind Under
the circumstances. They' might have “ walked over ”in
a leisurely manner. Ten men in all rode for three heats
of the 0.M.1.5.R., and Zim. won the final from Harris,
who beat Parsons for the £4 difference between second
and third moneys. The American champion beat the
English ditto in 2min. 36sec., and this was the sort of
stuff that 15,000 people had paid to gaze upon. They saw
no more of Zim. The International Five Mile Scratch
Race knew him not. Alas ! it is probable that five-mile
races are things of Zim. s past. At six-and-twenty years
of age he is rapidly growing old, in the present writer’s
opinion.
This International Contest was a medley of
pacemakers and bogus starters. Three out of six starters
wheeled off the track in the second lap. The remaining
three had each of them a tandem or triplet to urge them
on their wild careers. Finally', Harris won in 12min.
27sec., nearly overlapping Parsons. The other man, Car-
penter, earned his £3 for third place at a respectful dis-
tance in the rear. Parsons lolled over his bike in an
apparently fainting condition when Harris seemed to have
him beaten, and would have retired, but the crowd
groaned at their ex-idol, whereupon he shook off some of
his faintness and started going again.
It is hard to say whether Parsons becomes faint
when he is collared, or is collared when he becomes faint.
Anyway, the two circumstances happen simultaneously
in the Vic. champion’s case. Martin outrode him for a
mile race at the “Austral” meeting on the M.C.C.
ground, and he reeled in the saddle. Harris ran away
from Parsons in a five-mile race, and again the beaten
man began to reel.
Parsons, indeed, took his licking at the hands,
or feet, of Harris in the five-mile race with very poor
grace, and went off the picture of disconsolate disgust,
whilst his fickle admirers swarmed about the Britisher,
who lay all of a heap on the grass, and needed much
fanning and pumping before he could be dragged in.
Harris exhausted himself endeavoring to lap the Vic.
champion, which he was within an ace of doing.
A. W. Hill, The Bulletin’s fancy for the Aus-
tral, annexed a second-class mile handicap at the M.B.C.
holiday meeting, last week, in fair style and time. In his
heat for the two-mile Brassey Stakes, however, he rode
with very little dash and judgment, and was beaten out
of a place. Kellow won the £lOO prize with his head
rather than his legs, whilst Porta lost it in the same way.
The present resurrection of Melb. bike-racing
will not be for long unless a strong club is formed to
supersede the rival reigning powers. The M.B.C. has no
sporting bottom. It is represented by two or three
billiard-tables, and refreshment accommodation on the
third floor of a Collins-street building, and is mainly a
place of evening call for young bikers who would be much
better in bed. The club (which compounded with its
creditors for seven “ bob ” in the £ a couple of years ago)
works up a big summer meeting, at which its ardent
disciples ride for about 40 per cent, of the profits when
times are good, and have the satisfaction of seeing
“ foreigners ” capture the bulk of that. Anyway,
the M.B.C. spends a lot more money in organisa-
tion, &c., than it provides for the encouragement
of honest bike-racing, and under its auspices the
“ sport ” is resuming the old stink which made the public
so very sick in days of y'ore. The League of Wheelmen
may be a well-meaning matter, but it isn’t a sporting
institution, nor does the blessed league appear to have the
slightest purifying influence. If the public are to go on
paying their halfcrowns to look at these bits of boys
riding round a tiack, a solid bicycle club will be necessary,
not only for the improvement of prizes, but as some
guarantee of decent racing-a club with power to
punish cronk performers, and sweeten the atmosphere,
and, also, with capital enough to form its own racecourse.
* * * *
We may discover presently that cycling is the
one athletic sport at which a good little man is actually
better than a good big ’un. Although Zim., the great
original champion, is a six-footer, diminutive riders pro-
mise to star in the future. Parsons is only a bantam
(sft. s|in.) anyway, and Michael, the flying Welshman of
the moment, stands sft. half a foot shorter than the
average English sassiety girl. This would be very strong
evidence in favor of the small-champion theory, but for
the fact that tall, heavy men rarely rode bikes in the pre-
“ safety ” days, and are only now beginning to enter the
arena.

* * * *
Claims for the glory of recognition as the oldest
Australian cyclist roll in fast. E. S. Walter, of Albury,
and Prof. Kernot, of Melbourne, rode machines in 1869,
while Norman Selfe, of Sydney, claims to have made and
ridden a bicycle in ’6B. Another claimant is Alfred
Doubleday, a Melb. coachbuilder, who states that he
made and rode down Collins-street on a two-wheeled
bone-shaker “in the early sixties.” Prior to these dates
the four-wheeled velocipede had been the vogue.
* * * *
The Sydney League of Wheelmen should take
care not to kill the goose that 1 ays the golden eggs. Some
prominent League men, several of whom want to get into
Parliament on bikes, call upon Australian wheelmen at
large to form a colossal league and astonish the earth.
Better stay at home than bother about £309 a year. The
League made 1 1800 out of the last two Sydney meetings,
and has never been able to get its hat on since.

Sample of cycle report: Harris, the Englishman,
was beaten by Zimmerman in the Melb. Bicycle Club’s
mile scratch race. Harris was unwell (especially after the
race ; this fact not mentioned). Again, same day, Harris
beat Parsons in the Five-Mile Race. Parsons was suffer-
ingfrom influenza (caught it riding five miles behind
Harris symptoms not manifest till after the race). Zim-
merman didn’t compete in the five miles ; his doctor said
lie was too ill to ride. (Fact is, bicycle had been left in a
draught and had caught a bad cold). Give us a healthy
cyclist, somebody.
Yes, the cycling firms should advertise :
“ Wanted, a first-class rider who will not contract
dysentery, or marriage, or influenza just prior to a big
race.”

and won his heat sitting up.

The sturdy Leicester boy, Harris, is a prime
favorite with the Melb. crowd, chiefly because when fit he
goes for all he is worth. There is nothing suspicious
about his neat pedalling, and when he took the froth off
Parsons’ cup of glory on New Year’s Day, the leather-
lunged mob couldn’t yell loud enough with pleasure. One
elderly gent, was so overcome that he threw his arms
round the Polytechnic rider’s neck and kissed him fer-
vently on each cheek with a sonorous smack.

* * * *
Quiver’s victory in Sydney Tattersall’s Cup
stamps her as a weight-carrying stayer, but it doesn’t free
her reputation from the stigma of being an in-and-outer.
In the Summer Cup she appeared to have all sail set
coming into the straight, but her finishing run was feeble
and unconvincing, and she finished lengths behind the
placed horses. Her heavy weight in bad going was set
down as the cause, but Mahee, in the top division, won.
In Tatt.’s Cup she was never further away than third,
and eventually scored from the handsome little Acmena,
who was within an ace of beating - her. On several occa-
sions (Quiver has disappointed her party, notably in Melb.
Cup, for which she started second favorite. The penalised
Mahee was never in Tatt.’s Cup, shaping badly, but the
favorite, Fort, made even a worse exhibition. He ap-
peared jaded and stale. No end of cash was carried by
Response, who also failed to make a decent show. The
time, 3.314, was decent, but all past contests show
nothing ap, reaching record-smashing. Nightingale’s 3.30
in ’94, when Mahee ran third, is the best chronicled.
* * * *
The swelled-head disease, inevitably caught by
prominent jockeys, is an Australian turf characteristic,
and its existence isn’t to be wondered at. When a pre-
viously unknown rider pulls off a big cup or handicap he’s
promptly interviewed, paragraphed in every paper, bar,
perhaps, the War Cry and the Methodist Ebexe/.er, and
his face-picture duly printed. Also, he is lauded to the
skies by his friends ; congratulated by the big, big owner,
the punter, the chap he used to make mud-pies with, the
bookie, spieler, and other nondescripts, anxious to make
his acquaintance. All of which tends to make him believe
lie’s a most superior person. The scene changes, though,
when he’s guilty of “unseemly conduct” later, and gets
three months' rest. The papers abuse him roundly, and
say he got too big for his boots; his friends call him
bad names until he rides and wins again. Then the old
slobber is poured on with a pint-pot. There are tio flies
onany jock, in the public estimation, so long as he wins.

Melbourne started its new year with a fairly
disastrous day at Flemington. No actual favorite got
home, although the first winner, Kobold, was equal
favorite with Newhaven at 6 to 4 in a field of three. The
Tasmanian horse, Lord Charles Scott, made a good few
people happy after the Standish Handicap, but the lucky
on ‘s who played up their “ Scott” winnings went sorrow-
fully home. The Chevalier, often expected, turned up at
last, in the Bagot Handicap—a 15 to 1 chance in a field of
10, and the general grief came to a climax over the
victory of Blue Vest in the Criterion Stakes. Blue Vest,
a half-sister to Solanum, went out at 16 to 1 in a field of
eight. Fortunately, the V.R.C. had no cause to complain
about the gate-receipts.
M'Auliffe, who rode the Meddler, winner of
Tattersall’s Club Handicap, passing the winning-post,
thought Courallie had beaten him. So did Gough,
Courallie’s jockey, who remarked “ I just did you ! ” But
when the numbers went up M’Auliffe had won. M‘Auliffe
has just returned from America, where he has been riding
the kust two years. During ’92-93, M'Auliffe won 40 races
in i,.S YV., mostly country events.
* * * *
Austin Delaney, who rode the winner, True
Blue, in the Carrington Stakes, at Sydney, won on the
same horse the Nursery Handicap, 12 months ago, and
the Nursery Handicap at last Sydney Cup meeting. He
has been a jockey for six years, and this last is his biggest
win. Some three and a-half years ago, Delaney was hurt
in a railway accident, and didn’t ride for 18 months.

(£600) was but moderately backed by the public, but his
stable counted h ; m a good thing long back, and staked
big money on his chance.
* * * *
The ancient absurdities of the horse-reporter are
being graited upon bike journalism, it seems. The horse-
reporter is at his stupidest when he speaks—as he oiten
does speak—of certain animals showing better “ form ”in
weight-for-age races than in handicaps, or vice-versa. We
are gravely informed that, whereas Pepper-box is superior
to Mustard-pot at even weights in a mile handicap, Mus-
tard-pot is the better horse when they meet on even terms
in “ good-class company.” Poor little punters swallow
this stuff, and “the Pot” is generally accepted as a
weight-tor-age specialist. The bike-reporter for Melb.
Arous talks similar nonsense re Martin, the Irish-Amurkan
plugger. “ Martin’s riding is improving every day, and
he is probably the best handicap-rider in Australia. As
a matter of fact, the man is always one and the same
individual, whether he rides in a handicap or races with a
crowd of other men from scratch. What he can do he
ctn do, and what he can't he can’t. And when Martin
does less than he might, it is because he doesn't feel very
well or the money doesn't tempt him to go all the way,
under the circumstances.

Paddy Nolan, the fine cross-country horseman,
who had his pelvis broken in three p.aoes while schooling
Cumberoona over fences last May, will never again ride as
a jockey. But he’s an active trainer, and looks sound and
well.

The Sneaker, who won the Steeplechase at
Randwick last Saturday, is a queer jumper. He rises
so slowly that if he takes off Oin. closer than a fair thing
he hits the jump for certain; but it he takes off yards
behind a lair mark he goes over dead sure. The length of
his jump showed itsell against Reckless. The two would
race level to the jump, but over the jump the Sneaker
would be a length ahead.

The old question “Is early two-year-old racing
desirable ? ” bobbed up again in Melb. last week, when the
. V.R.C. committee decided not to alter things. At present
youngsters may be raced after August 1. Argument;
This is too early, for it deteriorates the racehorses. Other
siders favor Oct. 1. Oppositionists aver that this would
strike a severe blow at the breeding industry. Personally,
the writer wouldn’t race a horse until he was a three-year-
old, but poor owners want oat-money and ny at good and
bad juvenile stakes. The rich owner does ditto. Who
suffers then? The horse, undoubtedly. No animal can
matui e into a crack aged pacer if he is raced off his legs,
cartel, a rnut from one province to another, and has the
life anu spirit lashed out of him when a child.
* * * *
Mahee and Miner, failures at Flemington, were
equal to getting a cup apiece in the summer, though well-
weighted and untanoied. It s always the way. Good
horses get home sometimes, no matter what weight
they have, but it’s the exact time that trouoles what-
ever brain the punters possess. Anyhow, the old advice
is still good “ Always back the best. ’ If there’s no
“ best,” back the nag that people say has got no chance.

This story is told of a lately promoted Vic,
policeman. It was on board the old Holden Crown at a
publicans picnic that a certain well-known pug. (then at
the very top of the ladder) got nasty and was mildly
called to order by the very young anu harmless-looking
constable. “ Its all very well,” said the pug., “for you
to talk like that to ate—you know it s only your blanky
uniform saves you.” “If you think so,” quietly rejoined
the constable, “ I'm prepared to make you respect the
man as well as his uniform. Just you meet me at the
back beach.” They met, and the constable took off his
uniform. That pug. never forgot those eight rounds.
“ The ring,” he used to say, “lost a champion when that
fellow donned the blue.”

Griffo once again. He had a one-round go with
Auliffe in a Sixth Avenue saloon after cocktails, bluster
and language. The Rocks boy managed to locate the
right ear of M‘Auliffe, who responded with one on the
belt, and then they clinched. Onlookers took sides, and
one drew a pistol, but was floored and pitched out.
Others who had armed themselves with salad-bowls,
spittoons, and glasses, dropped ’em and cleared at the
word “Cops!” But Griffo remained behind. He hadn’t
finished his whisky.

C. MT’herson came out champion sawyer at the
recent Tasmanian contest. M’Pherson, who has twice
previously won the championship, cut a log Oft. 4in.
girth in linin. 55sec. —30sec. under all previous records,
and “is open to saw anything on earth.” The saw he
used was male specially for him in America.

There are eight courses around Sydney devotee!
t> the alleged sport of pony trotting, and, in more sense:.
6 ian one, Sydney is “full” of pony racing.
A race scene. A crowd of hard-faced punters
h ig over the fence which separates the Leger from thi
Grand-stand enclosure. They are waiting for the “office.”
An abbreviated horse emerges from the paddock, th
jockey-boy glittering in colors and gold. He ambles alom
bv the fence, and, in response to the anxious enquiries o
h i mates, and greatly to the dismay of the greenhorn
wi > has already backed his mount, informs all and
sundry that he “Ain’t ’avin’ any.” As this remark applie
to nearly all the “ competitors,” the most difficult task of
the backer is to find the “triers.”
In a recent race, only one horse was trying.
Fate was in the humor for a joke ; the trier fell, and then
the fun began. The efforts of the others to lose wen
better than a circus. The three leaders were dead lev el,
each boy pulling at his mount as hard as he dared, and ai
the same time beating the air furiously with his whip
Of course, one of them had to win, but the face of hi-
owner was as long as a wet week. Never was a man so
obviously disgusted at success.
The patrons of one pony course were some time
ago afforded the edifying spectacle of a race where every
horse was “ dead.” There were only two horses in who
had any chance of success, and the two owners had each
backed the other’s neddy ! The rest of the field were not
at all in a hurry.
The pony-owner proposes, but the jockey dis-
poses, and the tip from the latter is the safest. The
riders are probably the most cunning in the world. There
was, not long ago, a case where a boy backed his mount
fora place— i.e., backed him on the tote—and backed
another to win. The “other” won, his own mount
getting second, being half-a-head behind the winner !
A favorite practice of the non-trier is to get a
bad start; all he has to do then is to keep behind. The
“ dead ’un ” can usually be detected by the superhuman
activity of the rider. The most hideous feature of these
meets is the crowd of wretched, blighted boy-men who
patronise them.

The population of Geraldton, W.A., is
600. Of these some 200 are Chinese, 53 Japs and
22 Afghans. “ Room for the leper! Room ! ”
The tender of Walkers, Limited, Mary-
borough, has been accepted for the construction,
locally, of 30 out of 40 heavy goods-locomotives re-
quired by the Q. railway department.
M.L. Govt, paid a large sum for Little
Barrier Island, near Auckland, and appointed a
caretaker to prevent destruction of the valuable
birds it contained. Yet Baron Rothschild, a great
collector, has recently received 90 stitchbirds and
other varieties from M.L., and Govt, is waking
up about the matter, after the little beauties have
flown The 90 stitchbirds probably represent the
last of their kind in M.L. Extremely rare they
realise high prices in Europe, and Rothschild will
be accounted lucky in securing such a large con-
signment.

Yet another corpse now sleeps in Botany
Bay’s Procrustean,bed of prison quicklime. The
hanging of Sheridan was a wanton debasement of
the currency of justice. The Judge’s remarks in
sentencing the man left it to be inferred that he did
not wish him hanged, and most of the jurors have
declared that they did not contemplate execution
following on what they considered as merely a
formal verdict. The public, it seems plain, did
not desire the hanging, and, so far as The Bulle-
tin knows, there is not one newspaper which
has written on the subject without in
some _ form or other expressing the opinion
that life-imprisonment would have served all use-
ful purposes. For the hundredth time The Bul-
letin asks—When will some member of Parlia-
ment submit a bill empowering juries in capital
cases to say not only whether they believe a man
guilty, but if guilty how guilty they hold him to be ?
Yojury could possibly have bracketed the crime
of Sheridan, who by misadventure killed a woman
in procuring abortion, with that of the murderers
who kill for the sake of gain—with the deeds of
say) Nicholls and Lester, who, luring poor men
with promises of work, butchered them in cold
blood for the sake of their miserable silver watches
and trunks of clothing. The Bulletin knows
perfectly well that the hanging of Sheridan was
the deed not of Premier Reid, but was purely
attributable to certain pietistic influences repre-
sented in the Cabinet, and it sincerely hopes that
when the Assembly again meets the Premier will
introduce, or give his best support to, a bill under
which juries, instead of being able to shift any
measure of their responsibility on to the Execu-
tive, will be made to feel that the fate of a man
capitally charged -esi. so far as concerns the evi-

Sheridan, the Sydney abortionist hanged
last Tuesday, died penitent after the stereotyped
fashion. He was apparently an energetic, busi-
ness-like man, and when he got into the con-
demned cell, and the gallows came actually in
sight, he set himself methodically to save his soul
as if it was a case of so much dirt to be removed
with a shovel in so many hours at so many cubic
feet per hour. Says the Star :
He rose at 6; a quarter of an hour later he made
his morning prayer and meditation. At 7 he said the
Rosary and had breakfast, afterwards exercise (if
allowed). At Bhe would indulge in spiritual reading, and
at 9 Sacred Scripture from the old text. At a quarter to
10 he had rest, and paid attention to his spiritual affairs,
and having spent three-quarters of an hour in that way
he proceeded with the examination of conscience. Then,
at II o’clock, he received the Litany of the Holy
Name, and afterwards the Seven Penitential Psalms,
At noon, he had his dinner and walking exercise.
In the afternoon, Sheridan commenced with the Rosary
and the Stations of the Cross. At 3, he read light read-
ing matter, and, between that hour and 5, had tea and
exercise. At 5, he said the Rosary, 20 minutes later the
Litany of Our Blessed Lady and the Miserere; at 5.30,
the Meditations, and an hour later the examinations of
the day. At a quarter to 7he said his night prayers. He
made at 9 o’clock each night a preparation, and went to
sleep each night saying (hymn omitted). He read ex-
tensively from the “ Imitation of Christ ” chapters
marked by the rev. chaplain, and from the Scriptures he
read similar chapters. During the day he attended to
the following injunction by the chaplain “ Offer your
heart fervently to God during the day, and each time
regret the past. Have recourse frequently to the help of
Mary, your Mother in Heaven, and reflect after on the
vanity of the world. “ God is all; man is nothing.”
It sounds weirdly methodical, doesn’t it? So
much soul to be saved ; so many hours to save it
in at so much soul per hour ; rise at six and shovel
vigorously at the soul till seven ; then breakfast
and more shovelling at the soul till noon ; then
dinner and more soul, barring a space for “light
reading matter;” tea and another desperate
attack on the soul till bed-time. As related in
the soulless papers, it is all so hideously like tin-
case of a business man who has undertaken to
deliver a lot of meat in a given time, and just
rushes the last forequarter over the gangway as
the ship is about to sail. If salvation comes b\
hard digging, Sheridan, who pegged in for a whole
month at 1 1 hours a day, deserved to get it.
“Jim” ELigue, ex-editor of Sydney E
News (“ account o’ the hexercution —speshul ’dee
shun!”) headed the deputation of M’s. P. who
pleaded for the life of the condemned Sheridan
If Sheridan had been present he would probably
have dug James in the ribs and asked him whether
he really meant it.
Says the foreman of the Sheridan jury :
The Judge, when summing up, explained to us that
according to the evidence Sheridan was technically guilt}
of murder. He however, told us that it was within our
province to return a verdict of manslaughter. This 1
wanted to do, but my colleagues argued, “ What does it
matter whether it is one or the other, they will not han
him, and whichever verdict is brought in Sheridan i.-
sure to be kept in gaol for the remainder of his life.”
Sweet simplicity ! It’s perfectly plain that then
are still a number of people in the country utterly
ignorant of the history of N.S.W. Executive. Ii
the Sheridan jury didn’t wish Sheridan to be
hanged, why didn’t they recommend him to mercy
failing a verdict of manslaughter ? The history o
N.S.W. shows that every jury should, wherevei
possible, dispense its own mercy. Up to now, be
it said to the credit of the Reid Govt., no convici
recommended to mercy has been hanged by tha
Ministry.

“ I cannot prevent the introduction of the

Apropos the Sheridan hanging at Sydney :
It is not generally known that nearly every “for-
tune-teller” is an abortionist’s pimp, and receives
from the illegal practitioner a fee, averaging two
guineas, for every woman “ in difficulties ” whom
she sends to him.
The very latest version of things from the
Transvaal is that Dr. Jameson’s invading army
consisted of only 460 men, that it fought 2710
Boers for 36 hours, and only surrendered when its
ammunition was exhausted and it had lost 137
killed and 37 wounded. If this process of enlarge-
ment goes on The Bulletin expects to hear next
week that Jameson invaded the Transvaal with
only himself and another man, and that he fought
5000 Boers for six weeks, until the other man was
utterly demolished by a long bombardment with
canister shot. It is also stated that if Jameson
had been successful the German ships at
Delagoa Bay were under orders to land a force
of marines and crumple him up, which would Lave
involved a raid through British territory, with a
great and gory war as a natural sequence.
Finally, it is reported that Rhodes has resigned
the Premiership, so presumably the evidence of
his complicity in Jameson’s raid has become too
strong to be resisted. If Rhodes did plan that
ugly muddled rush into the Transvaal, then he
isn’t the, Napoleon he was supposed to be —not by
a very long way. According to the most coherent
report, Jameson started with 700 men, and 300
supports so far behind that they never got there,
and 100 reinforcements in front who were cut off
before they reached him.

Dear Bulletin, —Notice how, when a
Christian dies, his friends say “Poor A. has gone.
How they give themselves away—if, as of coursej
he has gone aloft. The common man under
Imperial Rome didn’t say “poor”: he said
‘ 1 divus, ” which means ‘ ‘ gone to glory. ” Catullus,
who believed nothing, said in his ode to his dead
brother, “ Are atque vale,” which means “So long,
I shan’t see you again.” A Christian who believes
his faith shouldn’t talk like that. —IGNOTUS.
Hydatid disease is most prevalent in South
Australia, one patient in every 100 admitted to
S. A. hospitals actually suffering therefrom, whereas
the general average for all Australasia is only 1 in
206. Following S.A. in descending order of fre-
quency come Vic., N.S.W., Tas., W.A., M.L., Q.
There is a special concentration of hydatid disease
in the S.E. corner of S.A., and the western dis
trict of Vic. Moreover, all through the Aus-
tralias the disease has become far more common
of late years.
The pearl-divers at Manahiki and Penrhyn
Islands are probably the best in the world They
don’t bother about sharks —indeed, the man-
eaters very seldom come on to the reefs, but they
are mortally afraid of the local eel —a fierce fellow
sft. long and vicious enough to snap at a kauri
board. The usual pay of the divers is 7.50 dols. a
month, and 5 cents per lb. on the shell they bring
up. Some of them make as much as 150 dols.
monthly. They spend it on European delicacies
and sewing-machines ; every house on the island
of Penrhyn has a hand sewing-machine, and both
men and women are expert machinists.
Nothing in particular recalls the story of a
rare old drill-sergeant, a master of his profession,
out feeble in grammar and other appurtenances of
language, who, years ago, had to drill Brisbane
Grammar-school girls, by way of a variation. The
dsars were out on parade, and the sergeant‘went
at ’em. “ Byer right, dress ! Now, watch my
movements : when I say, ‘ Ten-shern ! ’ do you
stand as I do. Heads well up, but chins down;
chests out, shoulders well back, hands straight
down at the sides, palms of the hands to the
front, little fingers in a line with the seam of the'
trousers —ahem, hem ” —then, turning to the lady-
principal, “Er, but I say, Miss, how’m I to tell
’em to keep their stummicks in ? I can’t say
‘stummicks,’ I s’pose?” “Not very well.” A
pause. And then, aloud —“ And mind you. girls,
keep yer waists well in—like I do. ”
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
John Archer, M.A., of Melbourne, refers
the public to “ a work not yet known,” for full in-
structions as to acceptable and answerable prayer.
Prayer, it appears, must be “offered in words
that are plain, simple, and concise without redun-
dancy of expression, without vain repetitions, and
without quotations from Scripture; and offered in
absolute confidence that it will be answered.”
Archer, M.A., confirms The Bulletin’s private
opinions on this all-important matter. The Lord
stipulates that even His most faithful petitioners
shall not drivel if He is to be expected to meet
their wishes. The prayer of the typical parson
availeth not at all. If you mean to get there, you
must pray in pithy, grammatical sentences. A
smart literary man’s prayer would come off every
time had he the necessary faith, therefore the
people who possess plenty of faith but no brains
or education should engage experienced paragraph-
ists to write them prayers which they may learn
by heart. A new industry.

The Argus, pronouncing the minimum-
wage anathema, declares with leaden humor: —
The navvy with the gigantic physique of a Maloney will
in future be able to claim no advantage over his brother
workman of the slender build of Jimmy M‘Kean.
Glossary : Maloney is the smallest of Melbourne’s
public men, M‘Kean the biggest. The Argus
idea is that the large, elephantine man who weighs
nearly a ton, and moves like a hippopotamus on
stilts, is the model worker, and that masses of
meat are most efficacious in shifting the earth.

The Jester of the Damned.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Laughter-Maker was dead who had shaken
the world with mirth ;
His soul flew up from the grave, leaving a mournful
earth ;
Up through the web of the stars, till he came to
the Gates of Gold,
And there he claimed admittance, right haughtily
and bold :
“ Out on the earth You created, my laughter can
move them all;
When thay saw my name on the coffin, they snig-
gered under my pall.
“ When the collar was tight and galling, the yoke
too heavy to bear,
I taught them how to ease it, I lightened the load
of care.
“ Weary and wasted women, haggard and hopeless
men,
Drank for a moment from Lethe, learnt to be
merry again ;
“ Laughter bred patient courage ; I, with my gibes
and quips,
Taught them to dance in the fetters, laugh at the
curling whips—
“ They whom love had forsaken, they whom love
had accurst,
T 1 © whose souls were ready out of their lives to
burst,
“They whom the world had trampled—asking
but breathing space—
Rose and conquered the world by laughing back
in its face.
“ Gaze on the green globe yonder, whirring and
spinning along !
Through the hoarse jar cf its erglues cat:'i you a
wisp of song ?
“ Mine is the song they are siugin,-,, mine is the
mirth you hear,
Over the steaming turmoil and jangle of rusted
gear:
“ Laugh when the strain's severest—make it aj
you can l
Smile at the nerve that tingles—conquer it—-prove a
ma n /
“ Play with the scythe and hour-glass—grin for his
grin give Death /
Show him you are his master —laugh with your
dying breath !
“ This is the creed I have practised, this is the faith
I taught—
Ask of the Millions yonder the miracles it has
wrought.
“How do you reckon the tally? What is the
wage I hold ?
Have I not earned my welcome? Open the Gates
of Gold ! ”
Strangely the Judge smiled, speaking, “Judge of
yourself you’ll be—
You are paid in the coin you pay with ; and for
the payment—See ! ”
Opened the lapis arches, parted the sapphired
sheen —
The lj m '•hter-Maker fell on his knees at the sight
revealed between.
All the glow of a garden, all that a sunset shows,
All the distracting beauty that flushes the face of
a rose !
Hues that play in the opal, shadows on molten
gold,
Just ere the ingot, cooling, frosts in the shielding
mould!
Music the midnight planets murmur down in the
deep, # •
All the haunting faces thronging a Poet’s sleep !
“Now,” said the Judge, “ for your verdict! What
does th it Ruby declare ?
Look in the mirror of blood and flame, and read
your answer there ! ”
A strutting, leering clown, with a hideous vainted
face,
Dull, soulless eyes and coarse lips curled in a
mountebank’s grimace !
Said the Judge, as there rose faint harmonies of
some celestial hymn:
“ How would that figure look, think you, in the
choir of the Seraphim ?
“ What have the Blest with laughter? They have
no whips to endure ;
You and your Art grotesque would here have a
sinecure. ”
The Laughter-Maker saw and heard, and he knew
his ugliness !
He turned away and dropped in dismay to the
depths of nothingness.
Till he came to the Other Gates—black and rusty
and grim—
And through the keyhole flew fiery tongues that
sputtered and spat at him.
Back thev flew at his summons, and out on the
roaring flame
Camj oiieers upon cheers in chorus, when the
Janitor gave his name.
“Welcome, O Laughter-Maker ! Bring him with
honors in,
Truly our Lord is good to the suffering sons of
Sin !
“ Here is the balm we have needed—give us a
rousing joke!
Help us to emptv our lungs of the stench of the
sulphur smoke!
“ Make us scoff at the brimstone, teach us to laugh
at the coals,
Yours shall it be to win the debris of the hearts of
poor damned souls.
“Way for the grand procession ! Room for the
Lords of Hell !
Guards, present your pokers ! Ring the Great
Fire Bell !
They marched the Laughter-Maker in : Cerberus
wagged his tail;
Charon caught a crab with joy, when he joined in
the crowd’s “ All Hail !”
They gave him a lofty throne on the edge of a
crater’s pit,
With heralds around in a ring to trumpet forth
his wit.
Loud they blared his laughter, over the burning
seas,
Into the bubbling cesspits, bringing a brief sur-
cease
To roast and toast and turning-spit and skewer
and frizzling hide,
The rabid devouring draught that flew through the
damper open wide.
They tied their tails in true-love knots, danced
round him where he sat;
But they prodded his ribs with toasting-forks
whenever the joke fell flat.
They splashed him over with lava, made him skip
on red-hot bricks,
Gave him to drink from a kettle’s mouth —till he
had to learn new tricks.
And when his wage they threw him, laugh ie .* and
shouting “ Encore! ”
He picked it up—’twas heated pence—and then
they laughed the more.
There in the City of Devils—star of comedians he,
Plays the lead in a one-act farce for all eternity.
And sitting where they have throned him, the
Jester of the Damned,
At times he thinks of the Gates of Gold, that in
his face were slammed,
And then his laugh is loudest! never he plays
so well,
As when he remembers Heaven, he —who is
damned in Hell !
Vic.
J. H. GREENE.

The Dutch Malthusian League, which
flourishes under. Government patronage and is
b y “ right-thinking people ”in its own
stolid gm-drinking country, has appointed a widow
lady to furnish information and advice to enquir-
mg wives and mothers re the rational limitation
of their family circles. Evidently, the young
Queen of Holland, when she marries, will not be

of nine.
Salicylic acid is now well known as a
remedy for rheumatism. It is given internally in
various forms, but recently a physician has been
trying its effects in rheumatic cases when applied
to the skin. The acid was applied in the propor-
tion of ten parts to ten parts of lanolin, and 100
parts of lard ; 2 drs. of this ointment were rubbed
into various portions of the patients’ body every
four hours, about 60grs. of the acid thus being
used in-24 hours. The effects are said to have
been satisfactory, more especially as no dis-
turbance of digestion took place.
For rheumatism use Colemane and Sons
Eucalypte Special Oil.*
Dear Bulletin, —P. Luftig calls the big
Queensland saurian an alligator ; Ohas. Hedley
says it’s a crocodile. Nathless so staid a journal of
SC i? n .]'^ < ' bght and leading as London Nature
called the Q. beast an alligator on at least one
occasion (May, 30, 1872), when it told how one
Easton found an alligator’s nest on Eighteen-Mile
Island in Fitzroy River. The crocodile has the
hind feet almost completely webbed, has a perpen-
dicular horny fringe on the back of the hind legs,
has the fourth tooth of the lower jaw fitted into
an external notch in the upper jaw, so as to be
generally visible when the mouth is closed. The
alligator has smoothly-rounded hind legs, very in-
completely-webbed hind toes, has fourth tooth of
the lower jaw fitted into an internal pit in the
upper ja,w, and therefore quite invisible when the
mouth is closed. Also, whereas crocodiles are
found the world over, alligators are confined to
America. The Central American crocodile has a
strong, musky odour. Re Mr. Hedley’s remarks
that, ‘ ‘ Probably the crocodile’s coasting certificate
allowed him to travel from river mouth to river
mouth within the shelter of the Barrier Reef, but
not to navigate the rough open sea,” the crocodile
is not indigenous to the Cocos Islands in the
Indian Ocean, yet at long intervals an odd croco-
dile turns up there to astonish the natives. The
nearest land to the Cocos is Java, about 700 miles
off, where crocodiles abound. If a crocodile can’t
navigate the rough, open sea, how does he ne-
gotiate that 700 miles ?—Steele Grey.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition; 12, a Wonderland;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long.*
We stepped on to Bowen Pier (writes a
cheerful correspondent) and were surrounded by a
crowd of black maidens dressed in white teeth and
satin polish—that was the lot. They had one,
only one, salutation—“ Chuck ’em sixpen.” Those
who understood threw a silver coin into the sea,
and the damsels aforesaid (full-grown women)
plunged in like black seals, reaching it before it
fluttered to the bottom of the clear water, and
rising with it in their lips. They were full-grown
women, so nice and plump that I couldn’t help
patting one gently on the back. These young
ladies had a chaperone, an old dame with a con-
certina face, who, shouting like a demon, skir-
mished round in a canoe. The old lady, who was
doubtless Adam’s grandmother, had the satin-
polish clothing without the teeth ; her figure was
like footballs with the wind out of them, and her
shanks ! Bowen Pier is miles long ; when you
have reached the shore end you flounder in sand,
eventually finding a main street with a magnificent
stretch up a hill. There are plenty of houses, but
the only living thing we saw was a horse grazing
in the middle of the road. He looked up with a
startled air when he saw us; we were evidently
the first human creatures he had ever seen. In
the distance we saw a dog walk across the street
and lie down ; for the rest, the town was a vast
solitude. We retreated back into the scrub—the
place made us sad. On board again, one of the
: passengers declared he had awakened an hotel-
keeper and got a drink, but for the rest of the
voyage this man was regarded as a hopeless liar.
For no one will wake up in Bowen till the last
trump.
